The Second Proof: Motion Cannot Be Eternal
Author proves impossibility of eternal motion by analyzing motion as transition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of motion as transition from one state to another proves that motion cannot be eternal, because its essence requires being preceded, which contradicts the definition of eternity.

Core Idea
Motion's definition proves it cannot be eternal: it must be preceded, but eternity has no before.

In this proof, the author focuses on motion as a type of event. By analyzing its very definition — transition from one state to another — he shows that motion necessarily depends on a prior state. This dependency is incompatible with the concept of eternity (al-azal), which has no beginning and nothing before it. Thus, motion cannot be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English versions to get the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the key definitions
Notice how the author defines motion as transition and eternity as that which has no beginning.
3
Understand the logical deduction
Trace the argument: motion must be preceded, eternity cannot be preceded, so combining them is a contradiction.
This deduction is the core of the proof.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Why does the author conclude definitively that motion cannot occur in eternity? What does this imply about the world?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في بيان أنه يمتنع وجود حوادث لا أول لها : أن نقول : الحركة عبارة عن الانتقال من حالة إلى حالة . ومن المعلوم بالضرورة : أن الانتقال من حالة إلى حالة ، يجب كونه مسبوقاً بالحالة المنتقل عنها . فماهية الحركة وحقيقتها ، تقتضي المسبوقية بالغير، وحقيقة الأزل تنافي المسبوقية بالغير، فكان الجمع بين حقيقة الحركة وبين حقيقة الأزل : جمعاً بين النقيضين ، وهو محال . فوجب القطع بامتناع حصول الحركة في الأزل .
The second proof in explanation that it is impossible for there to exist past events with no first: We say: Motion is the transition from one state to another. It is known necessarily that the transition from one state to another must be preceded by the state from which it transitions. So the essence and reality of motion require being-preceded-by-another, and the reality of eternity (al-azal) is incompatible with being-preceded-by-another. Thus combining the reality of motion with the reality of eternity is a combination of two opposites, which is impossible. Therefore it is necessary to definitively conclude that motion cannot occur in eternity.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a proof (hujja) demonstrating that past events cannot be eternal (i.e., have no beginning). The author focuses specifically on motion (haraka) as a type of event. He defines motion as 'transition from one state to another.' From this definition, he deduces that motion must be preceded by the state it leaves. The key point: 'being preceded by another' (mashuqiyya bi al-ghayr) is an essential property of motion. In contrast, eternity (al-azal) is defined as that which has no beginning and is not preceded by anything. Therefore, motion and eternity cannot coexist: one requires priority, the other negates priority. The author concludes that combining them is a contradiction, so motion cannot be eternal. This proof targets the notion that the world or its events could have existed eternally past (qidam al-hawadith).

Key Terms
حركة (motion)
أزل (eternity)
مسبوقية بالغير (being preceded by another)
نقيضان (two opposites)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might argue that motion could be eternal if the universe always existed.
Correction: The author counters by showing that the very definition of motion entails priority, which eternity excludes, so eternal motion is impossible regardless of whether the universe always existed.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of motion given in the second proof?
A) The movement from potentiality to actuality
B) The continuous flow of time
C) The transition from one state to another
D) The displacement of a body in space

Key Takeaway
Motion cannot be eternal because its essence requires being preceded by a prior state, while eternity has no prior.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life is a series of motions—every action, thought, and change. If motion cannot be eternal, what does that say about the permanence of your achievements? How does this truth confront your tendency to build a legacy as if it will last forever? Will you commit today to invest in what is eternal, even if it costs you temporary motion?
?
The author proves that motion requires a prior state. You did not bring yourself into existence; you came from nothing. How does this proof remind you of your absolute dependence on the Eternal Creator? In what specific area of your life are you living as if you are self-sufficient? What would it mean to actively acknowledge your need for Him in that area starting now?
?
Eternity is defined as having no beginning. You have a beginning. Your very existence is a testimony that you are not eternal. If you are not eternal, you will have an end. Does this truth unsettle you? How should it change the way you live today, knowing that your time is limited and that only the Eternal One can grant you a share in His eternity?

The Dichotomy Proof for a First Event
Author uses a dichotomy to prove a first event.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a simple logical dichotomy proves that past events must have a first beginning, and why an infinite regress of events is impossible.

Core Idea
Every chain of events must have a starting point—even in eternity.

This proof uses a clever either-or argument to show that events cannot stretch back infinitely. By examining both possibilities—either an event occurred in eternity or not—the author forces the conclusion that there must be a first event. This is a classic kalam argument against the eternity of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the logical flow.
Notice the clear 'either/or' structure.
2
Trace the two main branches
Identify the two possibilities: either no event occurred in eternity, or some event did. Then follow the reasoning for each.
The first branch (no event) directly leads to a first event; the second branch splits further.
3
Understand why infinite regress is impossible
In the second branch, if an event in eternity were preceded by another, that would imply eternity itself is preceded—a contradiction. If not preceded, it is the first event. So in all cases, a first event exists.
This refutes the idea of an endless chain of past events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : أن يقال : إنه حصل شيء من الحوادث في الأزل ، أو يقال : لم يحصل شيء من الحوادث في الأزل. والثاني : يوجب الاعتراف بثبوت أول وبداية [ للحوادث ] وأما الأول فنقول : إنه لما حصل شيء من الحوادث في الأزل ، فذلك الحادث إن كان مسبوقاً بالغير لزم كون الأزل مسبوقاً بغيره . وهو محال . وإن لم يكن مسبوقاً بالغير كان ذلك الحادث : أول الحوادث. فيكون قد حصل للحوادث أول . وهو المطلوب.
The third proof: It is said: Either some event occurred in eternity, or it is said: no event occurred in eternity. The second necessitates acknowledging the existence of a first and a beginning [for events]. As for the first, we say: When some event occurred in eternity, then that event – if it was preceded by another, then it would necessitate that eternity is preceded by another, which is impossible. And if it was not preceded by another, then that event is the first of events. Thus events have a first. And that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is another proof (hujja) that past events must have a first beginning. The author presents a simple dichotomy: either (1) an event occurred in eternity, or (2) no event occurred in eternity. If (2) is true, then there must be a first moment when events began – i.e., events have a beginning. If (1) is true, then we examine that event: if it was preceded by another event, then eternity itself would be preceded (by that prior event), which contradicts the definition of eternity as un-preceded. If it was not preceded, then it is itself the first event. Thus in any scenario, there must be a first event. The proof relies on the impossibility of infinite regress: there cannot be an event that is always preceded by another, because that would entail an eternal chain, which is rejected. The dichotomy ensures that a first event is inevitable.

Key Terms
أزل
حادث
أول
مسبوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an event occurs in eternity, it does not need to be preceded by anything and can always be part of an infinite chain.
Correction: The author shows that if an event in eternity is not preceded, it is precisely the first event, which is the intended conclusion. An infinite chain is ruled out because it would make eternity preceded.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, if no event occurred in eternity, what follows?
A) Eternity is impossible
B) Events must have a first beginning
C) Events must be eternal
D) Eternity is preceded by events

Key Takeaway
A logical dichotomy shows that either way, events must have a first beginning, ruling out an infinite past.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every chain of events must have a first, what does that mean for your own life? Do you live as though your existence depends entirely on the First Cause? What would change if you truly internalized that you are not self-sufficient?
?
The proof shows that eternity cannot be preceded. How does this deepen your reverence for God's eternal nature? In what specific area of your life do you need to acknowledge that God alone is un-preceded and un-caused?
?
When you consider the logical necessity of a first event, does it challenge any assumptions you hold about 'always having been' or 'never beginning'? What practical step can you take today to live in the reality that your existence and every moment is a gift from the First?

Refuting Three Arguments for an End to Events
The author refutes three arguments that would force an end to events in the future.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes three arguments for an end to events by showing that they lead to parallel absurdities regarding the future, exposing a mistaken view of pre-eternity and post-eternity.

Core Idea
Events' impossibility in the past does not force an end in the future; the same logic would prove too much.

The opponent argues that events cannot go backward infinitely, so they must have a beginning. The author cleverly turns this around: if that logic holds, then events cannot go forward infinitely either, forcing an end—which the opponent denies. This lesson walks through the three counter-arguments the author presents, showing the inconsistency in the opponent's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
The opponent believes events must have a first because motion requires being preceded, and pre-eternity precludes that. Grasp this original argument before seeing the refutation.
The opponent's reasoning is based on the nature of motion and the definition of azal.
2
First argument refutation
See how the author applies the same reasoning to the future: motion also requires being succeeded, and post-eternity precludes that, forcing an end.
This is a direct parallel: if the opponent accepts the logic for the past, they must accept it for the future.
3
Second argument refutation
Follow the dilemma the opponent uses for the past and see how it applies equally to the future.
The opponent's dilemma about events in azal/abad is shown to be symmetric.
4
Third argument refutation
Analyze the argument about non-existences and see the parallel in the past.
The opponent's third proof is mirrored by considering non-existences before each event.
5
Conclusion
Learn the key error: treating azal and abad as specific times rather than the absence of temporal limits.
The author concludes that all three proofs are sophistries based on this misconception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امتناع حدوث الحوادث في جانب الأزل، ووجوب انتهائها إلى أول، يوجب عليكم امتناع حدوث الحوادث في الأبد، ووجوب انتهائها إلى آخر. ولنقرر ذلك في كل واحد منها: فأما الوجه الأول: فهو أنكم قلتم: حقيقة الحركة تقتضي المسبوقية بالغير، وحقيقة الأزل تنافي المسبوقية بالغير، والجمع بينهما محال فوجب انتهاء الحوادث إلى أول. فنقول: كما أن كل جزء من أجزاء الحركة، يجب أن يكون مسبوقاً بجزء آخر، فكذلك يجب أن يكون ملحوقاً بجزء آخر، إذ لو بقي ذلك الجزء لانقلب سكوناً. وكلامنا فيما إذا كانت الحركة باقية. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: كما أن حقيقة الحركة تقتضي المسبوقية بالغير، فكذلك تقتضي الملحوقية بالغير، وحقيقة الأبد تنافي الملحوقية بالغير، والجمع بين الحركة وبين الأبد: محال. فوجب انتهاء الحركات في جانب الأبد، إلى آخر. وكما أن هذا الكلام فاسد، فكذا ما ذكرتموه. وأما الوجه الثاني: وهو قولكم: هل حصل في الأزل شيء من الحوادث أم لا؟ فإن لم يحصل فيه شيء من الحوادث، فللحوادث آخر على سبيل الوجوب. وإن حصل في الأبد شيء من الحوادث، فهل حصل عقيبه شيء آخر، أم لا؟ فإن كان الأول فقد حصل بعد الأبد شيء آخر، وذلك محال. لأن الأبد ينافي الملحوقية بالغير، وما حصل بعده شيء اخر فهو ملحوق بالغير، والجمع بينهما محال. وإن كان الثاني: وهو أنه لم يحصل بعد ذلك الحادث شيء آخر، فحينئذ يكون ذلك الشيء آخراً لكل الحوادث، فيكون لكل الحوادث آخر على سبيل الوجوب. وذلك محال. وأما الوجه الثالث من هذه الوجوه الثلاثة: فهو أن نقول: لما وجب في كل واحد من الحوادث، أن يحصل بعده عدم لا آخر له، كان مجموع هذه العدمات حاصل في الأبد، فإما أن يحصل في الأبد شيء من الموجودات أو لم يحصل. فإن كان الأول لزم أن يحصل السابق والمسبوق معاً، وهو محال. وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ لم يحصل في الأبد شيء من الحوادث، فوجب أن يحصل للحوادث آخر ونهاية. فيثبت: أن هذه الدلائل الثلاثة التي ذكرتموها إن أوجبت أن يحصل للحوادث أول، فكذلك أوجبت أن يحصل للحوادث آخر. ولما بطل ذلك، علمنا أن هذه الدلائل مغالطات، نشأت من اعتقاد: أن الأزل وقت معين في الماضي، وأن الأبد وقت معين في المستقبل. ولما كان ذلك باطلا، كانت هذه الدلائل التي ذكرتموها فاسدة.
The impossibility of the occurrence of events in the aspect of pre-eternity (al-azal), and the necessity of their termination at a first, necessitates upon you the impossibility of the occurrence of events in post-eternity (al-abad), and the necessity of their termination at a last. And we shall establish that with respect to each of them. As for the first aspect: it is that you said: The reality of motion necessitates being preceded by another, and the reality of pre-eternity precludes being preceded by another, and combining them is impossible, so the termination of events at a first is necessary. We say: Just as every part of the parts of motion must be preceded by another part, so likewise it must be succeeded by another part, for if that part remained it would become rest. And our discussion is when motion is continuous. Once you understand this, we say: Just as the reality of motion necessitates being preceded by another, so likewise it necessitates being succeeded by another, and the reality of post-eternity precludes being succeeded by another, and combining motion with post-eternity is impossible. So the termination of motions in the aspect of post-eternity at a last is necessary. And just as this statement is corrupt, so is what you mentioned. As for the second aspect: it is your statement: Did any event occur in pre-eternity or not? If no event occurred in it, then events have a last necessarily. But if some event occurred in post-eternity, then did something else occur after it or not? If the former, then something else has occurred after post-eternity, and that is impossible, because post-eternity precludes being succeeded by another, and what occurs after it is succeeded by another, and combining them is impossible. If the latter, i.e., that nothing else occurred after that event, then that thing is the last of all events, so all events have a last necessarily, and that is impossible. As for the third of these three aspects: it is that we say: Since it is necessary for every event that after it occurs a non-existence having no last, the sum of these non-existences occurs in post-eternity. Then either something of existents occurs in post-eternity or not. If the former, then the prior and the posterior would occur together, which is impossible. If the latter, then nothing of events occurs in post-eternity, so events must have a last and an end. So it is established that these three proofs which you mentioned, if they necessitate that events have a first, likewise they necessitate that events have a last. And since that is false, we know that these proofs are sophistries arising from the belief that pre-eternity is a specific time in the past and that post-eternity is a specific time in the future. And since that is false, these proofs which you mentioned are corrupt.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of three arguments that the opponent uses to prove that events must end in the future, paralleling their claim that events must have a beginning. The author turns each argument against the opponent. First, the opponent uses the nature of motion: motion requires being preceded by another part; pre-eternity (azal) precludes such precedence, so motion cannot extend infinitely backward. The author counters that motion also requires being succeeded by another part (otherwise it would be rest), and post-eternity (abad) precludes being succeeded, so by the same logic motion cannot extend infinitely forward. Since the opponent denies this conclusion, their original argument is invalid. Second, the opponent uses a dilemma: either no event occurred in azal, so events end; or some event occurred. But if some event occurred in abad, then either something occurs after it (which is impossible because abad has no after) or nothing occurs after it (making that event the last). The author notes that this dilemma applies equally to the past: if no event occurred in azal, events begin; if some event occurred, then either something before it (impossible) or it is the first. The opponent accepts the past conclusion but rejects the future, so the argument is inconsistent. Third, the opponent argues that after each event there is a non-existence without end, so the sum of these non-existences occurs in abad. If any existent also occurs in abad, the non-existence prior and the existent posterior would coincide (impossible); if no existent occurs, then events have a last. The author again points out the parallel: before each event there is a non-existence without beginning, and the sum occurs in azal; then either an existent occurred in azal (impossible) or no existent, giving a first. The author concludes that these proofs are sophistries stemming from wrongly treating azal and abad as specific times rather than the absence of temporal boundaries.

Key Terms
أزل
أبد
حركة
سكون
مسبوقية
ملحوقية
حوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if events cannot go backward infinitely, they cannot go forward infinitely either, so the opponent's argument is valid.
Correction: The author shows that the opponent's own reasoning leads to a contradiction because it would also force an end in the future, which the opponent denies. The error is in treating azal and abad as specific times.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the key error behind the opponent's three arguments for an end to events?
A) Confusing motion with rest.
B) Treating pre-eternity (azal) and post-eternity (abad) as specific points in time.
C) Denying the principle of causality.
D) Assuming events must have a beginning but not an end.

Key Takeaway
The three arguments for a future end fail because they rely on treating pre-eternity and post-eternity as specific time points, whereas they denote the absence of temporal limits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever think of Allah's eternity as a long, unending stretch of time? How does that mental picture affect your understanding of His relationship to creation? Can you commit to correcting this by remembering that eternity is not time but its absence?
?
The author shows that treating azal and abad as time points leads to logical contradictions. Where in your own life do you unconsciously confine Allah to your own mental timelines? Will you ask Him today to free you from that limitation?
?
If events can truly go on forever in the future, does that change your sense of urgency in seeking His pleasure? What one action will you take today that has eternal weight, knowing that the future is open-ended?

Every Event Requires a Preceding Event
The author presents a proof that every event must be preceded by another event ad infinitum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that every originated event must be preceded by another originated event, either directly through an originated cause or through an originated condition on an eternal cause.

Core Idea
Every originated thing demands an infinite regress of prior originated conditions.

This lesson presents a key argument from the fourth question: if the opponent claims that an infinite regress of events is impossible, the author offers a proof that such a regress is actually necessary. The argument relies on a basic principle of reason: every originated thing requires a cause, and if the cause is eternal, it still depends on an originated condition. This leads to an endless chain of prior originated events.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the key terms: حادث (originated), سبب (cause), قديم (eternal), شرط (condition), بديهة العقل (intuition of intellect).
2
Identify the two cases
The text states that every originated event must have either an originated cause or an eternal cause with an originated condition. Underline these two possibilities in the translation.
These two cases cover all possibilities, and both lead to a regress.
3
Examine the example
The author uses the example of theologians who say the world occurred at a specific time due to God's will. Notice how this example illustrates the second case: the eternal cause (God's power) is dependent on an originated condition (the specific time).
The example shows that even when the cause is eternal, a condition must arise, which is itself an originated event.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع: إن دل ما ذكرتم على امتناع حوادث لا أول لها، فههنا ما يدل على وقوعه لا محالة. وهو من وجهين. الأول: إن بديهة العقل حاكمة بأن كل أمر حادث، فلا بد له من سبب حادث، أو إن كان ذلك السبب قديماً، إلا أن تأثيره في ذلك الأثر الحادث، كان موقوفاً على شرط حادث. ألا ترى أن القائلين بالحدوث يقولون: العالم إنما حدث في الوقت المعين، لأن إرادة الله تعالى تعلقت بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين. وهذا في الحقيقة اعتراف بأن تأثير قدرة الله في وجود العالم، كان موقوفاً على حدوث ذلك الوقت. فثبت: أن كل حادث، فإنه لا بد وأن يكون معللاً بسبب حادث. وإن كان معللاً بسبب قديم، إلا أن تأثير ذلك السبب القديم في ذلك الحادث، كان موقوفاً على شرط حادث، وعلى كلا التقديرين، فهذا يقتضي أن يكون كل حادث، مسبوقاً بحادث آخر لا إلى أول.
The fourth question: If what you mentioned indicates the impossibility of events having no first, then here is what indicates its occurrence necessarily. And it is from two aspects. The first: The intuition of the intellect judges that every originated matter (amr hadith) must have an originated cause (sabab hadith), or if that cause is eternal (qadim), yet its effect on that originated effect was dependent on an originated condition (shart hadith). Do you not see that those who affirm origination (al-huduth) say: The world only occurred in the specific time because the will of God (ta'ala) was attached to bringing the world into existence at that specific time. And this is in reality an admission that the effect of God's power in the existence of the world was dependent on the origination of that time. So it is established that every originated thing must be caused by an originated cause. And if it is caused by an eternal cause, yet the effect of that eternal cause on that originated being was dependent on an originated condition. And on both suppositions, this requires that every originated thing be preceded by another originated thing without a first.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage begins the fourth question, which is a counter-argument: even if the opponent's proofs show that an infinite regress in the past is impossible, here is a proof that such an infinite regress actually occurs. The author mentions two aspects (two proofs), but only the first is given in the surviving text. The first proof relies on a basic principle of reason: every originated thing (hadith) requires a cause (sabab). If the cause is also originated, we have a regress; if the cause is eternal, then its effect depends on an additional originated condition (shart). For example, those who affirm the origination of the world say that the world occurred at a specific time because God's will was attached to that specific time. Thus the time itself is an originated condition on which the effect of God's power depends. Therefore, every originated thing ultimately depends on an infinite chain of originated conditions, leading to an infinite regress without a first. The author presents this as evidence that the opponent's denial of an infinite regress is false, because such a regress is actually necessary.

Key Terms
حادث
سبب
قديم
شرط
بديهة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a cause is eternal, it can produce an effect without any condition, thus avoiding an infinite regress.
Correction: The argument shows that even an eternal cause requires an originated condition for its effect to occur at a specific time, which itself is an originated event, leading to regress.

Checkpoint
According to the first aspect of the fourth question, what does every originated event require?
A) An eternal cause with no condition.
B) Either an originated cause or an eternal cause with an originated condition.
C) A simultaneous cause.
D) A cause that is prior but not originated.

Key Takeaway
Every originated event, whether directly caused by an originated cause or indirectly through an eternal cause with an originated condition, entails an infinite regress of prior originated events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says that every originated thing depends on an originated condition (shart hadith). Think of a single good deed you did today. What condition was necessary for it to occur? Now trace that condition back—does it lead to an endless chain? How does that realization make you feel about your own agency? What does it mean for your reliance on the Eternal (al-Qadim) in your next action? Commit to one specific act today that consciously acknowledges that only Allah is independent of conditions.
?
The author uses the example of God's will attaching to a specific time for creation. In your life, you often wait for the 'right time' to do something important—repent, forgive, or change a habit. But the passage shows that even that 'right time' is an originated condition. Could your waiting be a cover for not fully trusting in God's eternal power to act now? What would it look like to take one concrete step toward that goal right now, without waiting for the perfect condition?
?
The infinite regress described in this lesson seems to trap everything in dependency. Yet the author presents this as evidence for a deeper truth. Reflect: Does seeing yourself as part of an endless chain of causes make you feel insignificant or does it point you to the One who is beyond the chain? How can you transform the feeling of dependency into an act of worship? Write down one sentence of gratitude to the Eternal Being who alone breaks the chain, and place it where you will see it tomorrow morning.

First Proof: Every Originated Event Requires an Originated Cause or Condition
First proof: every event needs an originated cause or condition, leading to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that every originated event must have either an originated cause or an originated condition, forcing an infinite regress of events.

Core Idea
Every event needs a cause or condition that is itself originated, leading to an endless chain.

The opponent begins his argument by appealing to the intuition of reason: anything that comes into existence must have a cause. But that cause is either itself originated, or if eternal, its effect depends on an originated condition. This principle, he claims, inevitably leads to an infinite regress of events. In this lesson, we will unpack this reasoning and see how it challenges the impossibility of an infinite past.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure of the argument: premise, alternatives, example, conclusion.
Pay attention to the phrase 'بديهة العقل' – the intuition of reason.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage states that for every originated thing, there must be either an originated cause or an eternal cause with an originated condition. Mark these two branches.
3
Analyze the example of the world's creation
The opponent uses the example of those who affirm temporal origination: the world was created at a specific time because God's will was attached to that time. This shows that the effect of an eternal cause depends on an originated condition (the time).
This example illustrates the second branch.
4
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that in both branches, every originated thing is preceded by another originated thing without end. Reflect on why that follows.
The infinite regress is the key point the opponent wants to establish.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع : إن دل ما ذكرتم على امتناع حوادث لا أول لها . فههنا ما يدل على وقوعه لا محالة . وهو من وجهين . الأول : إن بديهة العقل حاكمة بأن كل أمر حادث ، فلا بد له من سبب حادث ، أو إن كان ذلك السبب قديماً ، إلا أن تأثيره في ذلك الأثر الحادث، كان موقوفاً على شرط حادث . ألا ترى أن القائلين بالحدوث يقولون : العالم إنما حدث في الوقت المعين، لأن إرادة الله تعالى تعلقت بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين. وهذا في الحقيقة اعتراف بأن تأثير قدرة الله في وجود العالم ، كان موقوفاً على حدوث ذلك الوقت . فثبت : أن كل حادث ، فإنه لا بد وأن يكون معللاً بسبب حادث . وإن كان معللاً بسبب قديم ، إلا أن تأثير ذلك السبب القديم في ذلك الحادث ، كان موقوفاً على شرط حادث ، وعلى كلا التقديرين، فهذا يقتضي أن يكون كل حادث ، مسبوقاً بحادث لا إلى أول . والثاني : إن كل حادث ، فإن عدمه يكون سابقاً على وجوده ، وذلك السبق أمر ما من الأمور ، كان حاصلا قبل حدوث ذلك الحادث . وهذا يقتضي أن يكون كل حادث ، مسبوقاً بحادث آخر ، لا إلى أول . فهذه جملة السؤالات على هذه الوجوه والله أعلم .
The fourth question: If what you have mentioned proves the impossibility of events without a beginning, then here is what proves its occurrence necessarily. And it is from two aspects. The first: The intuition of reason judges that every originated thing (حادث) must have an originated cause (سبب حادث), or if that cause is eternal, yet its effect in that originated effect was dependent on an originated condition. Do you not see that those who affirm origination (الحدوث) say: The world only originated at a specific time because the will of Allah Most High was attached to bringing the world into existence at that specific time. And this is in reality an admission that the effect of God's power upon the existence of the world was dependent on the occurrence of that time. Thus it is established that every originated thing must be caused by an originated cause. And if it is caused by an eternal cause, yet the effect of that eternal cause upon that originated thing was dependent on an originated condition. In both cases, this necessitates that every originated thing is preceded by an originated thing without end. The second: Every originated thing, its non-existence (عدم) precedes its existence, and that precedence is something among things that occurred before the occurrence of that originated thing. And this necessitates that every originated thing is preceded by another originated thing without end. So this is the totality of the questions upon these aspects, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection from an opponent against the claim that an infinite chain of events is impossible. The opponent asserts that not only is it possible, but it necessarily occurs. He gives two proofs. The first proof relies on the principle that every event requires a cause. That cause is either originated or eternal. If it is eternal, its effect on the originated thing depends on an originated condition. The opponent uses the example of the proponents of temporal origination who say the world was created at a specific time because God's will was attached to that specific time. This implies that God's power's effect was conditioned by the occurrence of that time. Hence, every event is caused by an originated cause or condition, leading to an infinite regress. The second proof focuses on the non-existence that precedes each event. That precedence itself is a thing that occurs before the event, so it is an originated thing preceding the event, again leading to infinite regress. Thus the opponent concludes that events must have no first beginning.

شرط حادث
بديهة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the opponent denies that an eternal cause can produce a temporal effect.
Correction: The opponent does not deny that; he allows an eternal cause but argues that its effect depends on an originated condition, which itself requires a cause, leading to regress.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The first proof argues that every originated event requires a cause or condition that is itself originated, leading to an infinite regress of events without a first.

Does that realization drive you to gratitude and dependence on God, or do you live as if you are self-sufficient?

Second Proof: Precedence of Non-Existence Demands Infinite Preceding Events
Second proof: the precedence of non-existence forces infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that the precedence of non-existence over every originated thing implies an infinite regress of events.

Core Idea
The non-existence that precedes an event is itself an originated thing, requiring an infinite chain.

The opponent's second proof is simpler but profound: every originated thing is preceded by its own non-existence. That 'precedence' is a real thing that occurred before the event. Since it is something that came about, it too must be preceded by something, ad infinitum. In this lesson, we will break down this argument and see how it leads to the same conclusion as the first.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the short but dense reasoning.
2
Understand the premise
The opponent states that every originated thing is preceded by its non-existence (عدم). That non-existence is a thing that occurred before the event.
The Arabic uses 'السبق' for precedence.
3
Recognize the implication
This precedence is itself an originated thing because it occurs before the event. Therefore, it too must be preceded by non-existence, and so on.
This leads to an infinite regress of non-existences or preceding events.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Thus, every originated thing is preceded by another originated thing without end, supporting the opponent's thesis.
Note that this argument does not rely on causation, only on the concept of precedence.

The Fifth Proof: Temporal Events Must Have a First Beginning
Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a proof that all temporal events have a first beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's argument that all temporal events require a first beginning by eliminating the possibility of a necessitating cause.

Core Idea
Temporal events cannot be caused by a necessitator, only by a free agent, which implies a first beginning.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents the fifth proof for the beginning of temporal events. He constructs a syllogism: all temporal events are acts of a free agent, and every act of a free agent has a first beginning. He then elaborates on why the cause of temporal events cannot be a necessitator, using the example of celestial motion. This lesson walks through each step of the proof, clarifying the logical moves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two premises
The proof is a syllogism with two explicit premises and a conclusion. Locate the premises: (1) all temporal events are acts of a free agent, (2) every act of a free agent has a first beginning.
3
Understand the elimination of necessitator
Why can't a necessitator produce temporal motion? The key argument: if the effecter were a necessitator, each part of motion would have to endure, but enduring parts would be rest, not motion. This contradiction shows the effecter must be free.
4
Grasp the conclusion
A free agent must be prior to its act. Hence the agent of celestial motions is prior to them. Anything preceded by another's existence has a first beginning. Thus all temporal events have a first beginning.
5
Reflect on the proof's significance
This proof is a key argument for the creation of the universe. Consider how it refutes the notion of an eternal universe with no beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة في بيان أنه لا بد للحوادث من أول : أن نقول : الحوادث بأسرها فعل فاعل مختار ، وكل ما كان كذلك ، فله أول . فالحوادث بأسرها يجب أن يكون لها أول . بيان المقدمة الأولى : إن هذه الحركات أمور حادثة ، وكل حادث فله مؤثر ، وذلك المؤثر إما أن يكون موجباً، وإما أن يكون مختاراً . لا جائز أن يكون ذلك المؤثر موجباً، لأن الموجب يلزم من دوامه، دوام الموجب. فلو كان المؤثر في وجود كل جزء من الأجزاء المفترضة في الحركة الفلكية موجباً بالذات ، لوجب أن يدوم ذلك الجزء من الحركة بدوام ذلك الموجب، لكن دوام جزء من أجزاء الحركة محال . لأن ذلك الجزء المعين من الحركة ، عبارة عن حصول الجسم في ذلك الحيز المعين ، بعد أن كان حاصلا في حيز آخر . ولو بقيت هذه الحالة لصار سكوناً لا حركة . فيثبت : أن القول ببقاء الجزء المعين من الحركة يوجب عدم الحركة ، والتقدير تقدير بقاء الحركة. فثبت : أن المؤثر في وجود الحركة ، يمتنع أن يكون موجباً بالذات . فلا بد وأن يكون فاعلاً بالاختيار [ والفاعل بالاختيار (۳) ] يجب كونه سابقاً على فعله ، فوجب أن يكون فاعل حركات الأفلاك ، سابقاً في وجوده عليها . وكل ما كان مسبوقاً بوجود غيره ، فلوجوده أول . فيلزم أن يحصل لجميع الحوادث أول وبداية . وهو المطلوب .
The fifth proof, in explaining that temporal events necessarily have a first beginning: we say: all temporal events are the act of a free agent, and everything that is so has a first beginning. Therefore all temporal events must have a first beginning. Elucidation of the first premise: these motions are temporal things, and every temporal thing has an effecter, and that effecter is either necessitating (mūjib) or free (mukhtār). It is not possible that that effecter be a necessitator, because from the perpetuity of a necessitator the perpetuity of the necessitated follows. So if the effecter in the existence of every part of the assumed parts in the celestial motion were a necessitator by essence, then that part of motion would have to endure with the endurance of that effecter, but the endurance of a part of motion is impossible. Because that specific part of motion consists in the body's obtaining that specific location after having been in another location. If this state remained, it would become rest, not motion. So it is established that the statement that a specific part of motion remains necessitates the non-existence of motion, while the assumption is the assumption of the continuation of motion. So it is established that the effecter in the existence of motion cannot be a necessitator by essence. Therefore it must be a free agent, and a free agent must be prior to its act. So the agent of the celestial motions must be prior in existence to them. And everything that is preceded by the existence of another has a first beginning for its existence. Consequently, all temporal events necessarily have a first and a beginning. And that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof that temporal events (ḥawādith) must have a first beginning. The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, structures the proof as a syllogism. He states two premises: (1) all temporal events are the act of a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār), and (2) everything that is an act of a free agent has a first beginning. The conclusion follows: all temporal events have a first beginning. Then he provides an elaboration of the first premise. He argues that these motions (e.g., celestial motions) are temporal (ḥāditha). Every temporal thing has an effecter (muʾaththir). That effecter must be either a necessitator (mūjib) or a free agent (mukhtār). It cannot be a necessitator because a necessitator, if it endures, necessitates the endurance of the necessitated. Applied to motion: if the effecter of each part of a celestial motion were a necessitator by essence, then each part would have to endure as long as the effecter endures, but the endurance of a specific part of motion is impossible. Why? Because a specific part of motion is defined as the body being in a specific place after having been in another place. If that state persisted, it would be rest, not motion. Thus the assumption that motion continues contradicts the idea that a part of motion endures. Therefore the effecter cannot be a necessitator; it must be a free agent. A free agent must be prior to its act. Hence the agent of celestial motions is prior to them. Anything preceded by another's existence has a first beginning. Therefore all temporal events have a first beginning. This proof aims to establish that the universe had a beginning, a key point in kalām.

Key Terms
حجة
حادث
فاعل مختار
موجب بالذات
أول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof assumes free agency without justification.
Correction: The author proves free agency by eliminating the alternative of necessitation through a logical contradiction.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the possibility that the effecter of temporal motions is a necessitator (mūjib)?
A) Because a necessitator cannot produce motion.
B) Because if the necessitator endures, each part of motion would have to endure, which would turn motion into rest.
C) Because a necessitator requires a material substrate.
D) Because the effecter must be prior to its effect.

Key Takeaway
Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's fifth proof shows that temporal events require a free agent, which implies a first beginning, thereby refuting the notion of an eternal universe.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi proves that a necessitator would make motion impossible, forcing a free agent. In your life, do you treat God as a 'necessitator'—expecting certain outcomes because you pray or obey—rather than as a free agent who acts according to His wisdom? How will you surrender to His free will today?
?
The proof shows that every temporal event has a first beginning. Think of a habit or pattern you consider 'just the way things are.' Does this lesson challenge you to see it as something that had a beginning and can change? What step will you take to break its chain?
?
Al-Razi argues that all temporal events are acts of a free agent. Is there an area of your life where you feel trapped by circumstances, forgetting that the ultimate free agent is God? What does it mean for you to trust that He has a first beginning for every trial?

Objection and Answer: The Heavy Object Analogy for Necessary Causation
Response to objection that necessary causation implies simultaneity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a necessary cause can produce successive motions without requiring each part to endure, using the analogy of a heavy object's descent.

Core Idea
A necessary cause can produce succession by conditioning each effect on the prior one ceasing.

This passage presents an objection against the possibility that celestial motions are caused by a necessary agent. The objector argues that a necessary cause would require all parts of motion to endure simultaneously. The response uses a simple example: a heavy object falling due to its natural weight. The weight remains constant, yet the object moves through distinct positions one after another. This shows that a necessary cause can indeed produce a sequence, as long as each later part is conditioned on the prior part having ended.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector asks: Why can't the effecter be a necessitator by essence? The implication is that a necessary cause would require all parts of the effect to coexist.
The objection is that necessary causation implies simultaneity of all parts.
2
See the response framework
The author denies the claimed implication. He asserts that it does not follow that each part endures because the cause endures.
3
Grasp the heavy object analogy
A heavy object falls due to its natural weight, which is a constant necessitating cause. Yet the object does not stay in one place; it moves through successive positions. The weight necessitates each later part only after the prior part has occurred.
This shows that a necessary cause can produce a sequence.
4
Apply to celestial motions
By analogy, even if the celestial motions are caused by a necessary agent, the agent's permanence does not require each motion part to endure. Each part is conditioned on the prior part's termination.
The philosophers' view is referenced for further elaboration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المؤثر في وجود هذه الحركات موجباً بالذات ؟ قوله : و لا يلزم (٢) أن يدوم كل جزء من الأجزاء المفترضة في تلك الحركة ، لاجل دوام ذلك الموجب » قلنا : لا نسلم أن ذلك لازم . وبيانه : أن الموجب لنزول الثقيل هو ثقله الطبيعي ، وذلك الثقل باقي في الأحوال كلها. ولم يلزم عن بقاء ذلك الثقل ، بقاء كل واحد من الأجزاء المفترضة [ في حركة (۳) ] ذلك الثقيل ، فكذا ههنا . بل التحقيق : أن نقول : إيجاب ذلك الثقل للجزء المتأخر من الحركة ، مشروط بحصول الجزء المتقدم : فالموجب والمؤثر هو الثقل . إلا أن إيجابه لكل جزء من الأجزاء المفترضة في تلك الحركة مشروط بانقضاء الجزء المتقدم . وعلى هذا التقدير لا يلزم من دوام العلة الموجبة لتلك الحركة ، دوام كل جزء من أجزاء تلك الحركة. وهذا الكلام قد أطنبنا في تقريره، حيث نقلنا و مذهب الحكماء في كيفية كون المبدأ (4) ] القديم علة لوجود الحوادث المتعاقبة
For it might be said: Why is it not permissible that the effecter in the existence of these motions be a necessitator by essence? He said: 'And it does not follow that every part of the assumed parts in that motion endures because of the endurance of that necessitator.' We say: We do not concede that this is necessary. Its clarification: the necessitator for the descent of a heavy object is its natural weight, and that weight remains in all states. Yet from the remaining of that weight, the remaining of every single one of the assumed parts in the motion of that heavy object does not follow. So too here. Rather the verification is that we say: The necessitation of that weight for the later part of the motion is conditional upon the occurrence of the prior part. So the necessitator and effecter is the weight, but its necessitation for each part of the assumed parts in that motion is conditional upon the termination of the prior part. On this supposition, from the perpetuity of the necessitating cause of that motion, the perpetuity of every part of the parts of that motion does not follow. This statement we have elaborated upon in its exposition, where we transmitted the doctrine of the philosophers concerning how the eternal principle is a cause for the existence of successive temporal events.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector asks: Why can't the effecter of these motions be a necessitator (mūjib bi-al-dhāt)? The author responds by first quoting the objector's argument: 'It does not follow that every part endures because the cause endures.' Then the author denies that this is necessary. He provides a clarification using the analogy of a heavy object (al-thaqīl) falling due to its natural weight. The weight is a necessitating cause that remains constant, yet it does not require that every part of the motion (i.e., each spatial position) endures. Instead, the weight's necessitation for a later part is conditional upon the prior part having already occurred. Similarly, for celestial motions, the necessitating cause (if it were such) would necessitate each part only after the prior part has ended. Thus the perpetuity of the cause does not entail the perpetuity of each part; rather, the parts are successive. After explaining this, the author notes that he has already discussed this topic in detail when transmitting the philosophers' view on how the eternal principle can be a cause for successive events. This refutes the objection by showing that even a necessary cause can produce a sequence of distinct parts without requiring each part to endure indefinitely.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A necessary cause cannot produce successive effects; all effects must coexist.
Correction: The analogy of a heavy object shows that a necessary cause can produce a sequence by conditioning each later part on the prior part's cessation.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the heavy object analogy in this passage?
A) To show that weight is not a necessary cause.
B) To demonstrate that a necessary cause can produce successive parts by conditioning each part on the prior part's cessation.

Key Takeaway
The heavy object analogy demonstrates that necessary causation does not require all parts of motion to coexist; succession is possible through conditional necessitation.

?

Do you become impatient when you don't see the whole change at once? What would it mean for you to accept the conditional nature of personal transformation, and to stay constant in your cause without demanding immediate total results?

First objection: the agent could be a necessitating cause
Refutes the objection that a necessitating cause implies perpetual parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a necessitating cause can produce temporal effects sequentially without each part being eternal, using the example of natural heaviness.

Core Idea
A permanent cause can produce sequential effects without each part being permanent.

This passage addresses an important objection: if the agent were a necessitating cause, wouldn't its effect be eternal and unchanging? The author refutes this by showing that even an unchanging cause can produce a series of distinct events, as illustrated by the natural heaviness of a falling object. Understanding this refutation is key to grasping how an eternal God can create temporal events.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the opening question and the author's brief reply. Notice how he denies the necessity of each part enduring.
The objector assumes that a necessitating cause requires its entire effect to be simultaneous and perpetual.
2
Examine the analogy of heaviness
Read the analogy: the heaviness of a heavy object remains constant, yet the parts of its motion do not persist. Understand why this analogy is effective.
3
Grasp the conditional causation
The author clarifies that each part of the motion is conditional on the prior part's passing away. The cause remains, but its effect is sequential.
This is the key logical insight: causation can be conditional.
4
Reflect on the divine application
Consider how this applies to God as an eternal cause creating temporal events. The author references a fuller discussion elsewhere.
This shows that a necessitating cause is compatible with temporal succession.

If it is said: 'Why is it not permissible that the efficient cause in the existence of these movements be a necessitating cause by essence?' – his statement: 'And it is not necessary that every one of the assumed parts of that movement endure because of the endurance of that necessitating cause.' We say: We do not accept that this is necessary. Its clarification: The cause for the descent of a heavy object is its natural heaviness, and that heaviness remains in all circumstances. Yet from the remaining of that heaviness, the remaining of every one of the assumed parts in the movement of that heavy object does not follow. So too here. Rather, the truth is that we say: The necessitation of that heaviness for the later part of the movement is conditional upon the occurrence of the prior part. So the necessitating cause and the efficient cause is the heaviness, except that its necessitation for each part of the assumed parts of that movement is conditional upon the passing away of the prior part. On this assumption, it does not follow from the endurance of the necessitating cause of that movement the endurance of every part of that movement. And we have elaborated this speech in its exposition where we have transmitted the doctrine of the philosophers concerning how the eternal principle is a cause for the existence of successive temporal events.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objector asks: Why can't the agent causing these movements be a necessitating cause by essence, implying that if the cause is eternal, its effect would be eternal, thus no beginning? The author responds by denying the necessity of each part enduring due to the cause's permanence. He provides an analogy: the natural heaviness of a heavy body remains constant, yet it does not necessitate that every part of its motion persists. Rather, the heaviness causes each later part conditionally upon the prior part's passing away. Similarly, an eternal necessitating cause can produce temporal events sequentially without requiring each part to be eternal. The passage concludes that this reasoning has been elaborated elsewhere in the work.

Objections to Divine Agency and Their Replies
Objections and replies on divine agency, totality, and time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam scholars defend divine agency against objections regarding the totality of actions, the necessity of origination, and the beginning of time.

Core Idea
Arguments against divine agency often fail due to circularity, infinite regress, or confusion about totality.

This passage presents four objections to the kalam view of divine agency and causality, along with responses from the author. Each objection challenges different aspects of how God acts in relation to created events and time. The author's replies demonstrate careful logical reasoning to preserve the doctrine of divine voluntary agency and the world's origination.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dialectical structure
Read the passage and note that it begins mid-argument, then presents the third and fourth objections, followed by answers to the first, second, and third questions. Recognise the back-and-forth format typical of kalam.
The first two objections are not fully stated in this excerpt, but their answers are given.
2
Understand the third objection
The opponent concedes that total movements are from a voluntary agent but questions why that requires temporal origination. Study how the author reframes the issue around efficacy (mu'thiriyya).
3
Examine the fourth objection
This objection attacks the proof for time's beginning, claiming it leads to an infinite regress before the first moment and contradicts intuitive reason. Notice how the author counters by arguing that the opponent's reasoning is obscure.
The answer to this objection is not fully given in this passage.
4
Analyze the author's replies
Review the answers to the first three objections. The first shows that the opponent's assumption leads to circularity or regress. The second clarifies that the totality is not separate from its parts. The third (incomplete) argues that voluntary agency implies causal priority, not necessarily temporal.
The third answer ends mid-sentence, but the main point is discernible.

For each one of them, but we do not concede: that He, exalted, is an agent of the totality of those movements, inasmuch as it is a totality. The proof for it is: the totality is necessarily obtained at the occurrence of each one of the individuals, and what is necessarily obtained by a cause is impossible to need another cause. The third question: we concede that the totality of movements is the act of a voluntary agent, so why do you say: everything that is an act of a voluntary agent must be originated (muhdath)? And fifteen proofs have preceded, in what came before: that what needs an agent does not have to be preceded by non-existence in a temporal precedence. And what we affirm is: this voluntary agent, either everything necessary for the occurrence of that efficacy (al-mu'thiriyya) has been obtained from him, or it has not been obtained. If the first, then the non-occurrence of the effect is impossible, as established previously. And if the second, then the occurrence of those considerations needs another agent. The fourth question: the proof that you mentioned, if it is established and sound, then time must have a beginning. And that is impossible, because every time that is supposed to be the first of all times, our clear reason affirms a before for it, and judges that its non-existence was established before it. And if your proof necessitates ending at a before the first, without a before before it, and that is repudiated by the intuition of reason, and affirming what is in accordance with intuitions is more appropriate than affirming what is in accordance with the obscure, ambiguous proof that you mentioned, we know then that your speech is invalid. And God knows best. The answer: As for the first question, which is his statement: that the emanation of each part of the movement from that eternal necessitating cause is conditioned by the expiration of the part that preceded it. Its answer is what has been previously established: that the becoming of that necessitating cause an agent in the occurrence of that subsequent part, after it was not an agent in it, inevitably requires a cause. And if that cause is that accident, then circularity or something else follows, and infinite regress follows, and both are impossible. So it is established that this speech is invalid. And someone may say: the pivot of this speech is that the thing of which it is true that it was not an agent and then it became an agent—that efficacy inevitably needs a cause, a reason, and a necessitator. We say: if this speech is true, then the statement of the origination of the world is invalidated. Because if the world were originated, it would be true of the Creator, exalted, that He was not an agent in it. And then the origination of that efficacy would need a cause, a reason, and a necessitator. If that cause is the world, circularity follows; if it is something else, infinite regress follows. So it is established: that the origination of efficacy, if it necessitates what you mentioned, necessitates for you the certainty of the corruption of the statement of the world's origination. And if the origination of efficacy does not necessitate its need for another cause, then this speech falls entirely. As for the second question, which is your statement: He, exalted, is an agent of each one of those events, or an agent of their totality. We say: since you conceded that He, exalted, is an agent of each one of those events, He must be an agent of their totality. His statement: the totality is necessarily obtained at the occurrence of its individuals. We say: do you mean by it that it is necessarily obtained at each one of its individuals, or do you mean by it that it is necessarily obtained at the totality of its individuals? The first is clearly corrupt, because ten is not necessarily obtained at the occurrence of each of its individuals. The second is also clearly corrupt, because ten is nothing but the sum of its individuals. So then the upshot of the speech reduces to: the totality is necessarily obtained at the occurrence of the totality, and that requires explaining a thing by itself, and that is impossible. As for the third question, which is his statement: the priority of the agent over the act is not necessary. We say: this is false, because the voluntary agent is the one who acts by means of
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (objection-and-reply) typical of kalam. The author first addresses a residual point about the totality of movements, then presents two new objections (the third and fourth), and then gives answers to the first three questions (the first two having been introduced earlier). The passage demonstrates the careful logical reasoning used to defend divine agency and the origination of the world. The third objection argues that if God is a voluntary agent, His act does not necessarily need to be temporally preceded by non-existence. The author responds by distinguishing whether all conditions for efficacy are present or not. The fourth objection attacks the proof for time's beginning, claiming it leads to absurdities and conflicts with intuitive reason. The author's answers show that the opponent's arguments either lead to circularity, infinite regress, or self-contradiction. The second answer clarifies that the totality of events cannot be treated as a separate entity requiring a distinct act, as it is nothing but the sum of its parts. The incomplete third answer begins by asserting that the voluntary agent acts through a means (bi-wasita).

intermediate
6 min
Objection on Efficacy and Its Refutation
Author presents an objection and its refutation regarding efficacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against the proof of temporal origination based on the efficiency of the Creator and the author's refutation showing that the objection leads to a self-defeating dilemma.

Core Idea
The objection that God's efficiency must have a cause collapses into a dilemma that refutes itself.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection against the kalam proof of the temporal origination of the world. The objector argues that if God's effectiveness changed from non-effective to effective, that change itself would need a cause, leading to either circularity or infinite regress. The author responds by showing that the objector cannot consistently maintain this position without undermining his own argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objector's reasoning
Read the passage carefully to grasp the objector's claim: a change in efficacy requires a cause, leading to circularity or infinite regress.
Focus on the first part of the passage: from 'الجزء المتأخر' to 'فثبت : أن هذا الكلام باطل'.
2
Identify the author's dilemma
Notice how the author responds by presenting a dilemma: either the change in efficacy requires a cause (which would disprove temporal origination) or it does not (which collapses the objection).
The key phrase is 'إن أوجب ما ذكرتم' and 'أما إن لم يوجب'.
3
Analyze the logical structure
Trace how the author turns the objection against itself, forcing the objector into an untenable position.
The author shows that the objection is self-defeating.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider why this defense strengthens the original proof of temporal origination.
This refutation shows the robustness of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجزء المتأخر ، بعد د أن كانت غير مؤثرة فيه : لا بد له من سبب ، وذلك السبب إن كان هو ذلك الحادث، لزم الدور أو غيره. ويلزم التسلسل . وكلاهما محالان. فثبت : أن هذا الكلام باطل
ولقائل أن يقول : مدار هذا الكلام على أن الشيء الذي يصدق عليه : أنه ما كان مؤثراً ، ثم صدق عليه أنه صار مؤثراً . فإن تلك المؤثرية لا بد لها من سبب وعلة وموجب ، فنقول : إن صدق هذا الكلام ، فقد بطل القول بحدوث العالم . لأنه لو كان العالم محدثاً لصدق على البارى تعالى ، أنه ما كان مؤثراً فيه . وحينئذ يلزم افتقار تلك المؤثرية الحادثة إلى سبب وعلة وموجب . فإن كان ذلك السبب هو العالم ، لزم الدور . وإن كان شيئاً آخر ، لزم التسلسل . فثبت : أن حدوث المؤثرية إن أوجب ما ذكرتم ، الزمكم القطع بفساد القول ، بحدوث العالم. وأما إن لم يوجب حدوث المؤثرية : افتقارها إلى سبب آخر ، فقد سقط هذا الكلام بالكلية
The posterior part, after [that] it was not effective in it: it must have a cause. If that cause is that event, circularity or other [vicious circle] follows. Infinite regress also follows. Both are impossible. So it is established: this statement is false. And one may say: the gist of this statement is that a thing which is described as having not been effective, then is described as having become effective: that effectiveness must have a cause and ground and necessitator. We say: if this statement is true, then the claim of the temporal origination of the world is nullified. Because if the world were temporally originated, the Exalted Creator would be described as not having been effective in it. Then that temporal effectiveness would necessarily depend on a cause and ground and necessitator. If that cause is the world, circularity follows. If it is something else, infinite regress follows. So it is established: that the temporal origination of effectiveness, if it necessitates what you mentioned, would compel you to the certainty of the corruption of the claim of the temporal origination of the world. But if the temporal origination of effectiveness does not necessitate its dependence on another cause, then this whole statement collapses entirely.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is an objection and response. The author first states the problem with the posterior part needing a cause, leading to circularity or infinite regress, concluding that the opponent's claim is false. Then the objector argues that the argument hinges on the change of a thing from non-effective to effective, which requires a cause. If this is true, it would disprove the world's temporal origination because God's effectiveness would then be temporal and need a cause, leading to circularity or regress. The author responds by presenting a dilemma: if the temporal origination of effectiveness requires a cause as you claim, then your argument forces you to deny the temporal origination of the world; if it does not require a cause, then your whole objection collapses. The author thus defends the original proof.

Sixth Proof: Past Cycles Must Be Finite
Author proves past cycles must have a beginning and responds to an objection about infinite past.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that past cycles must have a beginning and the key objection that something can be infinite on one side but finite on the other.

Core Idea
An infinite number of past events cannot be traversed to reach today.

The sixth proof tackles the possibility of an infinite past. The author argues that if past cycles were infinite, they could never end, yet we observe their end at the present day. He then addresses an opponent who claims that the series is infinite only from the past side. This lesson walks through the proof and the objection, showing why an infinite past is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a feel for the argument. Note the structure: proof, objection, clarification, and counter-objection.
The text ends abruptly, so do not expect a complete response.
2
Identify the key premises
The proof is a conditional: if no beginning then infinite, infinite has no end, but we observe an end, so there is a beginning. Identify each premise in the passage.
Think about the logical necessity of the argument.
3
Analyze the objection
The opponent says the series is infinite only from the past side, not from our side. This is a common counterargument. Understand why it seems plausible.
The proponent responds that the argument is about number, not direction.
4
Examine the proponent’s clarification
The proponent clarifies that the impossibility arises from the number of cycles being infinite, causing a contradiction when trying to reach a specific point like today.
This shifts the focus from 'direction' to 'magnitude'.
5
Reflect on the incomplete response
The objector’s counter is cut off, but we see he argues that an infinite number starting from a specific time cannot end at another. Consider why this would be problematic.
This points to the issue of traversing an infinite in actuality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : لو لم يكن للأدوار الماضية أول ، لكانت الأدوار الماضية غير متناهية، وما لا نهاية له ، لا ينتهي . فلو كانت الأدوار الماضية غير متناهية ، لامتنع ابتداؤها (۳) ولما دل الحس على انتهاء الأدوار الماضية [ إلى هذا اليوم (۱) ] علمنا : أن الأدوار الماضية لها أول .
(٤)
اعترض الخصم عليه . فقال : إن هذه الحوادث لا نهاية لها من جانب الأزل ، وهي متناهية من الجانب الذي يلينا ويتصل بنا . وكونه غير متناه من أحد الجانبين ، لا يناقض كونه متناهياً من الجانب الآخر . فإن قال المستدل : المراد من هذا الدليل : أن الدورات الماضية لو كانت غير متناهية في العدد ، لامتنع انتهاؤها إلى طرف معين، ومقطع معين ، وحينئذ يظهر وجه الاستدلال

The sixth proof: If past cycles had no beginning, then past cycles would be infinite, and that which has no end does not end. So if past cycles were infinite, their beginning would be impossible. And since sense perception indicates the ending of past cycles [up to this day], we know that past cycles have a beginning.
(٤)
The opponent objected to him and said: These events have no end from the side of pre-eternity, but they are finite from the side that faces us and connects with us. And its being infinite on one side does not contradict its being finite on the other side. For if the proponent says: The intended meaning of this proof is that if past cycles were infinite in number, their ending at a specific point and specific segment would be impossible, and then the aspect of the proof becomes clear.
The objector said: Numbers that are infinite, if they begin from a specific time, their ending at another time is impossible, such that it is judged that everything infinite has come into actuality between these two points. Because on this estimation, the duration of their occurrence would be...
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof and an objection. The proof argues: if past cycles had no beginning, they would be infinite, and what is infinite does not end. Since sense perception shows that past cycles end at the present day, they must have a beginning. The opponent objects that these events are infinite only from the past side but finite from our side, and being infinite on one side does not contradict being finite on the other. The proponent clarifies that the argument is about number: if past cycles were infinite in number, they could not end at a specific point. The opponent then argues that infinite numbers, if they start from a specific time, cannot end at another time because that would require the actualization of an infinite between two points. The text ends here, leaving the response incomplete.

Refuting a Circular Objection to the Proof of Temporal Finitude
The author refutes a circular objection to the proof of temporal finitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a circular objection begs the question in kalam arguments about the temporal finitude of the world.

Core Idea
A circular argument assumes what it tries to prove.

In this passage, the author of al-Matalib al-'Aliyah addresses an objection that attempts to show that the proof of temporal finitude is invalid due to circularity. He demonstrates that the objection itself is circular because it presupposes the very conclusion it seeks to refute. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins mid-sentence, continuing from a prior discussion.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The author contrasts two scenarios: (1) assuming events began at a specific time, which makes the objection circular, and (2) removing that condition, which makes the objection merely restate the dispute.
This is the core logical distinction.
3
Recognise the circularity
Understand that the objection's validity depends on the very conclusion it tries to disprove. This is a classic begging-the-question fallacy.
The author's critique is not about the impossibility of an actual infinite, but about logical consistency.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that such an objection cannot serve as a valid proof because it either begs the question or merely asserts the disputed point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محصورة بين حاصرين، وذلك يمنع من القول بكونها غير متناهية في العدد. إلا أن هذا المحال، إنما يلزم، لو قلنا: إن تلك الحوادث ابتدأت بالحدوث من وقت معين. وهذا هو عين المطلوب. فتصير صحة الدليل موقوفة على صحة المطلوب. وذلك باطل. وأما إن رفعنا هذا الشرط، وقلنا: إن الحوادث التي لا أول لها، يمتنع انتهاؤها إلى طرف معين من الجانب الذي يلينا. فهذا محل النزاع، فلا يمكن جعله دليلاً على صحة محل النزاع.
Confined between two limits, and that prevents saying that they are infinite in number. But this impossibility only follows if we say that those events began to occur from a specific time. And that is exactly the sought conclusion. So the validity of the proof becomes dependent on the validity of the sought conclusion, and that is invalid. But if we remove this condition and say that events which have no beginning are prevented from ending at a specific limit on the side facing us, then this is the point of dispute, so it cannot be made a proof for the validity of the disputed point.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection that attempts to argue for the impossibility of an infinite regress. The author points out that the objection implicitly assumes that the series of past events has a starting point (i.e., that they began at a specific time). But that assumption is precisely what the proof is trying to establish—the temporal beginning of the world. Thus, the objection is circular: its validity depends on the very conclusion it seeks to prove. If, instead, one removes the condition of a beginning and simply says that an infinite series cannot reach us, then that is merely an assertion of the disputed point, not a proof. The author concludes that such an objection cannot serve as a valid proof because it either begs the question or merely restates the claim at issue.

Key Terms
الدليل
المطلوب
محل النزاع
الحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the author is denying the impossibility of an actual infinite regress.
Correction: The author is not denying the impossibility of an actual infinite; he is showing that the objection's formulation is circular.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the objection that events are confined between two limits?
A) Because the objection is self-contradictory.
B) Because the objection assumes the very conclusion it aims to prove.
C) Because the objection relies on a false premise.
D) Because the objection is irrelevant to the proof.

Key Takeaway
A valid proof cannot presuppose the very conclusion it aims to establish, otherwise it is circular and invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson shows that an objection can disguise its own assumption. In your own life, when you argue with others about religious matters, can you identify when you or they might be assuming the conclusion? What would it take to be honest enough to remove that assumption?
?
The author says a proof cannot be based on the disputed point. When it comes to your own certainty in tawheed, are you building it on unexamined assumptions? Take a moment to examine the foundation of your conviction—is it truly built on sound proof or on assumptions you've never questioned?
