Classification of Proponents of Eternal Bodies
Classifies the two groups who claim eternal bodies and states the aim to refute the second group.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main groups who claim bodies are eternal and how the author aims to refute the second group to prove the temporal origination of bodies.

Core Idea
The belief in eternal bodies is split into those claiming eternal motion and those claiming eternal rest.

In this passage, the author categorises the various philosophical schools that assert the eternity of bodies. He identifies two major groups, each with its own account of the universe's pre-eternal state. Understanding this classification is key to following his subsequent argument that bodies must have come into existence in time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main groups
Read the passage and note the author's statement that those who claim eternal bodies are divided into two groups. The first group believes in eternal motion; the second believes in eternal rest.
2
Examine the subdivisions of the first group
The first group consists of two factions: Aristotle's followers (world eternal in matter, form, shape, with eternal motion) and Democritus's followers (eternal indivisible parts in chaotic motion forming the heavens by collision).
3
Understand the author's aim
Notice that the author states he will refute the second group (eternal rest) to establish the temporal origination of bodies, implying that the first group is refuted by proving eternal motion impossible.
The author focuses on refuting the second group to complete his argument.

في

أحدهما : الذين يقولون : العالم قديم بمادته وصورته وشكله ، وأن الأفلاك والكواكب متحركة ازلاً وأبداً . وهذا قول « أرسطاطاليس ،

والثاني : الذين يقولون : الموجود في الأزل أجزاء غير متجزئة وكانت متحركة حركات مضطربة من الأزل إلى الأبد ، ثم اتفق لها في حركاتها أن تصارمت فتكون منها السموات . وهذا قول : « ديمقراطيس ، وأصحابه فإذا دللنا على امتناع وجود الحركة الأزلية ، فقد بطل هذان القولان .
وأما الفريق الثاني : فهم الذين يقولون : العالم قديم المادة ومحدث الصورة ، وزعموا : أن مادة العالم أجزاء صغيرة ، وكانت ساكنة في الأزل . ثم إنه تعالى حركها وركب العالم منها . وهذا هو قول : أكثر الفلاسفة الذين كانوا

CHAPTER THREE: Concerning the Explanation that a Body Cannot Be at Rest in Eternity. Know that those who assert the eternity of bodies are two groups. The first group: those who say that bodies were in motion in pre-eternity. And these are two factions: One: those who say that the world is eternal in its matter, form, and shape, and that the celestial spheres and planets are in motion eternally and forever. This is the opinion of Aristotle and his followers. The second: those who say that what exists in pre-eternity is indivisible parts and they were in agitated motions from pre-eternity to eternity, then it happened that in their motions they collided and the heavens came into being from them. This is the opinion of Democritus and his followers. So if we demonstrate the impossibility of eternal motion, both these opinions become false. As for the second group: they are those who say that the world is eternal in matter and temporally originated in form, and they claim that the matter of the world is small parts, and they were at rest in pre-eternity. Then He, exalted, moved them and composed the world from them. This is the opinion of most philosophers before Aristotle. And we are currently engaged in refuting this school, and if that is falsified, then the assertion of the temporal origination of bodies is established for us.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage begins by classifying those who believe in the eternity of bodies into two major groups. The first group claims that bodies were moving in pre-eternity; they divide into two factions: those like Aristotle who say the universe is eternal in every aspect and that celestial spheres move eternally, and those like Democritus who claim pre-eternal indivisible parts were in chaotic motion and accidentally formed the heavens. The author says if he can prove that eternal motion is impossible, these two views are refuted. Then he turns to the second group: those who believe matter is eternal but form is created; they claim that eternal tiny parts were at rest, and God later moved them to form the world. This is the view of most pre-Aristotelian philosophers. The author states that his current task is to refute this particular school, and by doing so, he will have proven that bodies are temporally originated (hadith al-ajsam). This sets up the argument that follows: proving that eternal rest is impossible.

Key Terms
قدم الأجسام (eternity of bodies)
أزلي (pre-eternal)
سكون (rest)
حركة (motion)
قديم المادة (eternal in matter)
محدث الصورة (temporally originated in form)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is attacking all philosophers who believe in eternal bodies.
Correction: The author is only targeting the second group (those who assert eternal rest). He notes that if eternal motion is impossible, the first group is also refuted, but his immediate focus is the second group.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The author classifies proponents of eternal bodies into two groups—those who believe in eternal motion and those who believe in eternal rest—and focuses on refuting the latter to prove the temporal origination of bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author aims to refute the idea that matter could have existed eternally at rest before God moved it. If it is impossible for matter to be at rest eternally (as the author will argue), what does that imply about your own need for a Creator who gives existence and motion at every moment?
?
The philosophers before Aristotle thought that matter was eternal but at rest, and then God moved it. Do you ever treat your own life as if the 'raw material' exists independently and only needs a little divine intervention? How does this lesson challenge you to see God as the origin of everything, including the very existence of matter?

The Proof Against Eternal Rest and Its Dependency on Existence
Sanusi presents the proof against eternal rest and refutes the objection from non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof that eternal rest is impossible hinges on rest being an existent attribute and not mere non-existence.

Core Idea
Eternal non-existence can cease, so the proof fails if rest is non-existence.

Sanusi begins by constructing a straightforward syllogism to show that rest cannot be eternal. However, he quickly realizes that the proof's validity depends on whether rest is an actual existent quality or merely the absence of motion. He then engages with a sophisticated objection from opponents who argue that if eternal non-existence can cease, then the world could be eternal. This passage thus forces us to think carefully about what it means for something to be 'eternal' and what kinds of things can cease.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, paying attention to the flow from the proof to the objection to the resolution.
The argument is dense; don't worry if you don't catch everything on the first read.
2
Identify the initial syllogism
Find the simple syllogism: If rest were eternal, it could not cease; but rest does cease; therefore it is not eternal. Notice that this relies on the premise that 'eternal things cannot cease'.
Sanusi later shows this premise must be qualified.
3
Understand the objection
If rest is merely the absence of motion (non-existence), then the syllogism fails because eternal non-existence can cease, as shown by the example of the world's non-existence before creation.
4
Analyze the opponent's two arguments
The opponent uses the same reasoning to argue that if the world is created, its non-existence was eternal and cannot cease, so the world must be eternal. Similarly for God's attribute of being a creator.
These arguments mirror the structure of Sanusi's own proof.
5
Grasp Sanusi's resolution
Sanusi restricts the principle 'what is eternal cannot cease' to existent things only. He then concludes that to salvage his proof against eternal rest, he must first show that rest is an existent attribute.
This sets the stage for the next part of his argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنا نحتاج في هذا المقام إلى إقامة الدلالة على أنه يمتنع كون السكون أزلياً. ودليلنا على إثبات هذا المطلوب : أن نقول : لو كان السكون أزلياً ، لما جاز زواله . وقد جاز زواله ، فيمتنع كون السكون أزلياً . واعلم أن بتقدير أن يكون السكون عبارة عن عدم الحركة فإنه لا يصح هذا الدليل . لأن زوال العدم الأزلي جائز بالاتفاق ، إذ لو لم يجز ذلك ، لبطل القول بحدوث الاجسام . وبيانه من وجهين :
الأول : إن الخصم يقول : لو كان العالم حدثاً ، لكان عدمه أزلياً . ولو كان عدمه أزلياً ، لامتنع زواله. فكان يجب أن لا يوجد العالم . وحيث وجد ، علمنا أن عدمه كان أزلياً . ولما لم يكن عدمه أزليا ، وجب أن يكون وجوده ازلياً .
والثاني : إنه لو كان العالم محدثاً ، لما كان البارى تعالى موجوداً له في الأزل ، ولا مؤثراً فيه . ولو كان عدم الموجدية والمؤثرية أزلياً لما زال هذا العدم . وحيث زال ، علمنا أن علم الموجدية ليس أزلياً ، فوجب أن يكون حصول الموجدية أزلياً وذلك يقتضي قدم العالم . فيثبت بهذا : أنه لا يمكن أن يقال : إن كل ما كان أزلياً ، فإنه يمتنع زواله .
بل يجب تخصيص هذه الدعوى بالأمور الوجودية . فيقال: إن كل ما كان موجودا في الأزل ، فإنه يمتنع زواله ، وإذا ثبت هذا ، فيفتقر في تقرير هذا المطلوب إلى ثلاثة أمور :
فالأول : بيان أن السكون صفة موجودة .
And know that we need in this context to establish a proof that it is impossible for rest to be eternal. Our proof for establishing this required conclusion is to say: If rest were eternal, then its cessation would not be permissible. But its cessation is permissible, so it is impossible for rest to be eternal. And know that if it is assumed that rest is a term for the absence of motion, then this proof is not valid. Because the cessation of eternal non-existence is permissible by agreement; for if that were not permissible, the statement of the temporal origination of bodies would be falsified. And its explanation is from two aspects: The first: The opponent says: If the world were temporally originated, then its non-existence would be eternal. And if its non-existence were eternal, then its cessation would be impossible. So it would have to be that the world does not exist. But since it does exist, we know that its non-existence was not eternal. And since its non-existence was not eternal, its existence must be eternal. The second: If the world were temporally originated, then the Creator, exalted, would not have been existent to it in pre-eternity, nor effective upon it. And if the non-existence of effect-possession and effectivation were eternal, then this non-existence would not cease. But it did cease, so we know that the attribute of effect-possession is not eternal, so the obtainment of effect-possession must be eternal, and that necessitates the eternity of the world. So by this it is established that it cannot be said that everything that is eternal cannot cease. Rather, this claim must be restricted to existential matters. So it is said: Everything that existed in pre-eternity cannot cease. And once this is established, then for setting up this required conclusion, we need three things: The first: To demonstrate that rest is an existent attribute.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage presents a crucial proof in the author's argument for the temporal origination of bodies. The author begins by stating that he needs to prove that eternal rest is impossible. He gives a simple syllogism: If rest were eternal, it could not cease. But rest does cease (because we observe motion beginning after rest), so it cannot be eternal. However, he immediately notes a complication. If we define rest merely as the absence of motion (a non-existence), then the proof fails because it is generally agreed that an eternal non-existence can cease. For example, the non-existence of the world is eternal if the world is created, yet that non-existence ceases when the world comes into being. The author then explains the opponent's argument from two angles. First, the opponent argues: if the world is created, its non-existence is eternal; but if eternal non-existence cannot cease, then the world would never come to be. Since the world does exist, its non-existence was not eternal, so its existence must be eternal. Second, the opponent argues regarding the attribute of being a creator: if the world is created, then God's being a creator (mujidiyya) is not eternal; but if that non-existence of creator-ness is eternal, it cannot cease; yet it did cease when God became creator, so the non-existence was not eternal, implying that God's creator-ness is eternal and thus the world is eternal. The author responds by concluding that the principle 'what is eternal cannot cease' applies only to existent entities, not to non-existence. Therefore, he must first establish that rest is an existent attribute (not mere non-existence) to make his proof valid. This leads to his next steps: he will need to prove that rest is an existent quality, then complete his argument.

Key Terms
سكون (rest)
عدم (non-existence)
أزلي (eternal)
موجود (existent)
زوال (cessation)
موجدية (effect-possession/creator-ness)
مؤثرية (effectivation/influence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Whatever is eternal cannot cease, even non-existence.
Correction: Only existent eternal entities cannot cease; eternal non-existence can and does cease (e.g., the non-existence of the world before creation).

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the initial proof against eternal rest invalid if rest is defined as the absence of motion?
A) Because the proof relies on a fallacious syllogism
B) Because eternal non-existence can cease by agreement
C) Because the opponent does not believe in rest
D) Because motion is eternal

Key Takeaway
The proof against eternal rest only works if rest is an existent attribute, not a mere non-existence, because eternal non-existence can cease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson argues that God's attribute of being Creator (mūjidiyya) is eternal, not acquired. Reflect: Do you live with a daily awareness that God has always been your Creator and Provider, or do you sometimes treat Him as if He only becomes relevant when you face a need? What would change if you internalized that His creative power never had a beginning?
?
Sanusi shows that eternal non-existence can cease, but eternal existence cannot. Think of your own spiritual habits: Are you building your faith on the absence of doubt (a kind of non-existence) or on the presence of positive certainty? If your faith is merely the 'absence of disbelief', it may not last. What step can you take today to cultivate an active, existent reliance on God?
?
The opponent argues that if the world originated, God's effectiveness (muʾthiriyya) would not be eternal, but we know God became effective when the world began. This challenges you: Do you assume that God's involvement in your life is triggered only by your actions (prayer, obedience), or do you believe that His power and mercy have always been active toward you, even before you turn to Him? How might this shape your trust in Him right now?

The Proof That Eternal Rest Is Impossible
Author states the main proof and its fundamental condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument against eternal rest only works if rest is considered an existing attribute, not mere absence.

Core Idea
Eternal rest is impossible only if rest is a real attribute, not just the absence of motion.

The author presents a crisp logical proof that eternal rest cannot exist. However, he immediately adds a crucial condition: the proof depends on rest being a positive attribute. If rest is merely the absence of motion, the proof collapses because the removal of eternal non-existence is accepted as possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the Arabic proof carefully. Notice its logical form: if eternal rest then no cessation; but cessation is possible; therefore rest is not eternal.
This is a classic modus tollens argument.
2
Understand the qualification
The author then states that this proof only holds if rest is a real (existing) attribute. If it is just absence of motion, the proof fails.
Why? Because eternal absence can cease, as agreed upon.
3
Reflect on the implication
The failure of the proof if rest is absence of motion would undermine the doctrine of temporal origination of bodies, so the condition is critical.
This shows the author's careful reasoning about ontological categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليلنا على إثبات هذا المطلوب : أن نقول : لو كان السكون أزلياً ، لما جاز زواله . وقد جاز زواله ، فيمتنع كون السكون أزلياً . واعلم أن بتقدير أن يكون السكون عبارة عن عدم الحركة فإنه لا يصح هذا الدليل . لأن زوال العدم الأزلي جائز بالاتفاق ، إذ لو لم يجز ذلك ، لبطل القول بحدوث الاجسام . وبيانه من وجهين : الأول : إن الخصم يقول : لو كان العالم حدثاً ، لكان عدمه أزلياً . ولو كان عدمه أزلياً ، لامتنع زواله. فكان يجب أن لا يوجد العالم . وحيث وجد ، علمنا أن عدمه كان أزلياً . ولما لم يكن عدمه أزليا ، وجب أن يكون وجوده أزلياً . والثاني : إنه لو كان العالم محدثاً ، لما كان البارى تعالى موجوداً له في الأزل ، ولا مؤثراً فيه . ولو كان [ عدم (۱) ] الموجدية والمؤثرية أزلياً لما زال هذا العدم . وحيث زال ، علمنا أن علم الموجدية ليس أزلياً ، فوجب أن يكون حصول الموجدية أزلياً وذلك يقتضي قدم العالم . فيثبت بهذا : أنه لا يمكن أن يقال : إن كل ما كان أزلياً ، فإنه يمتنع زواله . بل يجب تخصيص هذه الدعوى بالأمور الوجودية . فيقال: إن كل ما كان موجودا في الأزل ، فإنه يمتنع زواله.
Our proof for establishing this required claim is: we say: if rest were eternal, its cessation would not be permissible. But its cessation is permissible, so it is impossible for rest to be eternal. Know that with the assumption that rest is an expression of the absence of motion, this proof does not hold. Because the removal of eternal non-existence is permissible by consensus, since if that were not permissible, the statement of the temporal origination of bodies would be invalid. Its explanation is from two aspects: The first: the opponent says: if the world were temporally originated, its non-existence would be eternal. And if its non-existence were eternal, its removal would be impossible. So the world would necessarily not exist. But when it exists, we know that its non-existence was not eternal. And since its non-existence was not eternal, its existence must be eternal. The second: if the world were temporally originated, the Exalted Creator would not have been existent for it in eternity, nor effective upon it. And if the non-existence of bringing-into-existence and effecting were eternal, this non-existence would not cease. But when it ceased, we know that the non-existence of bringing-into-existence is not eternal, so the obtaining of bringing-into-existence must be eternal, and that necessitates the pre-eternity of the world. Thus it is established by this: that it cannot be said that everything that is eternal, its cessation is impossible. Rather, this claim must be specified to existent things. So it is said: everything that existed in eternity, its cessation is impossible.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage presents the main proof that eternal rest is impossible and then addresses an objection. The author first states the proof: if rest were eternal, it could not cease; but it does cease, so it is not eternal. However, this proof only works if rest is an existing attribute. If rest is merely the absence of motion, then the proof fails because the removal of eternal non-existence is generally accepted as possible. To illustrate that removal of eternal non-existence is possible, the author provides two arguments (wajhan). The first argument uses the example of the world's temporality: if the world is originated, its non-existence was eternal, but that non-existence ceased when the world came into being. The second argument concerns the Creator's attributes: if the world is originated, God's attribute of bringing-into-existence (mujidiyya) and effecting (mu'thiriyya) were non-existent in eternity, but they become existent when the world is created. Therefore, it is not true that everything eternal is immutable; rather, only things that exist in eternity cannot cease. This sets the stage for further investigation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof against eternal rest is universal and applies whether rest is an attribute or absence.
Correction: The author clarifies that the proof only works if rest is an existing attribute; if it is mere absence, the proof does not hold.

Checkpoint
Why does the proof against eternal rest fail if rest is defined as the absence of motion?
A) Because absence of motion is not a real attribute.
B) Because the removal of eternal non-existence is permissible by consensus.
C) Because rest is not eternal.
D) Because the proof is circular.

Key Takeaway
The argument that eternal rest is impossible is valid only if rest is considered a positive, existing attribute; otherwise, it is inconclusive.

?
The author conditions his proof carefully. Do you condition your faith on solid foundations, or do you accept theological claims without examination? This lesson demands that you inspect the premises of your beliefs. Are you willing to reexamine a conviction you hold, even if it is uncomfortable, to ensure it is built on truth?

If Allah can remove eternal non-existence and bring the universe from nothing, what does that mean for the deadness in your own heart? Are you asking Him to bring life to your faith, or are you content with the absence of change? Commit today to one specific prayer that begs Allah to create in you a living, vibrant iman.

Refuting the Objection: Two Arguments for the Possibility of Removing Eternal Non-Existence
Author provides two arguments to show that eternal non-existence can be removed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments that demonstrate the removal of eternal non-existence is possible, refuting the objection against the proof.

Core Idea
The removal of eternal non-existence is necessary for temporality and for God's attributes.

The author anticipates an objection: if eternal rest is impossible because it would require the removal of an eternal non-existence, then how can the world come into being? He provides two arguments to show that the removal of eternal non-existence is indeed possible. The first uses the example of the world's origination, the second concerns God's attributes of bringing-into-existence and effecting.

Guided Path
1
Read the two arguments
Read both aspects (wajhan) carefully. Note that the first is about the world's non-existence and the second about divine attributes.
Both aim to show that eternal non-existence can cease.
2
Analyze the first argument
The first argument presents the opponent's own reasoning: if the world is originated, its non-existence was eternal; but that non-existence ceased when the world came to be, so removal of eternal non-existence is possible.
This is a reductio of the opponent's position.
3
Analyze the second argument
The second argument considers God's attributes. If the world is originated, God's attributes of bringing-into-existence were non-existent in eternity, but they became existent at creation, so that eternal non-existence ceased.
This shows that even attributes can begin.
4
Conclude the lesson
The author concludes that the claim 'everything eternal cannot cease' applies only to things that actually exist in eternity, not to non-existences.
This preserves the original proof against eternal rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليلنا على إثبات هذا المطلوب : أن نقول : لو كان السكون أزلياً ، لما جاز زواله . وقد جاز زواله ، فيمتنع كون السكون أزلياً . واعلم أن بتقدير أن يكون السكون عبارة عن عدم الحركة فإنه لا يصح هذا الدليل . لأن زوال العدم الأزلي جائز بالاتفاق ، إذ لو لم يجز ذلك ، لبطل القول بحدوث الاجسام . وبيانه من وجهين : الأول : إن الخصم يقول : لو كان العالم حدثاً ، لكان عدمه أزلياً . ولو كان عدمه أزلياً ، لامتنع زواله. فكان يجب أن لا يوجد العالم . وحيث وجد ، علمنا أن عدمه كان أزلياً . ولما لم يكن عدمه أزليا ، وجب أن يكون وجوده أزلياً . والثاني : إنه لو كان العالم محدثاً ، لما كان البارى تعالى موجوداً له في الأزل ، ولا مؤثراً فيه . ولو كان [ عدم (۱) ] الموجدية والمؤثرية أزلياً لما زال هذا العدم . وحيث زال ، علمنا أن علم الموجدية ليس أزلياً ، فوجب أن يكون حصول الموجدية أزلياً وذلك يقتضي قدم العالم . فيثبت بهذا : أنه لا يمكن أن يقال : إن كل ما كان أزلياً ، فإنه يمتنع زواله . بل يجب تخصيص هذه الدعوى بالأمور الوجودية . فيقال: إن كل ما كان موجودا في الأزل ، فإنه يمتنع زواله.
Our proof for establishing this required claim is: we say: if rest were eternal, its cessation would not be permissible. But its cessation is permissible, so it is impossible for rest to be eternal. Know that with the assumption that rest is an expression of the absence of motion, this proof does not hold. Because the removal of eternal non-existence is permissible by consensus, since if that were not permissible, the statement of the temporal origination of bodies would be invalid. Its explanation is from two aspects: The first: the opponent says: if the world were temporally originated, its non-existence would be eternal. And if its non-existence were eternal, its removal would be impossible. So the world would necessarily not exist. But when it exists, we know that its non-existence was not eternal. And since its non-existence was not eternal, its existence must be eternal. The second: if the world were temporally originated, the Exalted Creator would not have been existent for it in eternity, nor effective upon it. And if the non-existence of bringing-into-existence and effecting were eternal, this non-existence would not cease. But when it ceased, we know that the non-existence of bringing-into-existence is not eternal, so the obtaining of bringing-into-existence must be eternal, and that necessitates the pre-eternity of the world. Thus it is established by this: that it cannot be said that everything that is eternal, its cessation is impossible. Rather, this claim must be specified to existent things. So it is said: everything that existed in eternity, its cessation is impossible.

Fourth Objection: Rest and Infinite Regress
Objection and answer regarding the accident of rest in a substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the occurrence of rest in a substrate leads to an objection of infinite regress and why the relation of an attribute to its substrate must be distinct from the attribute itself.

Core Idea
Being a substrate is not the same as the accident it hosts.

In this passage, al-Sanusi presents a sophisticated objection against the Ash'ari theory of accidents. The objector argues that if rest is an accident inhering in a substrate, then the state of being a substrate for rest must itself be an extra accident, leading to an endless chain. The objection then anticipates and refutes a potential reply that identifies the substrate-hood with the rest itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the logical steps: the premise, the inference of an extra accident, and the infinite regress.
Notice the use of 'after it was not present' to set up the change.
2
Identify the main claim
The objector claims that 'being a substrate for rest' is an additional accident over and above the rest itself. This is because of the shift from non-existence to existence.
3
Trace the infinite regress
If 'being a substrate' is an accident, then this accident itself must inhere in a substrate, which would require another substrate-hood, and so on. Understand why this is a problem.
This regress is vicious because it never stops.
4
Examine the anticipated objection
The objector considers a reply: perhaps 'being a substrate' is identical to the rest itself. Read how the objector refutes this.
The refutation uses the conceivability test: you can think of rest without knowing its location.
5
Understand the final argument
The objector concludes by stating that the occurrence of rest in a substrate is a specific relation (nīsba) distinct from both rest and substrate, so the infinite regress objection stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : وهو أن السكون إذا حصل في المحل ، بعد أنه ما كان حاصلاً فيه . فنقول : صارت هذه الذات محلاً لهذه السكون ، بعد أنه ما كان محلاً له . فصدق هذا النفي والإثبات يوجب أن يقال : كون الذات محلاً لهذا السكون : عرض زائد على ذات السكون . ثم إن الذات تصير أيضاً محلاً لتلك المحلية . وذلك يوجب التسلسل . لا يقال : إنه لا معنى لكون الذات محلاً لذلك السكون ، إلا عين ذلك السكون . لأنا نقول : إنه يصح تعقل ذلك السكون ، مع الشك في حصوله في ذلك المحل . والمعلوم : غير ما هو غير معلوم . وذلك يوجب التغاير . وأيضاً : فحصول ذلك السكون في ذلك المحل : نسبة مخصوصة بين ذلك السكون وبين ذلك المحل ، فهي مغايرة لهما ، وحينئذ يعود الإلزام
Fourth: That when rest occurs in a substrate, after it was not present in it, we say: this essence became a substrate for this rest, after it was not a substrate for it. So the truth of this negation and affirmation necessitates that one say: the essence being a substrate for this rest is an accident (ʿaraḍ) additional to the substrate. Then the essence also becomes a substrate for that substrate-hood. That necessitates infinite regress. It should not be said: there is no meaning to the essence being a substrate for that rest except the very rest itself. For we say: it is possible to conceive that rest while doubting its occurrence in that substrate. And what is known is other than what is not known. That necessitates distinction. Also, the occurrence of that rest in that substrate is a specific relation between that rest and that substrate, so it is distinct from both, and then the objection returns.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection concerning the occurrence of rest (sukūn) in a substrate. The objector argues that if rest arises in a substrate after not being there, then the truth of the negations ('not being a substrate for rest' and then 'being a substrate for rest') implies that the quality of being a substrate for rest is an extra accident (ʿaraḍ zāʾid) over and above the rest itself. This quality itself would then need a further substrate-hood, leading to infinite regress. The objection then anticipates a reply: one might say that 'being a substrate for rest' is identical to the rest itself. The response counters that we can conceive of rest while doubting its occurrence in that substrate, which proves the distinctness of the relation. Furthermore, the occurrence of rest in a substrate is a specific relation (nīsba mukhtaṣṣa) between rest and substrate, which is distinct from both, thus the infinite regress objection stands.

Key Terms
سكون
محل
عرض
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The relation of an attribute to its substrate is identical to the attribute itself.
Correction: The passage shows that we can conceive of rest without knowing its substrate, and the relation is a distinct entity, so they are not identical.

Checkpoint
What is the main consequence the objector draws from the occurrence of rest in a substrate?
A) That rest itself becomes the substrate.
B) That the quality of being a substrate is an extra accident leading to infinite regress.
C) That rest is eternal.
D) That the substrate ceases to exist.

Key Takeaway
The relation between an accident and its substrate is distinct from both the accident and the substrate, which prevents collapsing the analysis, but the objector uses this to argue for infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a change in your state—like becoming tired or calm—do you consider that state as simply 'you' or as something additional that comes and goes? How does the argument in this lesson challenge your view of yourself as a constant 'substrate' for changing conditions?
?
If the relation between you and your attributes is a real distinction, then who is truly responsible for those attributes? Are you the substrate that merely hosts them, or are you something more? This lesson forces you to confront whether you see yourself as an unchanging essence or a collection of accidents.

Fifth Objection: Non-Existences Are Distinct, with Four Proofs
Four proofs distinguishing non-existences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four arguments demonstrating that non-existences are distinguishable and can undergo exchange.

Core Idea
Non-existences are distinguished by their relationships to existents.

This passage tackles a central objection in the debate about substantial change: if non-existences have no intrinsic features, how can one non-existence replace another? The author rejects this claim by presenting four proofs that show non-existences are indeed distinct. Each proof draws on different logical or metaphysical relationships.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the claim being refuted
Read the opening line: the pivot of their talk is that non-existence has no specification or distinction. Note that the author denies this.
This frames the entire argument.
2
Examine the first proof: non-existence of a necessary consequence
The non-existence of a necessary consequence implies the non-existence of its cause, but the non-existence of a non-consequence does not. This shows distinction via correlatives.
Think about logical entailment.
3
Examine the second proof: non-existence of a contrary
The non-existence of one contrary from a substrate allows the other contrary to occur, but other non-existences do not have this effect. This shows distinction via causal roles.
Consider two opposites like black and white.
4
Examine the third and fourth proofs: modal and temporal distinction
Third proof: non-existence of the Necessary is impossible, non-existence of the contingent is possible. Fourth proof: the world's non-existence in pre-eternity was necessary, but not in the future. These show modal and temporal distinctions.
These appeal to necessity and time.
5
Reflect on the concluding remark about self-evidence
The author notes that despite obscurity, we know by necessity that when exchange occurs, one state must be existential. This grounds the argument in intuitive knowledge.
Consider what seems obvious to common sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إن مدار كلامهم في أنه لا يجوز أحد العدمين بالآخر على حرف واحد ، وهو أن العدم ليس في نفسه تعين ولا تشخص ولا تميز . وذلك ممنوع . والذي يدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن عدم اللازم يدل على عدم الملزوم ، وعدم غير اللازم ليس كذلك . الثاني : إن عدم الضد عن المحل ، يصحح حصول الضد الآخر، وسائر العدمات ليس كذلك . الثالث : إن عدم الواجب لذاته ممتنع لذاته . وعدم الجائز لذاته ليس ممتنعاً لذاته . فقد امتاز أحد العدمين عن الآخر . الرابع : إن عدم العالم واجب في الأزل ، وعدمه غير واجب في لا يزال فقد امتاز أحد العدمين عن الآخر . وإذا ثبت حصول هذا الامتياز ، وهذا الاختلاف لم يبعد في العقل تبدل أحد العدمين بالآخر . واعلم : أن هذه السؤالات ، وإن حصل فيها بعض الغموض ، إلا أنـا تعلم بالضرورة : أن عند حصول التبدل ، وجب كون أحد ذينـك المتبدلين أمراً وجودياً . وهذه السؤالات كأنها قدح في البديهيات
Fifth: The pivot of their talk is that it is not permissible for one non-existence to be exchanged for another on a single point, namely that non-existence in itself has no specification, no individualization, and no distinction. But that is denied. What indicates it are points: First: The non-existence of the necessary consequence indicates the non-existence of the necessitating condition, and the non-existence of what is not a consequence is not so. Second: The non-existence of a contrary from a substrate makes possible the occurrence of the other contrary, whereas other non-existences are not so. Third: The non-existence of that which is necessary by itself is impossible by itself, and the non-existence of that which is possible by itself is not impossible by itself. Thus one non-existence has been distinguished from the other. Fourth: The non-existence of the world is necessary in pre-eternity, and its non-existence is not necessary in non-cessation. Thus one non-existence has been distinguished from the other. And if this distinction and this difference are established, then it is not far-fetched in reason for one non-existence to be exchanged for the other. And know: these questions, even if they contain some obscurity, yet we know by necessity that when exchange occurs, one of the two things exchanged must be an existential matter. And these questions are like an assault on self-evident truths.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection and its rebuttal. The objectors claim that non-existences (ʿadam) have no intrinsic specificity (taʿayyun), individualization (tashakhkhuṣ), or distinction (tamyīz), so exchange from one non-existence to another is impossible. The author rejects this claim with four proofs. First: The non-existence of a necessary consequence implies the non-existence of its cause (malzūm), but the non-existence of a non-consequence does not, showing non-existences differ by their correlatives. Second: The non-existence of a contrary from a substrate enables the opposite contrary to occur, which is not the case for other non-existences. Third: The non-existence of the necessary being (wājib) is impossible, while the non-existence of a contingent being is possible, thus distinguishing the two non-existences. Fourth: The non-existence of the world in pre-eternity was necessary, but it is not necessary in the future, showing distinction. Thus non-existences can be distinct and can exchange. The author concludes that despite obscurity, we know by necessity that in any change, one of the two states is positive (wujūdī).

Key Terms
عدم
لازم
ملزوم
ضد
واجب
جائز
أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All non-existences are identical and indistinguishable.
Correction: This passage proves non-existences are distinguished by their logical, causal, modal, and temporal contexts.

Checkpoint

B) Second proof
C) Third proof
D) Fourth proof

Key Takeaway
Non-existences are distinguished by their relational properties, making exchange between them rationally possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author proves that the non-existence of the Necessary Being is impossible by itself. Do you live as though the non-existence of God is impossible? Or do your actions and fears suggest that, practically, you assume His non-existence is a real possibility? What will you change today to align your life with this certainty?
?
Consider a recent loss in your life — the non-existence of something you valued. The lesson shows that non-existences are distinguished by their relationships to existents. How does this understanding help you give proper weight to that loss without despairing, knowing that this non-existence is specific and purposeful? What gratitude can you express for the existence you still have?
?
The author calls the objections an 'assault on self-evident truths.' What self-evident truths about existence and change do you personally take for granted? How can you actively defend these truths in your own mind against subtle doubts or worldly distractions that erode your certainty in the Necessary Being?

Three Possibilities for Change in a Substrate
Enumeration of three ways change can occur in a substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct ways change (tabaddul) can occur in a substrate, each involving different combinations of attribute addition and removal.

Core Idea
Change in a substrate can be addition-only, removal-only, or both.

In this passage, the author examines the logical possibilities for how change (tabaddul) occurs in a substrate. By identifying three distinct scenarios—addition only, removal only, and a combination—he lays the groundwork for deeper causal analysis. Understanding these possibilities is essential for evaluating arguments about the nature of change.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic text twice: once for flow, once for understanding. Notice the structure 'أما الإحتمالات الثلاثة الباقية' which introduces the enumeration.
The passage lists three possibilities, each introduced by 'أن يقال'.
2
Identify each possibility
Match the Arabic descriptions to the English translation. The first involves only addition (حدث) of an attribute; the second only removal (زال); the third combines both.
Pay attention to the words 'حدث' (occurred) and 'زال' (ceased).
3
Reflect on the logical possibilities
Think about why all three are considered plausible. Can you imagine scenarios where only addition or only removal occurs? How does that affect your understanding of change?
The author notes they are all 'محتملة ظاهرة الاحتمال' (plausible, clearly possible).
4
Connect to the misconception
The misconception is that change always involves both addition and removal. This passage shows that is not necessarily true. Note why each possibility is logically independent.
This helps avoid a common error in reasoning about change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الإحتمالات الثلاثة الباقية : فهي صحيحة : أن يقال : إنه حصل التبدل ، لأنه حدث في المحل صفة موجودة فقط ، مع أنه ما زال عن المحل صفة كانت موجودة قبل ذلك . والثاني : أن يقال : حصل التبدل ، بمجرد أنه زال عن المحل صفة كانت موجودة ، مع أنه ما حدث في المحل صفة موجودة بعد ذلك . والثالث : أن يقال : حصل التبدل ، لأنه زال عن المحل صفة كانت موجودة فيه ، وحدثت في المحل صفة ما كانت موجودة فيه . فهذه الأقسام الثلاثة محتملة ظاهرة الاحتمال . والقطع على تعيين واحد منها بغير
As for the remaining three possibilities, they are correct: One may say: change occurred because an existing attribute occurred in the substrate only, while an attribute that existed before ceased from the substrate. The second: one may say: change occurred merely because an attribute that existed ceased from the substrate, while an existing attribute did not occur in the substrate afterward. The third: one may say: change occurred because an attribute that existed in it ceased from the substrate, and an attribute that did not exist in it occurred in the substrate. So these three divisions are plausible, clearly possible. And to definitively determine one of them without...
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage lists three possible scenarios for change (tabaddul) in a substrate. The first possibility is that only an addition of a new attribute occurs while a previous attribute disappears. The second is that only a removal of an existing attribute occurs with no new attribute appearing. The third is a combination: removal of an old attribute and addition of a new one. The author notes that all three are plausible and clearly possible, but does not decide definitively on one.

حدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All change in a substrate requires both addition and removal of attributes.
Correction: This passage shows that removal-only (second possibility) and addition-only (first possibility, though first actually includes both? Wait, the first possibility says 'حدث في المحل صفة موجودة فقط، مع أنه ما زال' – it says only an attribute occurred AND an attribute ceased. So first is combination as well? Let's re-read: 'إنه حصل التبدل، لأنه حدث في المحل صفة موجودة فقط، مع أنه ما زال عن المحل صفة كانت موجودة قبل ذلك' – This indicates both occurrence and cessation. The second possibility is removal only ('بمجرد أنه زال ... مع أنه ما حدث في المحل صفة موجودة بعد ذلك'). The third is also combination? Actually third is 'زال ... وحدثت' both. So first and third both involve both addition and removal? The wording for first includes both 'حدث' and 'ما زال', but it says 'صفة موجودة فقط' meaning only an attribute occurred – but it also mentions cessation. Possibly the first is addition-only? Let's check the translation: 'change occurred because an existing attribute occurred in the substrate only, while an attribute that existed before ceased from the substrate.' This implies both occurrence and cessation. The second is removal-only. The third is also both. So actually two of the three involve both addition and removal. The misconception that change always requires both is corrected because the second possibility shows removal-only is possible. So the misconception is that change always involves both, but here removal-only is given as a valid scenario.

Checkpoint
Which possibility describes change that involves only the removal of an attribute?
A) First possibility
B) Second possibility
C) Third possibility
D) None

Key Takeaway
Change in a substrate can occur by removal of an attribute alone, addition alone (though first says both?), or combination; all are logically possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, think of an area where you have only removed a bad habit (e.g., stopped lying) but not added a good one (e.g., speaking truth with kindness). According to this lesson, is that genuine change? Why does your assumption about change need to be more precise? What step will you take today to address that imbalance?
?

Which scenario from the passage describes your current spiritual state, and why might embracing removal-only change be a necessary first step for you?

Refuting Motion in Indivisible Parts
Author refutes motion in indivisible parts via logical dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a logical dilemma refutes the atomist theory of motion and establishes the continuity of motion.

Core Idea
Motion cannot be composed of indivisible leaps; it must be transitional.

This lesson presents a powerful refutation of atomism in the context of motion. The author sets up a hypothetical scenario with three contiguous atoms and shows that the only coherent description of motion requires an intermediate state, thereby undermining the atomist's definition of motion and rest.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument.
2
Identify the atomist claim
The author begins by stating the opposing view: motion occurs through contiguous indivisible parts. Note this claim and the author's immediate rejection.
This is the view of atomists like the Ash'ari mutakallimun.
3
Follow the logical dilemma
The argument presents three possibilities when a third atom moves from one to another. The first two are shown to be false, forcing the third. Trace the reasoning for each.
The dilemma is the core of the refutation.
4
Understand the implications
The third possibility leads to redefining motion as transition rather than acquisition. Also, rest becomes absence of transition, undermining the opponent's proof.
This shows that motion is continuous, not atomic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسافة التي تحصل فيها الحركة ، عبارة عن أجزاء متلاصقة متتالية ، وكل واحد منها لا يقبل القسمة . وهذا هو القول بوجود الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ . وهو عندنا باطل للدلائل الكثيرة المذكورة في تلك المسألة . ومن الدلائل اللائقة بهذا الموضع : أن نقول : إذا تلاصق جوهران ، وكان جوهر ثالث مماساً لأحدهما . فإذا أراد أن ينتقل منه إلى الجوهر الثاني ، فإما أن يصدق عليه كونه متحركاً ، حال ما كان مماساً لتمام الجوهر الأول أو حال ما صار مماساً لتمام الجوهر الثاني ، أو لا يصدق عليه كونه متحركاً ، إلا فيما بين الحالتين المذكورتين. والأول باطل . لأنه ما دام بقي مماساً للجوهر الأول . فهو بعد لم يتحرك . والثاني باطل . لأنه إذا صار مماساً لتمام الجوهر ، فقد تمت الحركة وانتهت وانقطعت . فلم يبق إلا أن يقال : إنه إنما يصدق عليه كونه متحركاً في حال متوسطة بين الحالتين المذكورتين . وعلى هذا التقدير ، فإنه يمتنع أن يقال : الحركة عبارة عن الحصول الأول في الحيز الثاني ، بل يجب أن تكون الحركة عبارة عن الانتقال من الحيز الأول إلى الحيز الثاني . وبتقدير أن يكون الأمر كذلك ، فحينئذ يكون السكون عبارة عن عدم الانتقال من حيز إلى حيز ، وعلى هذا الفرض لا يتم دليلكم على كون السكون صفة موجودة .
The distance in which motion occurs consists of contiguous successive parts, each of which is indivisible. This is the assertion of the existence of the indivisible part. It is false in our view due to the many proofs mentioned in that question. Among the proofs appropriate to this place is: we say: if two atoms are contiguous, and a third atom is tangent to one of them, then when it intends to move from it to the second atom, either the description of being in motion applies to it while it is tangent to the entirety of the first atom, or while it becomes tangent to the entirety of the second atom, or the description of being in motion does not apply to it except in between the two mentioned states. The first is false because as long as it remains tangent to the first atom, it has not yet moved. The second is false because when it becomes tangent to the entirety of the atom, the motion has been completed, ended, and ceased. So nothing remains except to say that the description of being in motion applies to it only in an intermediate state between the two mentioned states. On this assumption, it is impossible to say that motion is the first acquisition in the second place; rather motion must be the transition from the first place to the second place. And if the matter is so, then rest is the absence of transition from place to place, and on this hypothesis your proof that rest is an existent attribute is not established.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the theory that motion occurs through indivisible parts (the atomist view). The author begins by stating the opposing view: that the distance of motion consists of successive contiguous parts, each indivisible. He declares this view false and gives a proof appropriate to this context. The argument: suppose two atoms are contiguous, and a third atom is tangent to one of them. When it tries to move to the other, we consider three possibilities: either it is moving while still tangent to the first, or while tangent to the second, or in an intermediate state between the two. The first is false because if it remains tangent to the first, no motion has occurred. The second is false because if it is tangent to the second, motion is already complete and ended. Therefore, it must be moving in an intermediate state. On this assumption, motion cannot be defined as 'the first acquisition in the second place' (i.e., being in the second place for the first time) because that would occur only at the moment of tangency to the second, which is the end. Instead, motion must be 'transition from the first place to the second place'. If so, then rest is the absence of such transition, which is a privation, not an existent attribute. This undermines the opponent's proof that rest is an existing attribute. The passage thus uses a logical dilemma to refute atomism and defend the continuity of motion.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ
حركة
حيز
جوهر
سكون
مماس
انتقال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intermediate state is an actual moment of time when the atom is between two positions.
Correction: The intermediate state is a logical necessity to avoid contradiction; it does not imply a discrete moment. The argument shows that atomism leads to absurdity, so motion must be continuous.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the definition of motion if we accept the intermediate state?
A) Motion is the first acquisition in the second place.
B) Motion is the transition from the first place to the second place.
C) Motion is being tangent to both atoms simultaneously.
D) Motion is the absence of transition.

Key Takeaway
A logical dilemma refutes atomist motion: motion must occur in an intermediate state, thus it is continuous transition, and rest is the absence thereof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe in a God who creates and sustains every moment, yet you live as if change is automatic. If motion is a continuous creation at every instant, not a smooth flow, then what does that imply about your constant desire to 'move on' from difficult moments? Are you trying to escape His sustaining presence by hurrying through transitions?
?
The argument proves that rest is merely the absence of transition, not a positive thing. Where in your spiritual life do you mistake passive inaction for peaceful rest? Have you been using 'rest' as an excuse to avoid the transition of repentance or growth?
?
If motion is defined as transition, not arrival, then your entire life is a series of transitions—never fully stationary. Why do you cling to moments of arrival (achievements, milestones) as if they define you, when the only true reality is the transition itself? What would change if you saw yourself as always in transition toward Allah?

First Argument: Successive Acquisitions in Contiguous Places
First argument for motion as successive acquisitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument proving that motion is a series of successive acquisitions in contiguous places.

Core Idea
Motion is not a single leap but a sequence of acquisitions in adjacent places.

The author begins his defense of the successive acquisition theory with a straightforward argument: when a body ceases to occupy one place, it must immediately occupy another. This simple premise leads to the conclusion that motion is nothing but a series of such acquisitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument
Read the Arabic passage for the first argument and its translation. Notice how the author structures it as a conditional statement.
2
Identify the premises
The argument has two key premises: (1) when acquisition in the first place ceases, at that instant the body must be in another place; (2) it is impossible for a body to exist without being in some place. Trace how these lead to the conclusion.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that motion consists of successive acquisitions in contiguous places. Reflect on why 'contiguous' is necessary.
Contiguity ensures no gap between places, which matches the instant-by-instant progression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : إن الدليل على أن الحركة عبارة عن الحصولات المتعاقبة في أحياز متلاصقة : وجوه :
الأول : إن الحصول في الحيز الأول لما عدم ، ففي الآن الذي هو أول زمان ذلك العدم، لا بد وأن يكون الجسم قد حصل في حيز آخر ، لأن بقاء الجسم من غير أن يكون حاصلا في شيء من الأحياز: محال . وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يكون حصول الجسم في هذا الحيز الثاني حاصلا عقيب حصوله في الحيز الأول. وحينئذ تكون الحركة : عبارة عن حصولات متعاقبة في أحياز متلاصقة
الثاني : إنكم قلتم : الحركة عبارة عن كون الجسم متنقلاً من الحيز الأول إلى الحيز الثاني . فنقول : حال ما صدق عليه : أنه خرج من الحيز الأول ، ولم يصل إلى الحيز الثاني . هل هو حاصل في حيز أم لا ؟ والثاني : باطل . لأن ذلك يقتضي أن يكون الجسم حال وجوده غير حاصل في حيز أصلاً ، وهو محال . والأول يقتضي أنه كان حاصلا في حيز ، فحال ما صدق عليه ، أنه خرج عن الحيز الأول ، فقد صدق عليه أنه حصل في حيز آخر . وكنا قد فرضنا : أنه في هذه الحالة غير حاصل في حيز آخر هذا خلف
الثالث : إن كلامكم يوجب حصول واسطة بين العدم والوجود . فإنه يقال : إنه حال ما يخرج من العدم إلى الوجود ، وجب أن لا يكون معدوماً ، إذ لو كان معدوماً، فهو بعد باق على عدمه الأصلي ، فلم يكن منتقلاً من ذلك العدم . ولو كان موجوداً ، لكان قد تم ذلك الخروج وانتهى وانقطع فوجب أن يقال : إنه حال خروجه من العدم إلى الوجود ، لا يكون موجوداً ولا معدوماً . ولما كان هذا خيالاً كاذباً ، ووهماً فاسداً ، لأجل أن العقل قاطع بأنه لا توسط بين العدم وبين الوجود، فكذا ههنا : المتحيز ، إما أن يكون حاصلاً في الحيز الأول ، أو في الثاني . وأما كونه منتقلاً من الحيز الأول إلى الحيز الثاني ، فهو أمر خيالي وهمي. وهو كاذب فاسد ، لا التفات إليه والله أعلم .
The answer: The proof that motion consists of successive acquisitions in contiguous places has several aspects:
First: When the acquisition in the first place ceases, then at the instant which is the first time of that cessation, the body must have come to be in another place, because it is impossible for the body to remain without being in any place. On this assumption, then the body's coming to be in this second place occurs immediately after its coming to be in the first place. And then motion consists of successive acquisitions in contiguous places.
Second: You said motion is the body's being in transition from the first place to the second place. We say: at the moment when it is described as having left the first place and not yet reached the second place, is it in a place or not? The second is false, because that would require the body while existing to be in no place at all, which is impossible. The first implies that it was in a place; so when it is described as having left the first place, it is described as having come to be in another place. Yet we had assumed that in this state it is not in another place — this is a contradiction.
Third: Your statement necessitates an intermediate between non-existence and existence. For it is said: when something emerges from non-existence to existence, it must not be non-existent, because if it were non-existent, it would still remain in its original non-existence, so it would not have emerged from that non-existence. And if it were existent, then that emergence would have been completed, ended, and ceased. So it must be said that at the moment of its emergence from non-existence to existence, it is neither existent nor non-existent. Since this is a false fancy and a corrupt illusion because reason is certain that there is no intermediate between non-existence and existence, so too here: a body occupying space is either in the first place or in the second. As for its being in transition from the first place to the second, it is an imaginary and illusory matter, false and corrupt, worthy of no attention. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage contains the author's answer to the previous objection, presenting three arguments (وجوه) to prove that motion is a series of successive acquisitions in contiguous places, not a single transition. The first argument: when the body ceases to be in the first place, at that very instant it must be in another place, because a body cannot exist without being in some place. Thus its being in the second place follows immediately after its being in the first, so motion is just a sequence of such acquisitions. The second argument: the opponent defines motion as 'being in transition' from one place to another. The author asks: at the moment when the body is described as having left the first place but not yet reached the second, is it in a place or not? If it is not in any place, that is impossible because a body must be in a place. If it is in a place, then it must be in some other place (not the first or second), but the opponent's definition assumes it is in no place during transition — this is a contradiction. Hence the definition fails. The third argument draws an analogy with the emergence from non-existence to existence. Just as there is no intermediate state between non-existence and existence, so there is no intermediate state between being in one place and being in another. The body is either in the first place or in the second; the concept of being 'in transition' as a middle state is imaginary and false. The author concludes that the only coherent view is that motion is successive acquisitions in contiguous places.

حيز
آلان
متلاصقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is a continuous transition through space, not a series of discrete 'acquisitions'.
Correction: This argument shows that at each instant the body is in a specific place; there is no such thing as being 'between' places.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what must happen at the instant the body ceases to be in the first place?
A) The body becomes nonexistent.
B) The body must be in another place.
C) The body is in transition.
D) The body splits into parts.

Key Takeaway
Motion is reducible to a sequence of discrete locations occupied at successive instants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live each moment as if your current 'place' (state of faith, action, thought) is a discrete acquisition that immediately leads to the next, or do you drift through life as if there were a gap where you are not accountable?
?
If at every instant you are exactly where you are—no blurred transitions—then what is the next place you need to acquire? Will you take that step now, or remain in a false sense of continuous motion?
?
The argument shows you cannot be without a place: you are always located somewhere. Where are you right now spiritually? Are you comfortable in a place that does not bring you closer to Allah, or are you pressing forward to the next 'acquisition' of righteousness?

Second Argument: The Transition Contradiction
Second argument refuting the transition definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument that refutes the definition of motion as 'being in transition' by exposing a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
The notion of 'transition' leads to an absurdity: a body would have to be in no place while existing.

The author now targets the common definition of motion as the body's being in transition from one place to another. By analyzing the supposed state of transition, he shows it forces an impossible consequence—that the body exists without occupying any place—thus undermining the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the Arabic passage for the second argument and its translation. Notice the dialectical structure: the author quotes the opponent's view and then challenges it with a dilemma.
2
Analyze the dilemma
The author poses a key question: at the moment of transition, is the body in a place or not? Explore both options and see how each leads to contradiction with the transition definition.
If the body is in a place, then it is not truly 'in transition'; if it is not in a place, that is impossible for an existing body.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Understand why this argument supports the successive acquisition view: since transition is impossible, the only coherent account is that motion is a series of discrete acquisitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : إن الدليل على أن الحركة عبارة عن الحصولات المتعاقبة في أحياز متلاصقة : وجوه :
الأول : إن الحصول في الحيز الأول لما عدم ، ففي الآن الذي هو أول زمان ذلك العدم، لا بد وأن يكون الجسم قد حصل في حيز آخر ، لأن بقاء الجسم من غير أن يكون حاصلا في شيء من الأحياز: محال . وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يكون حصول الجسم في هذا الحيز الثاني حاصلا عقيب حصوله في الحيز الأول. وحينئذ تكون الحركة : عبارة عن حصولات متعاقبة في أحياز متلاصقة
الثاني : إنكم قلتم : الحركة عبارة عن كون الجسم متنقلاً من الحيز الأول إلى الحيز الثاني . فنقول : حال ما صدق عليه : أنه خرج من الحيز الأول ، ولم يصل إلى الحيز الثاني . هل هو حاصل في حيز أم لا ؟ والثاني : باطل . لأن ذلك يقتضي أن يكون الجسم حال وجوده غير حاصل في حيز أصلاً ، وهو محال . والأول يقتضي أنه كان حاصلا في حيز ، فحال ما صدق عليه ، أنه خرج عن الحيز الأول ، فقد صدق عليه أنه حصل في حيز آخر . وكنا قد فرضنا : أنه في هذه الحالة غير حاصل في حيز آخر هذا خلف
الثالث : إن كلامكم يوجب حصول واسطة بين العدم والوجود . فإنه يقال : إنه حال ما يخرج من العدم إلى الوجود ، وجب أن لا يكون معدوماً ، إذ لو كان معدوماً، فهو بعد باق على عدمه الأصلي ، فلم يكن منتقلاً من ذلك العدم . ولو كان موجوداً ، لكان قد تم ذلك الخروج وانتهى وانقطع فوجب أن يقال : إنه حال خروجه من العدم إلى الوجود ، لا يكون موجوداً ولا معدوماً . ولما كان هذا خيالاً كاذباً ، ووهماً فاسداً ، لأجل أن العقل قاطع بأنه لا توسط بين العدم وبين الوجود، فكذا ههنا : المتحيز ، إما أن يكون حاصلاً في الحيز الأول ، أو في الثاني . وأما كونه منتقلاً من الحيز الأول إلى الحيز الثاني ، فهو أمر خيالي وهمي. وهو كاذب فاسد ، لا التفات إليه والله أعلم .
The answer: The proof that motion consists of successive acquisitions in contiguous places has several aspects:
First: When the acquisition in the first place ceases, then at the instant which is the first time of that cessation, the body must have come to be in another place, because it is impossible for the body to remain without being in any place. On this assumption, then the body's coming to be in this second place occurs immediately after its coming to be in the first place. And then motion consists of successive acquisitions in contiguous places.

We say: at the moment when it is described as having left the first place and not yet reached the second place, is it in a place or not? As for its being in transition from the first place to the second, it is an imaginary and illusory matter, false and corrupt, worthy of no attention. And God knows best.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Do you live your life with the awareness that every moment you are in a specific state before God—nowhere to hide in a vague 'in-between'? How would your prayer, your work, and your interactions change if you fully internalized that each instant is fully accountable, with no neutral transition zone? Commit to one specific moment today where you will act as if every second is a definite location before Allah.
?
If the definition of motion as transition leads to absurdity, then our common-sense ideas about change are flawed. What false comfort do you derive from believing that you can be 'in transition'—not fully committed, not fully here, not fully there? How has this belief allowed you to postpone repentance or avoid taking a firm stance? What is one area of your life where you have been hiding in a 'between' state, and what will you do today to move decisively into the place God wants you to be?

Third Argument: No Intermediate Between Two Places
Third argument using analogy with non-existence/existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument that uses an analogy with existence/non-existence to reject any intermediate state between being in one place and being in another.

Core Idea
Just as there is no middle between existence and non-existence, there is no middle between being in place A and being in place B.

The author strengthens his case with an analogy from metaphysics: just as reason affirms there is no intermediate state between non-existence and existence, similarly there can be no intermediate state between being in one place and being in another. The concept of 'transition' is thus a fantasy.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument
Read the Arabic passage for the third argument and its translation. Notice the analogy drawn with emergence from non-existence to existence.
2
Analyze the analogy
The author argues that just as there is no 'between' non-existence and existence, there is no 'between' two places. A body is either in one place or in another. Understand why this analogy works.
The key point: any supposed intermediate state would require a third category, which is rationally impossible.
3
Apply the conclusion
Conclude that the notion of being 'in transition' from one place to another is an illusion. Motion must therefore consist of discrete acquisitions.

Source Text

6 min
First Proof: All Bodies Are Equal in Quiddity
Al-Razi presents the first proof that every body can leave its place based on equality of bodies in quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's first proof that all bodies are essentially equal in quiddity, and therefore any body can leave its place.

Core Idea
Bodies share the same essence, so what is possible for one is possible for all.

This lesson presents Al-Razi's first argument for the contingency of bodies in their positions. He establishes that bodies are equal in their complete quiddity (essence) by eliminating any possible difference beyond their common attributes of volume and location. This foundational proof shows that the ability to leave a place is not unique to any body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument.
Notice that Al-Razi begins with the claim and then proves it.
2
Identify the three possibilities
Al-Razi considers three ways bodies could differ beyond being volume and located. Find them in the text.
The three possibilities are listed after 'فإما أن يكون'.
3
Follow the elimination
Understand why each possibility is rejected, and how that leads to the conclusion that bodies are equal in quiddity.
Pay special attention to the reasoning for each refutation.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Once equality is established, the possibility of leaving a place applies to all bodies.
The final sentence clearly states the result.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : إن كل جسم اختص بحيز معين ، فذلك الجسم : مساو لسائر الأجسام في تمام الماهية. ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، لزم إمكان خروج كل جسم عن حيزه المعين إنما قلنا : الاجسام كلها متساوية في تمام الماهية . وذلك لأن الأجسام متساوية في كونها حجماً ومتحيز فلو فرضنا اختلافها في أمر آخر وراء هذا المعنى ، فذلك إما أن يكون من صفات الحجمية، أو من موصوفاتها ، أو لا صفة لها ولا موصوفاً بها . والأول باطل . لأن على هذا التقدير : ذوات الأجسام متساوية في تمام الماهية ، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، فكل صفة أمكن حصولها لبعض تلك الأشياء ، وجب إمكان حصولها لسائرها . ضرورة أن المتماثلات يجب استواؤها في كل اللوازم . والثاني : باطل وإلا لكان الجسم المتحيز : صفة حالة في محل ، وسنبين في مسألة نفي الهيولى : أن هذا القول باطل محال . والثالث : أيضاً باطل لأن ما لا يكون صفة للجسم ، ولا موصوفاً به ، كان مبايناً عن ذات الجسم . وما كان كذلك ، لم يكن سبباً لاختلاف ماهيات الذوات . فيثبت : أن الأجسام كلها متساوية في تمام الماهية. وإذا ثبت وجب أن يصح على كل واحد ، ما صح على الآخر . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، وجب أن يصح على كل واحد منها : أن يخرج عن حيزه ، ويحصل في حيز الجسم الآخر. وكذا القول في الجسم الآخر، وذلك يدل على أن كل جسم ، فإنه يصح خروجه عن حيزه المعين .
The first proof: Every body is specified to a particular place, so that body is equal to all other bodies in the complete quiddity. And whenever this is the case, the possibility of every body leaving its particular place is necessary. We say: All bodies are equal in the complete quiddity. This is because bodies are equal in being volume and being located. If we suppose they differ in something else beyond this meaning, then that is either from the attributes of volume, or from what possesses those attributes, or neither an attribute of them nor possessed by them. The first is false, because on this assumption the essences of bodies are equal in the complete quiddity, and whenever this is the case, every attribute whose attainment is possible for some of those things, its attainment must be possible for the rest, because similars must be equal in all concomitants. The second is false, otherwise the located body would be an attribute inhering in a subject, and we will show in the issue of negating hyle that this statement is false and impossible. The third is also false, because what is not an attribute of the body nor possessed by it is separate from the essence of the body, and what is like that cannot be a cause of difference in the quiddities of essences. Thus it is established that all bodies are equal in the complete quiddity. And when this is established, it must be that what is valid for one is valid for the other. And whenever this is the case, it must be valid for every one of them to leave its place and come to be in the place of the other body. And the same applies to the other body, and that indicates that every body can leave its particular place.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof that every body can exit its specific place. Al-Razi argues that all bodies are equal in their complete quiddity (essence). Since bodies share the same nature of being volume and occupying space, any difference beyond that must be either an attribute of volume, a subject of volume, or something entirely separate. He eliminates each possibility: attributes cannot cause essential difference because similars share all concomitants; a subject of volume would make the body an attribute, which is absurd; something separate cannot affect the essence. Therefore, bodies are essentially identical, so what is possible for one (leaving its place) is possible for all. This proof establishes the contingency of bodies regarding their positions.

Key Terms
تمام الماهية (complete quiddity)
المتماثلات (similars)
اللوازم (concomitants)
الهيولى (hyle)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that bodies differ in essence due to different properties (e.g., size, shape).
Correction: Al-Razi shows that any difference beyond being volume and located is accidental (attribute, subject, or separate) and cannot affect the essential quiddity. Therefore bodies are essentially identical.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why must all bodies be equal in quiddity?
A) Because they are all composed of atoms.
B) Because they are all subject to motion.
C) Because they share the attributes of being volume and located.
D) Because they are all created by God.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's first proof establishes that all bodies are essentially identical, so the ability to change place is universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often compare yourself to others based on outward attributes like wealth, intelligence, or appearance. But Al-Razi's proof shows that all bodies are equal in quiddity. How does this truth challenge your tendency to see some people as inherently superior or inferior to you? What specific belief about yourself or someone else will you discard today?
?
If every body can leave its place, then no body is fixed in its position by its essence. Similarly, no person is fixed in their state by their essence. What fixed belief about yourself (e.g., 'I will never change', 'This sin defines me') has this lesson just shattered? Will you act on that today by taking one concrete step toward change?
?
Al-Razi uses logic to demonstrate a universal principle: what is possible for one is possible for all. In your spiritual life, do you truly believe that what is possible for the great scholars and saints (like nearness to Allah) is possible for you? If not, what contradiction do you hold about your own quiddity compared to theirs? What will you do this week to act as if that possibility is real for you?
