Second Proof: All Places Are Equal
Al-Razi presents the second proof for universal motion based on equality of places.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's second proof that all bodies are capable of motion because all places (extended voids) are essentially identical.

Core Idea
All places are essentially the same extended void; differences are accidental.

Al-Razi builds on the concept of space as an extended void. He argues that since all places share the same essential nature, any difference between them must be accidental. This leads to the conclusion that any body can occupy any place, making motion universally possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the overall argument before analyzing details.
Notice that Al-Razi uses a structured trilemma to refute possible differences.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the statement that all places (ahyaaz) are equal in complete quiddity and understand why this implies universal motion.
3
Follow the trilemma
Al-Razi eliminates three possibilities for how places could differ: (1) difference inheres in space, (2) space inheres in a subject, (3) there is no relation at all. He shows each is false, concluding all parts of space are equal.
This logical elimination is key to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : الأحياز بأسرها متساوية في تمام الماهية ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، كانت الأجسام بأسرها قابلة للحركة. بيان الأول : أن الفضاء والجهة والحيز : لا معنى له إلا وهذا الخلاء الممتد . وهذا المفهوم قدر مشترك بين جميع الأحياز. فلو فرضنا اختلافها ، لكان الأمر الذي به حصل ذلك الاختلاف ، مغايراً لهذا المفهوم ، الذي به حصل الاشتراك . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : ذلك الأمر الذي به حصلت هذه المخالفة، إما أن يكون حالاً في هذا الفضاء ، أو محلاً له ، أو لا حال فيه ، ولا محلاً له. والأول باطل . لأن أجزاء الفضاء لما كانت في أنفسها متساوية ، فكل ما صح حلوله في أحد تلك الجوانب ، صح حلوله في الجانب الآخر منه . والثاني أيضاً باطل . وإلا لكان هذا الفضاء حاصلاً في مادة ، وكل بعد حال في مادة ، فهو جسم عند من يثبت للجسم مادة . فأما عند من ينكر هذه المادة ، فالقول بحلول هذا البعد في المادة : محال . والثالث أيضاً باطل لأن ما لا يكون حالا في شيء ، ولا محلاً له ، امتنع أن يصير سبباً لاختلاف أحوال ذلك الشيء . فيثبت : أن الأجزاء المفترضة في هذا الخلاء ، وفي هذا الفضاء متساوية . فوجب أن يصح على كل واحد منها ، ما صح على الآخر. فكما صح على هذا الحيز ، أن يحصل فيه هذا الجوهر ، فكذلك وجب أن يصح عليه : أن يحصل فيه الجوهر الآخر . ومتى ثبت هذا ، لزم صحة الحركة على كل الأجسام
The second proof: All places are equal in the complete quiddity, and whenever this is the case, all bodies are capable of motion. Explanation of the first: Space, direction, and place have no meaning except this extended void. And this concept is a common factor among all places. If we suppose them to differ, then the thing by which that difference occurs would be distinct from this concept by which the commonality occurs. And when this is established, we say: That thing by which this difference occurs is either inhering in this space, or a subject for it, or neither inhering in it nor a subject for it. The first is false, because since the parts of space are equal in themselves, whatever is valid to inhere in one of those aspects is valid to inhere in the other aspect. The second is also false, otherwise this space would exist in a matter, and every dimension inhering in matter is a body according to those who affirm matter for the body. As for those who deny this matter, the statement that this dimension inheres in matter is impossible. The third is also false, because what is not inhering in something nor a subject for it cannot become a cause of difference in the states of that thing. Thus it is established that the parts assumed in this void and in this space are equal. So it must be valid for each one what is valid for the other. Just as it is valid for this place that this substance occurs in it, so too it must be valid for it that the other substance occurs in it. And when this is established, the validity of motion for all bodies follows.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the possibility of bodies moving from their places. Al-Razi argues that all places (ahyaaz) are equal in their complete quiddity, meaning they share the same essence of being an extended void. Any supposed difference between places would have to be due to something extra: either an attribute inhering in the void, a subject supporting the void, or something entirely separate. He refutes each: if it inheres, the parity of void parts means what inheres in one can inhere in another; if it is a subject, that would make the void a material dimension, which is either a body (if matter exists) or impossible (if matter is denied); if separate, it cannot affect the void. Therefore, all places are essentially identical, so any substance can occupy any place. This implies motion is possible for all bodies.

Key Terms
الأحياز
الخلاء الممتد
الفضاء
الجوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Places differ inherently (e.g., up and down are different in essence).
Correction: Al-Razi argues that any perceived difference is accidental; all places are essentially the same extended void.

Checkpoint
What is the common factor among all places according to Al-Razi?
A) They are all made of atoms.
B) They are all extended voids.
C) They are all occupied by bodies.
D) They are all created by God.

Key Takeaway
All places are essentially equal, so motion is possible for all bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you find yourself attaching spiritual significance to certain places (like a particular mosque or room) as if they are inherently more blessed? Al-Razi’s proof says all places are equal in essence. If this is true, how should it change your devotion to physical locations versus your intention and action? Will you actively remind yourself of this when you feel an attachment to a place?
?
Al-Razi uses pure logic to overturn a common assumption about the world. How often do you critically examine your own assumptions about faith and the physical world? Are you willing to let reason challenge your habits? This lesson demands that you not take things for granted. What is one assumption about the world that you hold that might be false upon reflection?
?
If all places are equal, then no place is inherently closer to God. Does this thought disturb you or liberate you? Your answer reveals whether you rely on external forms or internal sincerity. How will you change your practice to reflect that it is not the place but your heart that matters?

First Proof: Equality of Bodies Implies Possibility of Motion
Razi presents a deductive argument for motion based on essential equality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the equality of bodies in their essence to argue for the possibility of motion.

Core Idea
Essential equality of bodies implies equal possibilities, including motion.

Razi begins his proof for the possibility of motion with a simple but powerful premise: all bodies share the same essential quiddity. From this premise, he deduces that what is possible for one body must be possible for any other. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and shows how it leads to the conclusion that every body can leave its specific space.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the logical flow: premise, inference, conclusion.
Pay attention to the term 'quiddity' (māhiyya).
2
Identify the Premise and Conclusion
Identify the main premise (all bodies equal in complete quiddity) and the conclusion (each body can leave its space). Trace the logical inference.
The inference relies on the principle of equality: what holds for one must hold for another.
3
Consider the Misconception
Understand that the argument only proves possibility, not actual movement. Reflect on why this distinction matters in kalām debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا ماهيات الذوات . فيثبت : أن الأجسام كلها متساوية في تمام الماهية. وإذا ثبت وجب أن يصح على كل واحد ، ما صح على الآخر . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، وجب أن يصح على كل واحد منها : أن يخرج عن حيزه ، ويحصل في حيز الجسم الآخر. وكذا القول في الجسم الآخر، وذلك يدل على أن كل جسم ، فإنه يصح الخروج عن حيزه المعين .
This [concerns] the quiddities of essences. Thus it is established that all bodies are equal in complete quiddity. If this is established, it must be that whatever is valid for one is valid for the other. When this is the case, it must be valid for each one of them to leave its space and come to be in the space of the other body. The same applies to the other body, and that indicates that every body can leave its specific space.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the possibility of movement of bodies. It is a deductive argument based on the principle of equality of bodies in their essential quiddity. The author begins by stating that all bodies share an identical quiddity (māhiyya) in their entirety. Once this equality is granted, it follows logically that any property or state that applies to one body must also apply to another. In particular, if one body can occupy a given space, another must also be able to occupy that same space. Therefore, each body must be capable of leaving its own specific space (ḥayz) and moving into the space of another. The argument is straightforward: complete essential equality entails full symmetry of possibilities, including the possibility of motion.

Key Terms
ماهية (quiddity/essence)
جسم (body)
حيز (space)
الخروج عن الحيز (leaving the space)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that bodies actually move.
Correction: The argument only proves the possibility of movement, not that movement actually occurs.

Checkpoint
What is the logical consequence of all bodies being equal in complete quiddity?
A) They must all be composed of the same matter.
B) They must each be able to leave their own space and enter another's.
C) They must all be in motion at all times.
D) They must all be simple bodies.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first proof for motion rests entirely on the principle that bodies equal in essence must share the same possibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If all bodies are equal in essence, and therefore motion is possible for every one of them, what does that say about your own potential to change—to leave your current 'space'—whether physical, spiritual, or moral? Are you living as if that possibility is real, or are you denying it?
?
The proof establishes possibility, not actuality. Where in your life have you mistaken possibility for actuality—thinking that because something is possible, it is already realized? What step must you take today to turn that possibility into actual movement toward Allah?
?
This argument rests on the idea that what is valid for one is valid for another. Consider someone you look down upon or think is fundamentally different from you. Does this lesson challenge that assumption? In what specific way are you failing to treat them as equal in their essential humanity?

Second Proof for Movement: Equality of Spaces and the Objection of Up and Down
Razi presents the second proof for movement based on equality of all spaces, then answers an objection about up and down.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof for the possibility of movement based on the essential equality of all spaces, and how he defends it against the objection that spaces differ by being above or below.

Core Idea
If all spaces are identical, any body can move to any space — movement is universally possible.

Razi now presents a more refined argument for movement, focusing on the nature of space itself. He argues that all spaces are essentially the same, so no space is intrinsically tied to a particular body. But a natural objection arises: don't we experience spaces as different — some above, some below? Razi's answer reveals the relativity of direction and the uniformity of absolute space. This lesson walks through the entire argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the author's reasoning flow without analysis.
Notice the technical terms: حيز, خلاء, جوهر.
2
Identify the two premises of the proof
The proof has two premises: (1) all spaces are equal in quiddity, and (2) if spaces are equal, then all bodies are capable of movement. Locate where each premise is stated.
3
Follow the trilemma analysis
Razi examines three possibilities for what could differentiate spaces if they were not equal. See how he refutes each possibility, concluding that spaces are indeed equal.
The three options are: inhering in space, being a substrate for space, or neither.
4
Consider the objection and refutation
The objection claims spaces differ by being above or below. Razi's refutation shows that above/below are relative to our world, while absolute space beyond the world is uniform.
This refutation is crucial for defending the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : الأحياز بأسرها متساوية في تمام الماهية ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، كانت الأجسام بأسرها قابلة للحركة. بيان الأول : أن الفضاء والجهة والحيز : لا معنى له إلا وهذا الخلاء الممتد . وهذا المفهوم قدر مشترك بين جميع الأحياز. فلو فرضنا اختلافها ، لكان الأمر الذي به حصل ذلك الاختلاف ، مغايراً لهذا المفهوم ، الذي به حصل الاشتراك . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : ذلك الأمر الذي به حصلت هذه المخالفة، إما أن يكون حالاً في هذا الفضاء ، أو محلاً له ، أو لا حال فيه ، ولا محلاً له. والأول باطل . لأن أجزاء الفضاء لما كانت في أنفسها متساوية ، فكل ما صح حلوله في أحد تلك الجوانب ، صح حلوله في الجانب الآخر منه . والثاني أيضاً باطل . وإلا لكان هذا الفضاء حاصلاً في مادة ، وكل بعد حال في مادة ، فهو جسم عند من يثبت للجسم مادة . فأما عند من ينكر هذه المادة ، فالقول بحلول هذا البعد في المادة : محال . والثالث أيضاً باطل لأن ما لا يكون حالا في شيء ، ولا محلاً له ، امتنع أن يصير سبباً لاختلاف أحوال ذلك الشيء . فيثبت : أن الأجزاء المفترضة في هذا الخلاء ، وفي هذا الفضاء متساوية . فوجب أن يصح على كل واحد منها ، ما صح على الآخر. فكما صح على هذا الحيز ، أن يحصل فيه هذا الجوهر ، فكذلك ان وجب يصح عليه : أن يحصل فيه الجوهر الآخر . ومتى ثبت هذا ، لزم صحة الحركة على كل الأجسام. فإن قالوا : أليس أن الأحياز مختلفة ، لأجل أن بعضها فوقنا ، وبعضها تحتنا . فإذا حصل هذا النوع من الاختلاف، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : بعض الأجسام يجب حصولها في الأحياز الفوقانية ، والبعض الآخر يجب حصولها في الأحياز التحتانية ؟ فنقول : هذا في غاية البعد . وذلك لأنا قد دللنا على أن الفضاء خارج العالم ، لا نهاية له . فلما حصل هذا العالم صار بعض الأحياز بالنسبة إلى الواقفين على وجه الأرض فوقاً وبعضها تحتاً . فأما خارج العالم فليس هناك فوق وتحت بل ليس هناك إلا الفضاء المتشابه الآخر في تمام الطبيعة والماهية . وذلك هو المطلوب
The second proof: All spaces are equal in complete quiddity, and when this is the case, all bodies are capable of movement. Explanation of the first [premise]: The terms 'space', 'direction', and 'place' have no meaning other than this extended void. This concept is a common factor among all spaces. If we suppose they differ, then the thing by which that difference occurs would be distinct from this concept by which commonality occurs. When this is established, we say: that thing by which this difference occurs is either an accident inhering in this space, or a substrate for it, or neither inhering nor a substrate. The first is false because the parts of space are in themselves equal, so whatever inheres validly in one side validly inheres in the other side. The second is also false because then this space would occur in a matter, and every dimension inhering in matter is a body according to those who affirm matter for body; but for those who deny matter, the claim that this dimension inheres in matter is impossible. The third is also false because what is neither inhering in something nor a substrate for it cannot become a cause for difference in the states of that thing. Thus it is established that the parts assumed in this void and in this space are equal. Therefore, whatever is valid for one must be valid for the other. Just as it is valid for this space that this substance occurs in it, so also it must be valid that the other substance occurs in it. When this is established, the possibility of movement is necessary for all bodies. If they say: 'Are not spaces different because some are above us and some below us? When this kind of difference exists, why may it not be said that some bodies must occur in upper spaces and others in lower spaces?' We say: This is extremely remote. Because we have already demonstrated that the space outside the world is infinite. Thus when this world came to be, some spaces became 'above' and some 'below' relative to those standing on the surface of the earth. But outside the world, there is neither above nor below; there is only the other similar space in complete nature and quiddity. That is what is sought.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the possibility of movement, followed by an objection and its refutation. The proof argues that all spaces (aḥyāz) are identical in their essential quiddity. Since the concepts of space, direction, and place refer to the same extended void, any alleged difference between them must arise from an additional factor. The author examines three possibilities: that the differentiating factor inheres in space, that it serves as a substrate for space, or that it is neither. He shows each possibility leads to a contradiction, establishing that all parts of space are essentially equal. Consequently, any substance that can occupy one space can occupy any other, making movement possible for all bodies. The objection claims that spaces differ by being above or below, which would impose specific locations. The answer explains that 'above' and 'below' are relative to the world's inhabitants; beyond the world, space is uniform. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
حيز (space)
خلاء (void)
جوهر (substance)
فوق (above)
تحت (below)
جهة (direction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spaces are intrinsically different because some are above and some below, so certain bodies are naturally confined to upper or lower regions.
Correction: Above and below are relative to the observer's position on earth; absolute space beyond the world is uniform, so no space has an inherent up or down.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the objection that spaces differ because some are above and some below?
A) Above and below are essential properties of space.
B) The world's creation made some spaces above and below, but outside the world space is uniform.
C) The objection is correct, so movement is not universally possible.
D) Above and below are illusions with no basis in reality.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of movement follows from the essential uniformity of space; apparent differences in direction are relative to our position and do not affect space's intrinsic nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

But if all spaces are essentially equal, what makes you treat some positions as inherently better than others? Is your sense of importance merely relative to your own vantage point, like 'above' and 'below' on Earth? How might this truth shake the foundation of your worldly ambitions? Commit to one area where you will stop chasing relative status and instead seek what is absolutely true.
?
Razi argues that whatever holds for one space holds for all spaces. Do you apply the same logic to your own character – that what is valid for one situation should be valid for all? Or do you make excuses for yourself based on circumstance? If the possibility of movement is universal, so is the possibility of change. Where have you convinced yourself that you cannot move, when in reality the space is uniform and the obstacle is only in your mind? What step will you take today to prove that movement is possible for you?
?
The objection about up and down is rooted in an illusion of perspective. Think about your own worldview: what beliefs do you hold simply because you were born in a certain 'place' – a certain culture, family, or time? Would you still hold them if you stood outside the world, looking from that uniform space? Are you ready to accept that what seems 'obvious' is merely relative? Third Proof for Movement: Symmetry of Simple Parts
Razi presents the third proof for the possibility of movement based on the symmetry of simple parts of composite bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third proof for the possibility of movement, which uses the essential symmetry of simple parts to argue that all bodies are capable of movement.

Core Idea
Simple parts have identical sides; thus any contact change requires movement.

Razi's third proof tackles the possibility of movement from a different angle: the composition of bodies. By analyzing the implications of simple parts having symmetrical sides, he builds a logical necessity for movement. This proof is more subtle than the previous ones, relying on the concept of essential equality and a reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Try to follow the logical flow without getting bogged down by terminology.
The argument is tightly reasoned; take your time.
2
Understand the body division
Razi begins by dividing bodies into simple and composite. Note that composites decompose into simples. This sets the stage for the argument.
A simple body is indivisible in essence.
3
Follow the symmetry argument
Each simple part must have two sides (right and left) that are identical in quiddity. If they were different, the part would be composite, contradiction.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Apply equality of concomitants
Since the sides are essentially equal, any property valid for one side must be valid for the other. Specifically, if the right side can be touched, the left can also be touched.
Change in contact requires movement of the part.
5
Conclude the possibility of movement
Since such a change is possible, movement of the part is possible. By extension, all bodies are capable of movement.
This proves logical possibility, not actual occurrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة على صحة الحركة على جميع الأجسام : أن نقول : الجسم إما أن يكون بسيطاً أو مركباً . فإن كان مركباً فكل مركب لا بد وأن ينتهي تحليل تركيبه إلى البسائط ، فوجب أن يكون ذلك المركب مركباً من الأشياء ، كل واحد منها في نفسه يكون بسيطاً ، وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : كل واحد من تلك البسائط، فإنه يجب أن يحصل له جانبان : أحدهما : يمينه . والآخر يساره . وطبيعة يمينه : لا بد وأن تكون مساوية الطبيعة يساره في تمام الماهية. وإلا لكان أحد هذين الجانبين مخالفاً للجانب الآخر في تمام الماهية. وحينئذ يكون ذلك الجسم مركباً، مع أنا فرضناه بسيطاً. هذا خلف . وإذا ثبت استواء جانبي ذلك الجزء في تمام الماهية ، فكل ما يصح على أحد ذينك الجانبين ، وجب أن يصح على الجانب الآخر . ضرورة أن المتساويات في تمام الماهية ، يجب استواؤها في جميع اللوازم وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : الجسم الذي هو ممسوس يمينه، وجب أن يصح كونه ممسوساً يساره، وبالعكس. ولا يمكن حصول هذا المعنى ، إلا بحركة تلك الأجزاء . فلما كان التبدل في المماسة ممكناً ، ولا يحصل ذلك التبدل إلا عند حصول حركة الأجزاء ، وجب القول بأن تلك الحركة أيضاً : ممكنة وهو المطلوب
The third proof for the validity of movement upon all bodies: We say: The body is either simple or composite. If it is composite, every composite must eventually, in the analysis of its composition, terminate in simples. So that composite must be composed of things, each of which in itself is simple. When this is established, we say: Each one of those simples must have two sides: one is its right, the other its left. The nature of its right must be equal to the nature of its left in complete quiddity. Otherwise, one of these two sides would be different from the other in complete quiddity, and then that body would be composite, despite our having assumed it simple. This is a contradiction. When the equality of the two sides of that part in complete quiddity is established, then whatever is valid for one of those two sides must be valid for the other, by necessity, because things equal in complete quiddity must be equal in all concomitants. When this is established, we say: The body whose right side is touched must validly be capable of having its left side touched, and vice versa. This meaning cannot occur except through the movement of those parts. Since change in contact is possible, and that change does not occur except when movement of the parts occurs, it must be said that that movement is also possible. That is what is sought.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the possibility of movement of all bodies. It is an argument that focuses on the composition of bodies and the symmetry of simple parts. The author begins by dividing bodies into simple and composite. Composite bodies decompose into simples. Each simple part must have two sides (e.g., right and left) that are identical in essence; otherwise the part would be composite, contradicting its simplicity. Since the sides are essentially equal, any property of one side must apply to the other. In particular, if the right side can be touched, the left side must also be touchable. This change in contact requires movement of the part. Since such a change is possible, movement of that part is also possible, and by extension, all bodies are capable of movement. The proof is deductive and relies on the principle that essential equality entails equality in logical concomitants.

Key Terms
بسيط (simple)
مركب (composite)
يمين (right side)
يسار (left side)
مماسة (contact)
لوازم (concomitants)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof demonstrates that bodies actually move.
Correction: The proof only establishes the logical possibility of movement, not its actual occurrence.

Checkpoint
Why must the two sides of a simple body be equal in complete quiddity?
A) Because they were created simultaneously.
B) Otherwise the body would be composite, contradicting the assumption of simplicity.
C) Because all bodies are made of the same matter.
D) Because movement requires equality.

Key Takeaway
Razi's third proof shows that movement is logically necessary given the essential symmetry of simple parts and the principle of equality of concomitants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If even the simplest parts of creation have the logical possibility of movement, what does that say about your own capacity for spiritual change? Are you convinced that change in your heart is possible, or do you act as if it is impossible? What one specific movement—a change in habit, attitude, or relationship with Allah—can you initiate today, trusting that it is not beyond possibility?
?
Razi argues that the two sides of a simple part are equal in quiddity, so what applies to one applies to the other. Reflect on your own soul: do you treat different aspects of your faith (prayer, charity, patience) as equally within your capacity, or do you allow some to be neglected? Which 'side' of your deen have you left untouched, and how can you move towards it starting now?
?
The proof shows that change in contact (engagement with the world) requires movement. You are constantly 'in contact' with people, desires, and duties. Are you moving deliberately toward righteousness, or are you passively accepting whatever contacts you? What one contact today—a conversation, a task, a moment of solitude—can you transform into a movement of obedience to Allah?

Refutation That Tark Means Absence of Action
Refutes that tark is power over non‑existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why refraining (tark) cannot be a power over non-existence and how the author employs a dilemma to refute the opponent's position.

Core Idea
Refraining is not power over nothing; it is an act of doing the opposite.

This passage addresses a critical objection: if God's pre‑eternal power is power over refraining from creating the world, then refraining must be more than mere absence of action. The author presents two forceful proofs that tark cannot be non‑existence, then constructs a dilemma that collapses the opponent's argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
Recognize that the opponent interprets God's pre‑eternal power as power over refraining (tark), which they equate with power over non‑existence.
The term 'original continuous non‑existence' refers to the state before creation.
2
Examine the two reasons
Read the two reasons why tark cannot be an expression of non‑existence. First: non‑existence is pure negation, while power is an effective attribute. Second: original non‑existence remains, and power cannot affect what already exists (taḥṣīl al‑ḥāṣil is impossible).
These reasons show that power cannot be effective in nothing or in what already persists.
3
Follow the dilemma
Once tark is defined as the act of doing the opposite, the author derives a dilemma: God in pre‑eternity either creates the world or creates its opposite. Both lead to consequences the opponent rejects, thus invalidating their position.
The dilemma forces the opponent to accept either the eternity of the world or the possibility of non‑existence for the Eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدوراً له . فنقول : هذا الترك لا يجوز أن يكون عبارة عن العلم الأصلي المستمر ، لوجهين : الأول : إن العدم نفي محض، والقدرة صفة مؤثرة ، فامتنع كون القدرة مؤثرة في العدم الثاني : إن العدم الأصلي باق. والقدرة لا تأثير لها في الباقي . لما ثبت أن تحصيل الحاصل محال . فيثبت : أن الترك يمتنع أن يكون عبارة عن عدم الفعل، فوجب أن يكون عبارة عن فعل ضد الشيء . وإذا ثبت أنه تعالى قادراً في الأزل . فهو في الأزل، إما أن يكون فاعلاً للعالم ، وإما أن يكون تاركاً له . فإن كان الأول ، لزم قدم العالم ، وإن كان الثاني لزم قدم ضد العالم . وبتقدير أن يكون الأمر كذلك ، فإنه يمتنع دخول العالم في الوجود إلا عند زوال ذلك الضد الأزلي ، فيكون هذا قولاً بجواز العدم على القديم . وهو يبطل كلامكم .
We say: This 'refraining' (al-tark) cannot be an expression of the original continuous non‑existence, for two reasons: First: Non‑existence is pure negation, while power (al-qudra) is an effective attribute, so it is impossible for power to be effective in non‑existence. Second: The original non‑existence remains, and power has no effect on what remains, because it has been established that the acquisition of what is already obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) is impossible. Thus it is established that refraining cannot be an expression of the absence of an act; so it must be an expression of the act of doing the opposite of the thing. And when it is established that He, exalted, is powerful in pre‑eternity (al-azal), then in pre‑eternity He is either an agent of the world or one who refrains from it. If the first, then the eternity of the world follows; if the second, then the eternity of the opposite of the world follows. And assuming that this is the case, it is impossible for the world to come into existence except when that pre‑eternal opposite disappears, and this amounts to saying that non‑existence is permissible for the Eternal (al-qadīm). And that invalidates your argument.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. The opponent claims that God's power in pre‑eternity is a power over refraining (tark), interpreted as a power over non‑existence. The author gives two reasons why tark cannot be an expression of original continuous non‑existence. First: Non‑existence is pure negation, while power is an effective attribute, so power cannot be effective in something purely negative. Second: Original non‑existence is permanent and power has no effect on what already exists, because 'acquiring what is already obtained' is impossible. Therefore, tark must not be the absence of an act but rather an act of doing the opposite of the thing (i.e., creating the opposite of the world). From this, the author draws a dilemma: God in pre‑eternity is either an agent (fāʿil) of the world or a refraining one (tārik). If He is an agent, the world would be eternal (qadīm); if He is refraining, then the opposite of the world (e.g., another universe or nothingness) is eternal, and the world can only appear when that opposite ceases, implying non‑existence for the Eternal, which the opponent rejects. Thus the opponent's position is invalidated.

Key Terms
الترك (tark)
القدرة (al‑qudra)
العدم (al‑ʿadam)
القديم (al‑qadīm)
تحصيل الحاصل (taḥṣīl al‑ḥāṣil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That refraining (tark) is a power over non‑existence.
Correction: The passage shows that tark cannot be power over non‑existence because non‑existence is pure negation and power cannot affect what already persists; rather, tark is an act of doing the opposite.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for power (qudra) to be effective in non‑existence (ʿadam)?
A) Because non‑existence is an attribute of God
B) Because non‑existence is pure negation and power is an effective attribute
C) Because power only applies to what is possible
D) Because non‑existence is eternal

Key Takeaway
Refraining is not a passive absence but an active opposite, and claiming that God's pre‑eternal power is over refraining leads to unacceptable consequences that invalidate the opponent's position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In what area of your life do you pretend that your inaction is not a choice, while in reality you are actively choosing its opposite? What is that opposite, and what does it say about your heart?
?
If God's refraining is an active opposite, what does that mean for your own responsibility when you neglect an obligation? Are you merely 'not doing' it, or are you actively engaged in its opposite? How does this change your repentance?
?
When you claim 'I just can't stop sinning,' are you distorting your power to choose? What might be the 'opposite act' you are actually performing, and what concrete step can you take today to replace it with the real good deed?

The Seventh Objection: Parity of Eternal Non-Existence and Existence
An objection applying the same proof for the eternity of existence to the eternity of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the seventh objection turns the opponent's own proof against them by applying the same logic to non-existence, revealing an inconsistency.

Core Idea
If eternal existence cannot cease, then eternal non-existence also cannot cease — leading to absurdity.

This objection challenges the proof that the eternal cannot cease. It argues that the same reasoning used to prove that existence, once begun, cannot be removed, would also prove that non-existence, if eternal, cannot be removed. This leads to the absurd conclusion that the world could never have come into existence. Thus, the proof is self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Read the first part where the objection sets up a dilemma: either the non-existence of the world is necessary or possible by itself.
This mirrors the standard kalam categorization.
2
Identify implications of each horn
Notice what follows if non-existence is necessary (world never exists) vs. possible (needs a preponderator).
3
See the parallel to the original proof
The objection argues that whatever divisions the opponent used for existence apply equally to non-existence.
This is the key move.
4
Recognize the conclusion
If the proof for existence being immutable is sound, then non-existence is also immutable, meaning the world never came to be. Since the world does exist, the proof must be flawed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال السابع : عدم العالم وعدم مؤثرية قدرة الله تعالى في العالم إما أن يكون واجباً لذاته، أو ممكناً لذاته. فإن كان الأول امتنع زواله البتة ، فكان يجب أن لا يوجد العالم البتة . هذا خلف . وإما إن كان ممكناً . فإما أن يكون له مرجح ، أو لا يكون . فإن كان الأول ، فقد جوزتم الرجحان في طرف العدم لا المرجح . فلم لا يجوز مثله في طرف الوجود ؟ وإن كان الثاني فذلك المرجح إما أن يكن مختاراً أو موجباً. وجميع ما ذكرتموه من التقسيمات في علة الوجود ، عائد في علة العدم الأزلي، سواء بسواء من غير تفاوت فيثبت : أن الدليل الذي ذكرتموه ، إن دل على أن الوجود الأزلي ، ممتنع الزوال. فهو بعينه أيضاً يدل على أن العدم الأزلي ، ممتنع الزوال . ولو صح هذا ، لوجب كون العالم قديماً أزلياً ، على ما سبق تقريره . وحيث فسد هذا، علمنا : أن الأزلي جائز الزوال .
The seventh question: The non-existence of the world and the non-effect of God's power in the world are either necessary (wājib) by themselves or possible (mumkin) by themselves. If the first, then its removal is absolutely impossible, so the world should never exist at all. This is absurd. And if it is possible, then either it has a preponderator (murjih) or it does not. If the first, then you have allowed preponderance on the side of non-existence without a preponderator. Why then is the like not allowed on the side of existence? And if the second, then that preponderator is either free or necessitating. All the divisions you have mentioned concerning the cause of existence apply equally to the cause of eternal non-existence, without any difference. Thus, it is established that the proof you mentioned, if it indicates that eternal existence cannot cease, then it equally indicates that eternal non-existence cannot cease. And if this were sound, the world would have to be eternal and pre-eternal, as previously established. And since this is corrupt, we know that the eternal is susceptible to ceasing.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection, which is a more detailed argument. It begins by posing a dilemma regarding the non-existence of the world and God's power not affecting the world: either this non-existence is necessary by itself, or possible by itself. If necessary, then it could never cease, so the world would never exist—contradicting the observed existence. If possible, then it needs a preponderator to become actual. The author then argues that the same reasoning used by the opponent to prove that existence (once existent) cannot cease would also apply to non-existence. The opponent's proof tries to show that eternal existence cannot cease, but the same logic shows that eternal non-existence cannot cease either, leading to the conclusion that the world must be eternal (since non-existence never ceases). Since this is absurd (the world is not eternal), the original claim that the eternal cannot cease is refuted. The objection cleverly turns the opponent's own proof against them by applying it to the state of non-existence.

Key Terms
عدم (ʿadam)
وجود (wujūd)
واجب بالذات (wājib bi-dhātihi)
ممكن بالذات (mumkin bi-dhātihi)
مرجح (murjih)
علة (ʿilla)
أزلي (azalī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this objection denies God's power or causality altogether.
Correction: The objection does not deny causality; it only shows the inconsistency of the opponent's specific proof by applying it symmetrically to non-existence.

Checkpoint
What is the main strategy of this objection?
A) It argues that God's power is limited.
B) It applies the opponent's own proof for the immutability of existence to the state of non-existence, showing a contradiction.
C) It proves that the world is eternal.
D) It denies that non-existence can be possible.

Key Takeaway
The seventh objection demonstrates that the opponent's proof for the impossibility of change in the eternal applies equally to non-existence, leading to an absurdity and thus refuting the proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This objection shows that even the absence of something (non-existence) can cease. Where in your life have you accepted a spiritual 'non-existence'—like lack of faith, hope, or repentance—as permanent? Will you today ask God to remove that non-existence, trusting that He can bring existence out of nothing?
?
The objector demands consistency: if you apply a rule to existence, apply it equally to non-existence. Are you consistent in how you view God's power? Do you believe God can forgive your worst sin (bringing good from evil) but doubt He can change a deep-rooted habit? What contradiction might you be living with?
?
The opponent's proof crumbled because it led to an absurd conclusion. What belief about God or yourself have you held onto that, if followed through, leads to an absurdity? Are you willing to let that belief go today, even if it feels comfortable?

Refuting the Proof of Eternal Persistence by Mirroring Logic
Direct refutation of the proof for the impossibility of cessation for the eternal, using the opponent's own logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the proof for the impossibility of cessation for the eternal by applying the same logical disjunction to the 'soundness of the world's existence,' exposing contradictions in the opponent's argument.

Core Idea
The opponent's own logic, when applied to the world's existence, leads to the very conclusion they deny.

This passage marks a direct attack on the core argument used to assert the eternal's impossibility of cessation. The author does not just present a new objection but turns the opponent's method against them, revealing that their reasoning would also prove the impossibility of the world's existence in pre-eternity or allow for the cessation of the eternal. By mirroring the opponent's disjunction, the author exposes a fatal flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the opponent's original proof
First, note how the author summarizes the opponent's argument: the eternal is either necessary (wajib) or possible (mumkin); if necessary, it must persist; if possible, it needs an effecter. This sets the stage for the refutation.
The seven previous objections are mentioned but not detailed; the focus is on this new attack.
2
Observe the author's mirroring technique
The author applies the same 'necessary or possible' dichotomy to the 'soundness of the world's existence' (sihhat wujud al-alam). This is a strategic move to test the proof's consistency.
Notice that the author does not reject the dichotomy itself but uses it against the opponent.
3
Trace the consequences of each horn
If the soundness is necessary by itself, it must be permanent, contradicting the opponent's claim that the world's existence was impossible in pre-eternity. If soundness is possible, its origination either has an effecter or not, both leading to absurdities or to the allowance that the eternal could cease.
The passage cuts off mid-sentence; the conclusion is implied.
4
Reflect on the incomplete argument
Consider what the author might conclude: likely that the opponent's proof fails, and the eternal could be subject to non-existence. The incomplete ending suggests a continuation on the next page.
This lesson is based on a partial passage; a full understanding requires subsequent pages.

فهذه الأسئلة السبعة جارية مجرى النقوض على الذي ذكرتموه في تقرير : أن الأزلي لا يزول ثم إنا بعد هذه النقوض ، تعترض على الدليل الذي ذكرتم : فنقول ما الدليل على أن القديم لا يصح عليه العدم ؟ قوله : هذا الأزلي، إما أن يكون واجباً لذاته ، أو ممكناً لذاته . فإن كان واجباً لذاته، لزم القول بوجوب دوامه ، وإن كان ممكناً ، افتقر إلى مؤثر وذلك المؤثر إما موجب أو مختار ، فنقول : صحة وجود العالم ، إما أن تكون واجبة لذاتها، وإما أن تكون ممكنة لذاتها . فإن كانت واجبة لذاتها ، وجب كونها دائمة الثبوت، فيكون صحة وجود العالم ، حاصلة في الأزل ودليلكم يوجب امتناع وجود العالم في الأزل . والصحة والامتناع متناقضان فلما ثبت القول بدوام الصحة ، فسد القول بثبوت الامتناع في الأزل ، ولئن جاز أن يقال : الصحة واجبة الثبوت لذاتها، مع أنها غير دائمة . فلم لا يجوز أيضاً : أن يقال : إن ذلك القديم واجب الثبوت لذاته ، مع أنه غير دائم الثبوت ؟ وأما إن كانت هذه الصحة ، ممكنة الثبوت لذاتها . فنقول : حدوث هذه الصحة ، إما أن يكون لمؤثر أو لا لمؤثر. والأول باطل . لأن كل ما حصل بمؤثر ، فعند فرض عدم ذلك المؤثر ، يرتفع ذلك الأثر ، فعند فرض عدم ذلك المؤثر ، يلزم أن لا يبق الممكن ممكناً في نفسه ، بل ينقلب ممتنعاً لذاته . وذلك باطل . والثاني يوجب القول بأن تلك الصحة حصلت بعد عدمها ، لا لمؤثر . وإذا جاز ذلك ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن ذلك الأزلي يصير معدوماً ، بعد أن
Now these seven questions serve as objections (nuqūḍ) against what you have established: that the eternal does not cease. Then, after these objections, we object to the proof you mentioned: we say: what is the proof that the old (qadīm) cannot be subject to non-existence? His statement: 'This eternal is either necessary by itself or possible by itself. If it is necessary by itself, then the claim of its perpetuity is necessary, and if it is possible, it needs an effecter (muʾaththir) and that effecter is either necessitating or free.' We say: the soundness (ṣiḥḥa) of the world's existence is either necessary by itself or possible by itself. If it is necessary by itself, then it must be permanent, so the soundness of the world's existence would be realized in pre-eternity, and your proof would require the impossibility of the world's existence in pre-eternity. But soundness and impossibility are contradictory. Since the statement of the permanence of soundness is established, the statement of the establishment of impossibility in pre-eternity is invalid. And if it is permissible to say: 'Soundness is necessary by itself while it is not permanent,' then why is it not also permissible to say: 'That old (qadīm) is necessary by itself while it is not permanent'? And if this soundness is possible by itself, we say: the origination (ḥudūth) of this soundness either has an effecter or does not. The first is invalid, because everything that occurs by an effecter, when the non-existence of that effecter is assumed, that effect disappears. Thus, when the non-existence of that effecter is assumed, the possible would no longer remain possible in itself, but would become impossible by itself. That is invalid. The second necessitates the statement that this soundness occurred after its non-existence, without an effecter. And if that is permissible, then why is it not permissible to say: 'That eternal becomes non-existent, after...'
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a direct refutation of the opponent's proof that the eternal cannot cease. The author first summarizes the seven previous objections and then launches a new attack. He asks for the proof that the old (qadīm) cannot undergo non-existence. He then cites the opponent's argument: the eternal is either necessary or possible; if necessary, it must persist; if possible, it needs an effecter. The author deconstructs this by applying the same logical structure to the 'soundness of the world's existence.' He argues that if the soundness is necessary, it must be permanent, leading to a contradiction with the opponent's own conclusion that the world's existence is impossible in pre-eternity. If the soundness is possible, then its origination must be due to an effecter or not. The author shows that both alternatives lead to absurdities or to the conclusion that the eternal can become non-existent. The argument is incomplete in this window; it cuts off mid-sentence, but the pattern suggests the author is pushing the opponent into a similar dilemma to show the proof's weakness.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is just one of several independent objections to the proof of eternal persistence.
Correction: It is a direct refutation of the root proof itself, using the opponent's own logic against them to reveal inconsistencies.

Checkpoint
What is the primary purpose of the author's application of the 'necessary or possible' disjunction to the 'soundness of the world's existence'?
A) To prove that the soundness of the world's existence is necessary.
B) To refute the opponent's proof by showing its own logic leads to contradictions.
C) To argue that the eternal must be possible.
D) To establish that the world's existence is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the proof for the persistence of the eternal by showing that the same reasoning, when applied to the soundness of the world's existence, leads to contradictions or to the very possibility of cessation the opponent denied.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief that is challenged by a logical argument, are you quick to defend it or do you sincerely examine the counterargument? What might this reveal about your commitment to truth over comfort?
?
This lesson shows how the same reasoning can be turned against its user. In your own life, are there areas where you apply standards to others but not to yourself? How can you practice consistency in your judgments?
?
If you were the opponent in this debate, would you admit defeat once your logic is mirrored back to you? What would it take for you to change a deeply held conviction based on rational argument?

First Objection: The Possibility of the World's Existence
First objection against the proof of the eternal's permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the proof that the eternal cannot cease, and how it uses a parallel argument to challenge the necessity of the eternal's permanence.

Core Idea
The possibility of the world's existence is used as a mirror to challenge the necessity of the eternal's permanence.

This lesson presents the first objection raised against the proof that the eternal cannot cease to exist. The objector does not reject the existence of a Creator but instead applies the same logical structure to the 'possibility of the world's existence' to reveal an inconsistency. By doing so, the objector forces the defender to either accept a contradiction or concede the possibility of the eternal ceasing. This subtle dialectical move is a classic example of kalam argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the overall argument. Note that the objector first reconstructs the opponent's proof, then applies a parallel analysis.
2
Identify the parallel structure
Notice how the objector takes the concept of 'possibility of the world's existence' and treats it exactly as the opponent treated the eternal. See the two cases: either it is necessary or possible.
3
Analyze the consequences
Follow each branch: if necessary, contradiction arises because the opponent denies the world's existence in eternity; if possible, the objector argues that its occurrence without a cause implies the eternal could also cease without a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه الأسئلة السبعة جارية مجرى النقوض على الذي ذكرتموه في تقرير : أن الأزلي لا يزول . ثم إنا بعد هذه النقوض ، تعترض على الدليل الذي ذكرتم : فنقول ما (١) الدليل على أن القديم لا يصح عليه العدم ؟ قوله : ( هذا الأزلي، إما أن يكون واجباً لذاته ، أو ممكناً لذاته . فإن كان واجباً لذاته، لزم القول بوجوب دوامه ، وإن كان ممكناً ، افتقر إلى مؤثر [ وذلك المؤثر (۲) ] إما موجب أو مختار ، فنقول : صحة وجود العالم ، إما أن تكون واجبة لذاتها، وإما أن تكون ممكنة لذاتها . فإن كانت واجبة لذاتها ، وجب كونها دائمة الثبوت، فيكون صحة وجود العالم ، حاصلة في الأزل ودليلكم يوجب امتناع وجود العالم في الأزل . والصحة والامتناع متناقضان (۳) فلما ثبت [ القول (4) ] بدوام الصحة ، فسد القول بثبوت الامتناع في الأزل ، ولئن جاز أن يقال : الصحة واجبة الثبوت لذاتها، مع أنها غير دائمة . فلم لا يجوز أيضاً : أن يقال : إن ذلك القديم واجب الثبوت لذاته ، مع أنه غير دائم الثبوت ؟ وأما إن كانت هذه الصحة ، ممكنة الثبوت لذاتها . فنقول : حدوث هذه الصحة ، إما أن يكون لمؤثر أو لا لمؤثر. والأول باطل . لأن كل ما حصل بمؤثر ، فعند فرض عدم ذلك المؤثر ، يرتفع ذلك الأثر ، فعند فرض عدم ذلك المؤثر ، يلزم أن لا يبق الممكن ممكناً في نفسه ، بل ينقلب ممتنعاً لذاته . وذلك باطل . والثاني يوجب القول بأن تلك الصحة حصلت بعد عدمها ، لا لمؤثر . وإذا جاز ذلك ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن ذلك الأزلي يصير معدوماً ، بعد أن كان موجوداً - لا لمؤثر . وهذا سؤال غامض
These seven questions serve as objections to what you have stated in establishing that the eternal does not cease. Then after these objections, we object to the proof you mentioned: we say: what is the proof that the eternal (al-qadīm) cannot be subject to non-existence? Your statement: 'This eternal either is necessary in itself (wājib bi-dhātihi) or possible in itself (mumkin bi-dhātihi). If it is necessary in itself, then the necessity of its perpetuity follows; and if it is possible, it needs an effecter [and that effecter (2)] either necessitating (mūjib) or free (mukhtār).' We say: the possibility (ṣiḥḥa) of the existence of the world either is necessary in itself or possible in itself. If it is necessary in itself, then it must be permanently established, so the possibility of the world's existence would be present in eternity, whereas your proof necessitates the impossibility of the world's existence in eternity. Possibility and impossibility are contradictory. Since the statement of the perpetuity of possibility is established, the statement of the establishment of impossibility in eternity is invalidated. And if it is permissible to say that the possibility is necessarily established in itself while it is not perpetual, then why is it also not permissible to say that that eternal is necessarily established in itself while it is not perpetually established? And if this possibility is possibly established in itself, we say: the origination of this possibility either is from an effecter or not from an effecter. The first is false, because everything that occurs from an effecter, upon the assumption of the non-existence of that effecter, that effect ceases, so upon the assumption of the non-existence of that effecter, it follows that the possible does not remain possible in itself, but becomes impossible in itself. That is false. The second necessitates the statement that that possibility occurred after its non-existence without an effecter. And if that is permissible, then why is it not permissible to say that that eternal becomes non-existent after it was existent without an effecter? And this is a subtle question.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is the first objection (السؤال الأول) raised against the proof that the eternal cannot cease to exist. It begins by noting that there are seven objections. The objector then reconstructs the opponent's argument: the eternal is either necessary in itself or possible; if necessary, it must endure; if possible, it needs an effecter that is either necessitating or free. The objector then applies a parallel argument to the 'possibility of the world's existence' (ṣiḥḥat wujūd al-‘ālam). This possibility, he says, is either necessary in itself or possible. If necessary, it would be eternal, but the opponent's proof denies the world's existence in eternity—contradiction. If the opponent claims that a necessary thing can be non-eternal, then the same could be said of the eternal. If the possibility is itself possible, then its coming into existence requires a cause; but the objector argues that if it came without a cause, then the eternal could also cease without a cause. The objection thus uses a parity argument to challenge the necessity of the eternal's permanence.

أزلي
قديم
صحة وجود العالم
ممكن
واجب
مؤثر
موجب
مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This objection is an atheistic denial of the Creator.
Correction: The objection is a logical challenge within the kalam framework; it does not reject the existence of a Creator but uses a parallel argument to test consistency.

Checkpoint
What is the main strategy of the first objection?
A) Denying the existence of a Creator
B) Applying a parallel argument to the possibility of the world's existence
C) Asserting that the eternal is necessarily non-existent
D) Claiming that the world is eternal

Key Takeaway
The first objection uses a parity argument on the possibility of the world's existence to expose a contradiction in the proof that the eternal cannot cease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read a subtle logical objection that challenges a proof about the eternal. When you face an argument that seems to undermine your beliefs, do you immediately reject it or do you pause to analyze its logical structure? How can this lesson train you to engage with objections more honestly?
?
The objector uses a parity argument—what would it take for you to apply the same rigorous reasoning to your own assumptions about God's attributes? Are you willing to question the strength of your own proofs, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
If the possibility of the world's existence can be analyzed in this way, what does that say about your reliance on analogy and logical parallelism in your own reasoning about Allah? Will you commit to studying logic and kalam more deeply so that your faith is built on sound arguments, not just inherited beliefs?

Second Objection: Non-Existence Needs a Cause Too
Objection: possible non-existence also needs a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to the causal argument, which claims that possible non-existence also requires a cause.

Core Idea
If existence needs a cause, non-existence must need one too.

In theological debates about the eternity of the world, the opponent often argues that a possible being requires a cause. But the objector turns this logic around: if existence demands a cause, why not non-existence? This passage presents that symmetrical challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Note that it begins with 'السؤال الثاني' (The second question).
The passage is a single sentence in Arabic, so pay attention to the logic.
2
Identify the core analogy
The objection draws a parallel: if a possible existent needs a cause, then possible non-existence (the state of not being) also needs a cause. This is the key move.
Possible means contingent — something that could be or not be.
3
Notice the examples
The objector gives two examples: the non-existence of the world in eternity (before creation) and the non-existence of God in eternity (hypothetically, as a parity argument).
This does NOT assert God was non-existent; it's a logical tool.
4
Understand the threat
The objection says the same division (necessary/possible etc.) applies to non-existence, leading to infinite regress or absurdity if we require a cause for non-existence.
This challenges the opponent's assumption that only existence needs a cause.
5
Grasp the common misunderstanding
Many mistakenly think this objection claims God was non-existent. Actually, it's a hypothetical argument to test the consistency of causality.
The objector uses 'God's non-existence' as a logical possibility, not a reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : أن نقول : إن كان الموجود الممكن ، لا بد له من علة ، فالعدم الممكن أيضاً لا بد له من علة . فعدم وجود العالم في الأزل ، وعدم موجودية الله [ تعالى(۱) ] في الأزل ، لا بد له من علة . ويعود التقسيم المذكور فيه ، إلى آخره .
The second question: that we say: if a possible existent must have a cause, then possible non-existence must also have a cause. So the non-existence of the world in eternity, and the non-existence of God [Exalted (1)] in eternity, must have a cause. And the division mentioned therein returns, etc.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is the second objection (السؤال الثاني). It draws an analogy: if a possible being (like the world) requires a cause for its existence, then possible non-existence—the state of not being—also requires a cause. The objector specifically points to the non-existence of the world in eternity (before its creation) and the non-existence of God in eternity (but note: this is not implying God is non-existent; it is a hypothetical parity argument). He then says the same division (into necessary/possible etc.) applies, leading to an infinite regress or other problems. The objection aims to show that the opponent's reasoning about causality applies symmetrically to non-existence, undermining the claim that the eternal's non-existence is impossible.

Key Terms
علة
موجود ممكن
عدم ممكن
أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection claims God was non-existent in eternity.
Correction: This is a hypothetical parity argument to challenge the causal framework, not an assertion of God's non-existence.

Checkpoint
What does the second objection claim about possible non-existence?
A) It needs no cause
B) It needs a cause just like possible existence
C) It is impossible
D) It is eternal

Key Takeaway
The objection forces the defender of causality to explain why non-existence does not need a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Third Objection: God's Choice of a Specific Time
Challenges the agent's choice of a specific time for creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that challenges divine free will by focusing on the problem of timing creation, and how the argument attempts to force a choice between necessitation and an infinite regress of preponderators.

Core Idea
If God had to choose when to create, either He had no choice or His choice needs another cause.

This passage presents the third objection to the Ash'ari position on the world's origination. It zeroes in on the specific moment of creation: why this instant rather than another? The objector constructs a dilemma meant to show that divine free will cannot account for temporal specificity without leading to logical absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection's dilemma
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the two horns of the dilemma: either God could not create at another time (making Him a necessitator) or He could (making the choice arbitrary or requiring a cause).
The dilemma sets up the entire argument.
2
Explore the first horn: necessitation
Consider the first possibility: if God was unable to create at any other time, then He is a necessitator (mūjib bi-dhātihi). This implies the world is eternal because His action is necessary and timeless.
3
Explore the second horn: need for a preponderator
If God could create at another time, then why did He choose this one? The objector argues that this choice requires a preponderator (murajjiḥ) to tip the scales. But then that preponderator itself needs a reason, leading to infinite regress.
4
Analyze the final reductio
The objector then says that if we accept that preference can occur without a preponderator, then we must also accept that the eternal (al-qadīm) could cease to exist without cause. This is meant to show the absurdity of allowing preference without cause.
This is a key move in the argument.
5
Reflect on the overall challenge
Think about how this objection targets the concept of voluntary divine action. It tries to show that temporal specificity cannot be rationally explained without giving up either divine freedom or logical consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : لو كان العالم حادثاً ، لكان حدوثه مختصاً بوقت معين . فالفاعل الذي خصص إحداثه بذلك الوقت [ المعين (٢) ] إن امتنع منه أن يحدثه في غير ذلك الوقت ، فحينئذ يكون موجباً بالذات ، لا فاعلا بالاختيار . ثم يلزم من كونه موجباً بالذات، قدم العالم. وأما إن جاز من ذلك الفاعل ، أن يحدث العالم في ذلك الوقت، وأن يحدثه في غيره ، بدلاً عن الأول . فحينئذ إما أن يكون ذلك الترجيح (۳) موقوفاً على المرجح ، أو لا يكون كذلك . والأول باطل ، لأن الكلام في اختصاص ذلك الوقت بذلك المرجح ، كالكلام في اختصاصه بذلك الحادث ، ويعود الطلب فيه بعينه، ويمر إلى ما لا نهاية لـه فيبقى القسم الثاني، وهو أن يقال : اختصاص ذلك الوقت بهذا الأثر : رجحان لا لمرجح . فنقول : إذا جاز هذا ، فلم لا يجوز أيضاً : أن يعدم القديم بعد وجوده لا المرجح ، ولا المخصص ؟ فإنه ليس أحد البابين أظهر امتناعاً وفساداً عند العقل من الثاني (4)
The third question: if the world is originated, then its origination would be specific to a particular time. So the agent who specified its bringing into existence at that [particular] time (2): if it is impossible for him to bring it about at other than that time, then he would be a necessitator (mūjib bi-dhātihi), not a free agent (fā‘il bi-l-ikhtiyār). Then from his being a necessitator by essence, the eternity of the world follows. But if it is possible for that agent to bring about the world at that time and to bring it about at another time instead of the first, then either that preference (tarjīḥ) (3) depends on a preponderator (murajjiḥ) or not. The first is false, because the discussion about the specificity of that time to that preponderator is like the discussion about its specificity to that event, and the inquiry returns in the same way and goes on to infinity. So the second division remains, which is to say: the specificity of that time to this effect is a preference without a preponderator. We say: if this is permissible, then why is it not also permissible that the eternal (al-qadīm) become non-existent after its existence without a preponderator or specifier? For neither of the two doors is more apparent in impossibility and corruption in the intellect than the second (4).
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is the third objection (السؤال الثالث). It focuses on the problem of time specification. If the world is created in time, why this particular moment? The objector presents a dilemma: if God was unable to create at another time, then He is a necessitator (mūjib bi-dhātihi), which would imply the world is eternal (since His action would be necessary and timeless). If He could create at another time, then why did He choose this time? This choice requires a preponderator (murajjiḥ) to tip the scales. But that preponderator itself would need another preponderator, leading to infinite regress. The only alternative is to allow preference without a preponderator (rājih lā li-murajjiḥ). The objector then argues: if such preference is accepted, then why not also accept that the eternal can cease to exist without any specifier or cause? He claims that both possibilities (temporal preference and cessation of the eternal) are equally absurd or acceptable to the intellect.

Key Terms
حادث
فاعل بالاختيار
موجب بالذات
ترجيح
مرجح
اختصاص وقت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This objection denies that God has free will.
Correction: The objection actually problematizes the notion of temporal choice without a cause; it does not deny divine free will but challenges its rational coherence in the context of a created world.

Checkpoint
What is the third objection's main point about the agent who creates at a specific time?
A) The agent must be omnipotent
B) The agent's choice either implies necessitation or requires a preponderator, leading to regress
C) The agent cannot be free
D) The agent must be eternal

Key Takeaway
The objection attempts to prove that temporal specificity of creation forces an unacceptable choice between divine necessitation and infinite regress, thereby undermining the concept of voluntary divine action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a decision where no option seems rationally superior, do you trust that there is an unseen reason for your choice, or do you believe your will itself is the ultimate decider? How does this lesson's discussion of 'preference without preponderator' challenge your view?
?
If God's choice of the time of your birth was not arbitrary, what does that imply about the significance of every moment you live? Will you live today as if every second has a divine purpose, or will you drift aimlessly?
?
The objector argues that allowing groundless preference would permit the eternal to cease without cause. Do you rely on the certainty that God is eternal and unchanging, or do you subconsciously doubt His permanence? How does this passage call you to strengthen your conviction?

Fourth Objection: Challenging the Necessary Origination of Free Acts
The objector grants a premise but challenges the opponent's universal claim about free acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's strategy of conceding a minor premise while challenging the opponent's universal claim that every act of a free agent is originated.

Core Idea
Free agency does not imply temporal origination.

In this passage, the objector makes a strategic concession: even if an eternal were to cease, it would require a preponderator. He then questions why that preponderator cannot be a powerful agent. He counters the opponent's claim that every act of a free agent is originated, pointing to arguments that the dependence of an effect on an effecter is not conditioned by origination. This forces us to reconsider the relationship between free will and temporal creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the structure of the objection come through.
Notice the strategic concession at the beginning.
2
Identify the concession and the challenge
The objector grants the need for a preponderator for cessation but then challenges why that preponderator cannot be a powerful agent.
3
Explore the key terms
Focus on 'مرجح', 'فاعل مختار', 'محدث', and 'استناد الأثر إلى المؤثر'. Understand how they frame the debate.
The counter argument hinges on 'istinād al-athar ilā al-mu’aththir' not requiring 'ḥudūth'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع : سلمنا أن ذلك القديم ، لو عدم بعد وجوده ، فلا بد له من مرجح . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك المرجح [ قادراً (۰) ؟ ] قوله : ( كل ما كان فعلا، لفاعل مختار فهو محدث قلنا : هذا معارض بالوجوه الكثيرة المذكورة في بيان أن استناد الأثر إلى المؤثر غير مشروط بالحدوث .
The fourth question: we grant that that eternal, if it were to become non-existent after its existence, must have a preponderator. So why is it not permissible that that preponderator be [capable (0)?] His statement: 'Everything that is an act of a free agent is originated (muḥdath).' We say: this is opposed by the many aspects mentioned in the explanation that the dependence of an effect on an effecter is not conditioned by origination (ḥudūth).
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is the fourth objection (السؤال الرابع). The objector concedes (as a hypothesis) that if the eternal were to cease, it would need a preponderator. But then he asks: why can't that preponderator be a powerful agent (qādir)? The opponent reportedly claims that every act of a free agent is necessarily originated (muḥdath). The objector counters that this is not universally accepted; he refers to various arguments (not detailed here) showing that the dependence of an effect on an effecter (istinād al-athar ilā al-mu’aththir) is not necessarily tied to temporal origination (ḥudūth). In other words, an eternal effect could still depend on a free cause. This objection thus challenges the opponent's assumption that free agency implies temporal origination.

Key Terms
مرجح
فاعل مختار
محدث
استناد الأثر إلى المؤثر
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is rejecting free will entirely.
Correction: The objection does not reject free will; it challenges the link between free agency and temporal creation, arguing that an eternal effect can depend on a free cause.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth objection concede and then challenge?
A) It concedes the world is eternal and challenges the need for a cause
B) It concedes the need for a preponderator for cessation and challenges the claim that acts of free agents are always originated
C) It concedes God is not free and challenges causality
D) It concedes the eternal cannot cease and challenges all objections

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection shows that free agency does not necessitate temporal origination; an eternal effect can still depend on a free cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In what area of your belief might you be confining God to your own categories of time and causation? Will you consciously repent of that and ask God to expand your understanding?

When you encounter a theological objection that challenges a widely-held assumption (like 'free acts are always originated'), do you react with humility or defensiveness? Examine your own heart: are you willing to hold your beliefs with openness to correction from deeper reflection, or do you cling to them out of comfort?
?

Take a moment to ask God to show you how your connection to Him transcends time.

Four Objections Against the Proof of Creation
Al-Razi enumerates four objections to the proof of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand four rational objections to the proof of the world's temporal origination, focusing on issues of causality, time specification, and conditional causation.

Core Idea
The objections probe the logical coherence of creation by raising puzzles about causation and time.

In this passage, al-Razi presents four demanding objections against the standard proof of creation. These objections target different aspects: the causation of non-existence, the problem of time specification, the agent's freedom, and conditional causation. We will examine each objection in turn to grasp the subtlety of the dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections carefully
Read the Arabic passage of the four objections once, then the translation. Notice how each objection is introduced as 'السؤال الثاني' etc.
The objections are numbered second through fifth; the first is missing from this passage.
2
Identify the key claim of each objection
For each objection, write down the main point it tries to make. The second objection argues that non-existence also needs a cause; the third deals with time specification and preponderance; the fourth challenges the claim that every act of a free agent is originated; the fifth introduces a privative condition.
3
See how each targets a different aspect
Notice that the objections are not random but systematically attack different premises of the original proof: causality, time, agency, and conditional causation.
4
Consider why al-Razi calls them 'obscure'
Al-Razi labels the first objection (not here) as obscure. Reflect on the complexity of these objections and why they might be considered deep puzzles.
The answer will later dismiss them as attacks on necessary truths.

كان موجوداً - لا لمؤثر . وهذا سؤال غامض
السؤال الثاني : أن نقول : إن كان الموجود الممكن ، لا بد له من علة فالعدم الممكن أيضاً لا بد له من علة . فعدم وجود العالم في الأزل ، وعدم موجودية الله [ تعالى(۱) ] في الأزل ، لا بد له من علة . ويعود التقسيم المذكور فيه ، إلى آخره .
السؤال الثالث : لو كان العالم حادثاً ، لكان حدوثه مختصاً بوقت معين . فالفاعل الذي خصص إحداثه بذلك الوقت [ المعين (٢) ] إن امتنع منه أن يحدثه في غير ذلك الوقت ، فحينئذ يكون موجباً بالذات ، لا فاعلا بالاختيار . ثم يلزم من كونه موجباً بالذات، قدم العالم. وأما إن جاز من ذلك الفاعل ، أن يحدث العالم في ذلك الوقت، وأن يحدثه في غيره ، بدلاً عن الأول . فحينئذ إما أن يكون ذلك الترجيح (۳) موقوفاً على المرجح ، أو لا يكون كذلك . والأول باطل ، لأن الكلام في اختصاص ذلك الوقت بذلك المرجح ، كالكلام في
اختصاصه بذلك الحادث ، ويعود الطلب فيه بعينه، ويمر إلى ما لا نهاية لـه فيبقى القسم الثاني، وهو أن يقال : اختصاص ذلك الوقت بهذا الأثر : رجحان لا لمرجح . فنقول : إذا جاز هذا ، فلم لا يجوز أيضاً : أن يعدم القديم بعد وجوده لا المرجح ، ولا المخصص ؟ فإنه ليس أحد البابين أظهر امتناعاً وفساداً عند العقل من الثاني (4)
السؤال الرابع : سلمنا أن ذلك القديم ، لو عدم بعد وجوده ، فلا بد له من مرجح . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك المرجح [ قادراً (۰) ؟ ] قوله : ( كل ما كان فعلا، لفاعل مختار فهو محدث قلنا : هذا معارض بالوجوه الكثيرة المذكورة في بيان أن استناد الأثر إلى المؤثر غير مشروط بالحدوث .
السؤال الخامس : سلمنا أن ذلك المؤثر موجب ، فلم يلزم من دوامه :
دوام المعلول ؟ بيانه : أن [ المؤثر (۱) | الموجود لا يبعد أن يتوقف تأثيره في معلوله على شرط عدمي ألا ترى أن الثقل يوجب نزول الثقيل، بشرط عدم السلسلة العائقة عن النزول ، وأنتم جوزتم في العدم الأزلي : أن يزول . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الموجود الواجب لذاته ، كان علة لوجود ذلك القديم، إلا أن ذلك التأثير ، كان مشروطاً بشرط عدمي أزلي . ثم إن ذلك الشرط العدمي الأزلي ، قد زاله . فلا جرم زال ذلك المعلول
