Resolving the Fixed Stars Controversy
The author refutes the claim that ancients erred and offers an alternative explanation involving an extra sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the ancient observers and proposes an alternative explanation for the discrepancy in fixed star observations.

Core Idea
The ancients could not have erred; an extra sphere explains the apparent change.

The author addresses the controversy regarding the different rates of fixed star motion reported by ancient and later astronomers. He strongly defends the ancients by pointing to their accurate observations of the difficult planet Mercury, and then provides a novel astronomical explanation involving an additional sphere that causes the fixed stars to appear to move faster over time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Understand the defense and the alternative explanation.
2
Examine the Mercury argument
See why the author considers the ancients' reliability.
3
Understand the proposed cause
Learn about the extra sphere and its effect on the fixed star pole.
4

ومن عجائب أحوال هذه الحركات أن في حركات الأفلاك قولان. فالقول الأول وهو المشهور أن أسرع الكرات حركة هو الفلك الأعظم، فإنه يتحرك في اليوم والليلة على التقريب دورة تامة من المشرق إلى المغرب، وأما الفلك الذي حصل مماساً له في داخله وهو فلك الثوابت، فإنه يتحرك على مذهب المتقدمين في كل مائة سنة درجة واحدة، وعلى مذهب المتأخرين في كل ست وستين سنة درجة واحدة. ثم اختلفوا، فمنهم من قال هذا التفاوت إنما كان لأن المتقدمين غلطوا في الرصد، وأما المتأخرون فقد تنبهوا لذلك الغلط. ومن المحققين من قال نسبة هذا الغلط إلى المتقدمين كالبعيد جدا، وذلك لأن رصد عطارد كالمتعذر عند العقل، وذلك لأنه ليس له بعد كثير من الشمس البتة، وإنما يظهر أياما قلائل، وفي تلك الأيام فهو كوكب صغير الجرم، وليس له نور قاهر حتى يسهل ضبطه في آلات الرصد، فمع هذه الصعوبة التامة صارت أرصاد المتقدمين وافية بضبط حركاته ومعرفة أفلاكه، فلما لم يتفق لهم الغلط البتة في هذا المقام الصعب، فكيف يتفق لهم الغلط في حركات الكواكب الثابتة مع عظم أجرامها وقوة أنوارها؟ بل السبب في حصول هذا التفاوت أنه حصل فوق كرة الثوابت وتحت الفلك الأعظم كرة أخرى، وقطب فلك الثوابت منطبق على نقطة من تلك الكرة التي ذكرناها انطباقا، ليلزم من حركة هذه الكرات وصول قطب فلك الثوابت إلى حيث يصير منطبقا على قطب الفلك الأعظم، فلأجل هذه الحالة يصير مقدار الميل الأعظم متناقضا أبدا، وتصير حركات الثوابت أسرع في النظر والحس.
And among the wondrous aspects of these movements is that concerning the movements of the spheres there are two opinions. The first opinion, which is well-known, is that the fastest of the orbs in movement is the highest sphere, for it moves in a day and night approximately one complete revolution from east to west. As for the sphere that is tangent to it on its interior, which is the sphere of fixed stars, it moves according to the doctrine of the ancients one degree every hundred years, and according to the doctrine of the later scholars one degree every sixty-six years. Then they differed: some said this discrepancy is only because the ancients erred in observation, while the later scholars became aware of that error. But some investigators said that attributing this error to the ancients is extremely far-fetched, because the observation of Mercury is virtually impossible by reason, since it never has much distance from the sun, and it only appears for a few days, and during those days it is a small-bodied planet, and it does not have overpowering light to make it easy to fix in observational instruments. Yet despite this complete difficulty, the observations of the ancients were sufficient for recording its movements and knowing its spheres. Since they did not make any error in this difficult matter, how could they make an error in the movements of the fixed stars, given the greatness of their bodies and the strength of their lights? Rather, the cause of this discrepancy is that above the sphere of fixed stars and below the highest sphere there occurs another sphere, and the pole of the sphere of fixed stars coincides with a point of that sphere which we mentioned, so that from the movement of these spheres it necessarily follows that the pole of the sphere of fixed stars reaches a point where it comes to coincide with the pole of the highest sphere. Because of this state, the amount of the greatest inclination perpetually decreases, and the movements of the fixed stars become faster in sight and sensation.

This resolves the discrepancy without accusing the ancients of error.

The Second Opinion on Celestial Motions
The author presents the second opinion and its first rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second opinion that all celestial spheres move from east to west with the outermost fastest, and the rational basis for this view.

Core Idea
All spheres move east to west; the fastest is the highest sphere.

After presenting the first opinion, the author now introduces a competing view: all celestial spheres move from east to west, but with varying speeds. The author claims this opinion is more rational. The passage explains the apparent westward motion of fixed stars as an illusion due to their lag behind the highest sphere, and gives the first of two proofs: the outermost sphere is naturally the fastest.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the author's argument structure.
The passage ends abruptly; the second proof is cut off.
2
Identify the key claim and explanation
The second opinion claims all spheres move east to west. The apparent westward motion of fixed stars is explained as an illusion due to lag. Understand this mechanism.
The lag accumulates over time, causing the illusion.
3
Analyze the rational proof
The first proof is rational: the outermost sphere (highest) is fastest, inner spheres progressively slower. This is presented as more logical than the first opinion.
Consider why this might be considered more rational.

والقول الثاني إن الحركات الفلكية كلها آخذة من المشرق إلى المغرب. وأسرعها هو الفلك الأعظم، ويليه في السرعة حركة فلك الثوابت. فإنا إذا فرضنا أن الفلك الأعظم وذلك الثوابت أخذا في الحركة من نقطة معينة، فإذا وصل الفلك الأعظم في اليوم الثاني إلى عين تلك النقطة، فإن فلك الثوابت لا يصل في ذلك المقدار إلى عين تلك النقطة بل يتخلف عنها بشيء قليل. فإذا اجتمعت تلك المقادير، وقع في الحس، كان الكواكب الثابتة تتحرك من المغرب إلى المشرق، وليس الأمر كذلك. وإنما وقع الأمر على هذا الوجه في الحس، للسبب الذي ذكرناه. وأصحاب هذا القول، قالوا إن هذا القول أولى من القول الأول ويدل عليه وجهان: الأول إن هذا القول أقرب إلى العقل. فإن المحيط الأصلي هو الفلك الأعظم. فأسرع الحركات له. ثم الذي يليه أبطأ منه، وأسرع داخله. وعلى هذا التقدير فأبطأ الحركات الفلكية فلك القمر، وبعده كرة النار، ثم كرة الهواء. وأما حركات الماء والبحر ففي غاية البطء، وأما الأرض ففي غاية
The second opinion is that all celestial movements are taking from east to west. The fastest of them is the highest sphere, followed in speed by the movement of the sphere of fixed stars. For if we suppose that the highest sphere and that of fixed stars start moving from a certain point, when the highest sphere reaches the same point on the second day, the sphere of fixed stars does not in that amount reach the same point but lags behind it by a little something. When those amounts accumulate, it occurs in sensation that the fixed stars move from west to east, but it is not so. It only occurs in this way in sensation because of the cause we mentioned. The proponents of this opinion said that this opinion is more deserving than the first opinion, and two proofs indicate it: first, this opinion is closer to reason. For the original encompassing sphere is the highest sphere, so the fastest movement belongs to it. Then what follows it is slower than it, and faster than its interior. On this estimation, the slowest of the celestial movements is the sphere of the moon, after it the sphere of fire, then the sphere of air. As for the movements of water and sea, they are extremely slow, and as for the earth, it is extremely [slow].
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage presents the second opinion regarding celestial motions and its first proof (the second is cut off). The author states that all spheres move from east to west, with the highest sphere fastest, then the fixed stars slower, etc. The apparent westward-to-eastward motion of fixed stars is explained as an illusion due to the lag of the fixed stars behind the highest sphere. The supporters of this opinion claim it is preferable to the first opinion, and they provide two proofs. The first proof is rational: the outermost sphere (the highest) is naturally the fastest, and inner spheres are progressively slower, following a logical order from fastest (highest) to slowest (earth). This is more rational than the first opinion which had the fixed stars moving very slowly despite being near the highest sphere. The second proof is not given in the available text.

The Principle of Celestial Motion and Rest
A simple enumeration of the principle of motion and rest in concentric spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in concentric spheres, motion is associated with the periphery and rest with the center, and speed is proportional to distance from the center.

Core Idea
In celestial spheres, the periphery moves, the center rests, and speed increases outward.

This lesson introduces a fundamental principle of ancient astronomy and natural philosophy: the relationship between motion and rest in concentric celestial spheres. Understanding this principle is essential for grasping later arguments about the motions of the fixed stars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation, to get the direct meaning.
Notice the rhythmic progression from periphery to center.
2
Identify the three claims
The passage makes three linked claims: (1) the periphery has motion, the center has rest; (2) closeness to periphery increases speed; (3) closeness to center decreases speed to extreme rest.
These are presented as self-evident.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this principle matters for understanding celestial motion. It establishes a natural order of movements in the cosmos.
This principle will be used later to argue about the spheres.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السكون . فالمحيط له الحركة ، والمركز له السكون . وكل ما كان أقرب إلى المحيط ، كان أسرع حركة . وكل ما كان أقرب إلى المركز ، كان أبطأ ، حتى انتهى الأمر إلى غاية السكون . فهذا أمر معقول مناسب .
والثاني : إن فلك الثوابت حال حركتها بطبعه إلى المشرق ، هل يتحرك بتحويل الفلك الأعظم إلى المغرب ؟ أو يقال : إنه في أحد الزمانين يتحرك بطبعه إلى أحد الجانبين ، وفي الزمان الثاني يتحرك بالقسر إلى الجانب الثاني فلا تحصل الحركتان معاً، في ذلك الجسم؟ والأول. باطل. لأنه إذا تحرك بطبعه إلى هذا الجانب ، فقد حصل في هذا الجانب. وإذا تحرك بسبب القسر إلى ذلك الجانب ، فقد حصل في ذلك الجانب. فلو اجتمع هاتان الحركتان ، لزم حصول الجسم الواحد دفعة واحدة في مكانين ، وذلك محال . والعلم بامتناعه بديهي . سواء قلنا : إنه حصلت الحركتان طبيعيتين أو قسريتين ، أو إحداهما طبيعية ، والأخرى قسرية
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إنه يحصل إحدى الحركتين في زمان ، والحركة الثانية في زمان آخر ، فحينئذ يلزم انقطاع الحركات الفلكية وعدم بقائها . وذلك محال . وهؤلاء الناس رجحوا هذا القول على القول الأول بهذين الوجهين . وأما أصحاب القول الأول ، فقالوا : لو كان الأمر على هذا التقدير، فحينئذ قد دارت الشمس دورة واحدة بالتمام في اليوم الواحد ، وإنما حصل التخلف بمقدار وسط الشمس ، ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لوجب أن تتخلف أحوال الأظلال ، كما تتخلف عند كون الشمس في أول السرطان ، وفي أول الجدي. وحيث لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، علمنا هذا القول
Rest. So the periphery has motion, and the center has rest. And everything that is closer to the periphery is faster in motion. And everything that is closer to the center is slower, until the matter reaches the extreme of rest. So this is a reasonable and appropriate matter.

And the second: Regarding the sphere of the fixed stars, while it naturally moves towards the east, does it move by the rotation of the highest sphere towards the west? Or is it said that it moves in one period naturally to one side, and in the second period forcibly to the second side, so that the two motions do not occur together in that body? The first is false. Because if it moves naturally to this side, then it has arrived at this side. And if it moves due to force to that side, then it has arrived at that side. If these two motions were to combine, it would necessitate the same body being in two places at one time, and that is impossible. The knowledge of its impossibility is self-evident. Whether we say that both motions are natural or forced, or one is natural and the other forced.

As for the second division: that is, it is said that one of the two motions occurs in one time, and the second motion in another time, then this necessitates the interruption of celestial motions and their non-continuance. And that is impossible. And these people preferred this opinion over the first opinion by these two aspects. As for the proponents of the first opinion, they said: If the matter were according to this estimation, then the sun would have completed one full rotation in one day, and the lag would only be by the amount of the mean sun. And if the matter were so, it would be necessary that the states of shadows lag, just as they lag when the sun is at the beginning of Cancer and at the beginning of Capricorn. And since the matter is not so, we know this opinion.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about the motion of celestial bodies, specifically the sphere of fixed stars. It opens with a general principle: the periphery is associated with motion, the center with rest; proximity to the periphery increases speed, proximity to the center decreases it. This is presented as self-evident.

The main discussion then addresses a second issue: whether the sphere of fixed stars, which naturally moves eastward, can also be moved westward by the highest sphere. Two possibilities are examined. The first possibility—that both motions occur simultaneously—is rejected because a single body cannot be in two places at once. This is a basic logical impossibility, whether the motions are natural or forced.

The second possibility—that the motions occur sequentially in different times—is also rejected because it would mean the celestial motions are intermittent, which is impossible given the perpetual nature of the cosmos.

The author then reports that some scholars prefer the second opinion for these two reasons (simultaneous impossibility and interruption). However, proponents of the first opinion (that the sun rotates fully each day) respond with an empirical argument: if the sun completed a full rotation daily, the lag of shadows would behave differently than observed—specifically, it would lag like at solstices. Since observations do not match this, the first opinion is (in their view) correct. The passage ends without explicitly resolving the debate, leaving it as a point of consideration.

الحركة
المحيط
المركز

A) At the center
B) At the periphery
C) Midway between center and periphery
D) It does not specify

Key Takeaway
The periphery moves, the center rests, and speed increases outward from the center.

The Impossibility of Two Simultaneous Motions in One Celestial Body
A detailed argument against two simultaneous contradictory motions in the sphere of fixed stars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a celestial body cannot have two simultaneous contradictory motions, and what alternative explanations were proposed and refuted.

Core Idea
A single body cannot be in two places at once, so two simultaneous motions are impossible.

This lesson tackles a key puzzle in medieval astronomy: how the sphere of fixed stars can move both eastward (by nature) and westward (by the highest sphere). The author considers two possibilities and argues against both, while also presenting an empirical counterargument from supporters of the first opinion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to follow the logical steps: the question, two possible answers, and the refutation of each.
Note the key terms: 'طبيعي' (natural), 'قسر' (forced).
2
Understand the first refutation
The passage argues that if both motions occurred simultaneously, the body would be in two places at once, which is impossible.
This is a logical impossibility, not empirical.
3
Understand the second refutation
If motions occur sequentially, celestial motions would be intermittent, which is also impossible.
4
Consider the empirical argument
The proponents of the first opinion (full rotation daily) argue that observations of shadows contradict the prediction of the alternative theory.
This shows how empirical evidence was used.

السكون . فالمحيط له الحركة ، والمركز له السكون . وكل ما كان أقرب إلى المحيط ، كان أسرع حركة . وكل ما كان أقرب إلى المركز ، كان أبطأ ، حتى انتهى الأمر إلى غاية السكون . فهذا أمر معقول مناسب .
والثاني : إن فلك الثوابت حال حركتها بطبعه إلى المشرق ، هل يتحرك بتحويل الفلك الأعظم إلى المغرب ؟ أو يقال : إنه في أحد الزمانين يتحرك بطبعه إلى أحد الجانبين ، وفي الزمان الثاني يتحرك بالقسر إلى الجانب الثاني فلا تحصل الحركتان معاً، في ذلك الجسم؟ والأول. باطل. لأنه إذا تحرك بطبعه إلى هذا الجانب ، فقد حصل في هذا الجانب. وإذا تحرك بسبب القسر إلى ذلك الجانب ، فقد حصل في ذلك الجانب. فلو اجتمع هاتان الحركتان ، لزم حصول الجسم الواحد دفعة واحدة في مكانين ، وذلك محال . والعلم بامتناعه بديهي . سواء قلنا : إنه حصلت الحركتان طبيعيتين أو قسريتين ، أو إحداهما طبيعية ، والأخرى قسرية
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إنه يحصل إحدى الحركتين في زمان ، والحركة الثانية في زمان آخر ، فحينئذ يلزم انقطاع الحركات الفلكية وعدم بقائها . وذلك محال . وهؤلاء الناس رجحوا هذا القول على القول الأول بهذين الوجهين . وأما أصحاب القول الأول ، فقالوا : لو كان الأمر على هذا التقدير، فحينئذ قد دارت الشمس دورة واحدة بالتمام في اليوم الواحد ، وإنما حصل التخلف بمقدار وسط الشمس ، ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لوجب أن تتخلف أحوال الأظلال ، كما تتخلف عند كون الشمس في أول السرطان ، وفي أول الجدي. وحيث لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، علمنا هذا القول
Rest. So the periphery has motion, and the center has rest. And everything that is closer to the periphery is faster in motion. And everything that is closer to the center is slower, until the matter reaches the extreme of rest. So this is a reasonable and appropriate matter.

And the second: Regarding the sphere of the fixed stars, while it naturally moves towards the east, does it move by the rotation of the highest sphere towards the west? Or is it said that it moves in one period naturally to one side, and in the second period forcibly to the second side, so that the two motions do not occur together in that body? The first is false. Because if it moves naturally to this side, then it has arrived at this side. And if it moves due to force to that side, then it has arrived at that side. If these two motions were to combine, it would necessitate the same body being in two places at one time, and that is impossible. The knowledge of its impossibility is self-evident. Whether we say that both motions are natural or forced, or one is natural and the other forced.

As for the second division: that is, it is said that one of the two motions occurs in one time, and the second motion in another time, then this necessitates the interruption of celestial motions and their non-continuance. And that is impossible. And these people preferred this opinion over the first opinion by these two aspects. As for the proponents of the first opinion, they said: If the matter were according to this estimation, then the sun would have completed one full rotation in one day, and the lag would only be by the amount of the mean sun. And if the matter were so, it would be necessary that the states of shadows lag, just as they lag when the sun is at the beginning of Cancer and at the beginning of Capricorn. And since the matter is not so, we know this opinion.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about the motion of celestial bodies, specifically the sphere of fixed stars. It opens with a general principle: the periphery is associated with motion, the center with rest; proximity to the periphery increases speed, proximity to the center decreases it. This is presented as self-evident.

The main discussion then addresses a second issue: whether the sphere of fixed stars, which naturally moves eastward, can also be moved westward by the highest sphere. Two possibilities are examined. The first possibility—that both motions occur simultaneously—is rejected because a single body cannot be in two places at once. This is a basic logical impossibility, whether the motions are natural or forced.

The second possibility—that the motions occur sequentially in different times—is also rejected because it would mean the celestial motions are intermittent, which is impossible given the perpetual nature of the cosmos.

The author then reports that some scholars prefer the second opinion for these two reasons (simultaneous impossibility and interruption). However, proponents of the first opinion (that the sun rotates fully each day) respond with an empirical argument: if the sun completed a full rotation daily, the lag of shadows would behave differently than observed—specifically, it would lag like at solstices. Since observations do not match this, the first opinion is (in their view) correct. The passage ends without explicitly resolving the debate, leaving it as a point of consideration.

Key Terms
فلك الثوابت
القسر
الطبيعي
الفلك الأعظم
الحركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the passage denies the possibility of any two motions in a celestial body.
Correction: The passage only denies two simultaneous contradictory motions; sequential motions are also rejected but for a different reason (interruption of continuity).

Checkpoint
Why is the possibility of two simultaneous motions rejected?
A) It would cause the celestial spheres to collide.
B) It would require the same body to be in two places at once.
C) It contradicts the principle of natural motion.
D) It is not supported by astronomical observation.

Key Takeaway
A celestial body cannot have two simultaneous contradictory motions because that would place it in two locations at once, and sequential motion is impossible due to the requirement of continuity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, are you trying to pursue two contradictory goals at the same time, hoping to be in two different places spiritually? Just as the sphere cannot be simultaneously east and west, you cannot serve two masters. What internal contradiction must you resolve today?
?
The source shows that even the celestial motions must be coherent and consistent. Where in your heart do you accept inconsistency—believing one thing but living another—assuming contradiction is harmless? What step will you take to bring your actions in line with your beliefs before the day ends?
?
The empirical argument from shadows confirms the logical truth. Are you ignoring clear signs in your life that your current direction is impossible, hoping to avoid the logical conclusion? What sign from Allah is pressing on you right now, and what does it demand you change?

The Six Ranks of the Lower World
Al-Razi enumerates the six ranks of consideration in the lower world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six ranks of consideration in the lower world as categorized by al-Razi.

Core Idea
The lower world's order reflects divine wisdom through six ranks.

Al-Razi begins by classifying what we study in the lower world into two main categories: the mothers (elements) and the born ones (creatures). He then lists six specific ranks that form a comprehensive framework for understanding God's wisdom in creation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two categories
Read the opening lines where al-Razi states that what is considered in the lower world is either the mothers (elements) or the born ones (creatures). This sets up the classification.
Mothers refer to the four elements: earth, water, air, fire.
2
Memorize the six ranks
Study the six ranks from upper effects to the anatomy of the human soul. Notice the progression from celestial to human.
3
Connect to the overall purpose
Reflect on why these ranks are mentioned. Al-Razi's aim is to show the effects of God's wisdom throughout creation.
This lesson sets the foundation for the detailed discussion in six books.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أن المعتبر في العالم الأسفل : إما الأمهات ، أو المواليد . فمراتبها
الأول : اعتبار أحوال الآثار العلوية .
والثاني : اعتبار أحوال المعادن .
والثالث : اعتبار أحوال النبات .
والرابع : اعتبار أحوال الحيوان .
والخامس : اعتبار تشريح أبدان الناس .
والسادس : اعتبار تشريح نفس الإنسان .
وتشتمل على هذه المراتب الستة : كتب سنة على الاستيفاء والاستقصاء . وسنستوفي القول فيها في هذا الكتاب إن قدر الله ذلك : فأما اعتبار أحوال الأمهات الأربعة : فنقول : أما الأرض . فقد ثبت في العلوم الرياضية : أن المعمور منها سدسها . وأما الباقي فمغمور في الماء . ثم إن هذا السدس على أربعة أقسام :
أحدها : البحار السبعة . وثانيها : الجبال . وثالثها : المفاوز . ورابعها : العمرانات ، فإذا قسنا أحوال العمرانات ، وجدتها بالنسبة إلى الأقسام الثلاثة الباقية ، كالقطرة في البحر ، ومع ذلك فإن تلك الأقسام الثلاثة : كأنها مخلوقة لمصالح هذا القسم الرابع .
أما البحار فلاجل أن يقدر الإنسان بواسطة ركوب البحر من نقل الأمتعة من بعض جوانب الأرض إلى الجانب الآخر . وأما المفاوز . فلاجل أن الأهوية تتعفن بسبب نفس الحيوانات ، والأبخرة المنفصلة منهم . فلما كان أكثر أهوية العالم هو أهوية المفاوز الخالية ، لا جرم اندفعت العفونات عن الأهوية ، وصلحت لمعيشة الحيوانات. وأما الجبال فإنها تحتقن تحتها البخارات ، وتنفجر منها الأدوية والعيون ، وتنصب إلى المواضع القابلة للعمارات . فهذه الأقسام الثلاثة ، وإن كانت أوسع وأكبر إلا أنها مخلوقة لمصالح هذا القسم الرابع وهو العمرانات .
واعلم أنه كلما كان وقوف الإنسان على آثار حكمة الله تعالى في العالم الأعلى والعالم الأسفل : أكثر ، كان إقراره بأنه الإله الحكيم الرحيم : أتم . وبالله التوفيق
Know: that what is considered in the lower world is either the mothers or the born ones. Their ranks: First: consideration of the states of the upper effects. Second: consideration of the states of minerals. Third: consideration of the states of plants. Fourth: consideration of the states of animals. Fifth: consideration of the anatomy of human bodies. Sixth: consideration of the anatomy of the human soul. Six books covering exhaustively and completely include these six ranks. We will complete the discussion of them in this book, if God enables that. As for the consideration of the states of the four mothers, we say: As for the earth, it has been established in the mathematical sciences that the inhabited part of it is one sixth. As for the rest, it is submerged in water. Then this one sixth is divided into four sections: One of them: the seven seas. Second: the mountains. Third: the deserts. Fourth: the inhabited areas. If we measure the inhabited areas, you find them in relation to the remaining three sections like a drop in the sea. Yet those three sections are as if created for the benefits of this fourth section. As for the seas, it is so that man, by means of sea travel, can transport goods from one side of the earth to the other. As for the deserts, it is because the airs become putrid due to the breath of animals and the vapors separated from them; since most of the air of the world is the air of empty deserts, inevitably the putrefactions are repelled from the airs, and they become suitable for the livelihood of animals. As for the mountains, they store vapors beneath them, and from them burst forth medicines and springs, and they flow down to places suitable for cultivation. So these three sections, even though they are wider and larger, are created for the benefits of this fourth section, which is the inhabited areas. Know that the more a person apprehends the effects of God's wisdom in the upper world and the lower world, the more complete is his acknowledgment that He is the Wise, the Merciful God. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on the effects of divine wisdom in the lower world, focusing on the earth and its divisions. The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, begins by stating that what is considered in the lower world is either the 'mothers' (the four elements: earth, water, air, fire) or the 'born ones' (minerals, plants, animals, and humans). He then lists six ranks of consideration: first, the upper effects (celestial phenomena); second, minerals; third, plants; fourth, animals; fifth, human anatomy; sixth, human soul anatomy. He notes that six comprehensive books cover these ranks, and he will discuss them fully in this book. He then turns to the consideration of the four mothers, specifically the earth. He cites mathematical sciences establishing that only one-sixth of the earth is inhabited; the rest is submerged. That one-sixth is divided into four sections: seas, mountains, deserts, and inhabited areas. The inhabited areas are minuscule compared to the other three, yet those three are created for the benefit of the inhabited fourth. He explains each: seas enable maritime transport of goods; deserts purify the air from putrefaction caused by animal breath and vapors, as most air is over empty deserts; mountains store vapors underground and release springs and minerals for cultivation. Thus, though larger, they serve the inhabited part. He concludes that the more one perceives these effects of divine wisdom, the more one acknowledges God as the Wise and Merciful.

Key Terms
أمهات (mothers/elements)
مواليد (born ones/creatures)
مراتب (ranks)
آثار علوية (upper effects)
معادن (minerals)
نبات (plants)
حيوان (animals)
تشريح (anatomy)
نفس (soul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Consideration of the states of animals
B) Consideration of the anatomy of human bodies
C) Consideration of the anatomy of the human soul
D) Consideration of the states of minerals

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi provides a structured framework of six ranks to study the lower world, demonstrating order and purpose in creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you see the order in your own existence—your physical body, your thoughts, your soul—as part of a deliberate divine plan, or do you take it for granted? What would change if you truly saw each rank as a sign from Allah?
?
Al-Razi moves from the celestial to the mineral, then to plants, animals, your body, and finally your soul. Have you ever paused to reflect that the same God who arranged the stars also arranged every cell in your body? How does that reshape your sense of purpose today?
?
If the highest rank is the anatomy of your own soul, are you actively studying it—your intentions, your character, your connection to Allah—or have you neglected it for lower ranks? What is one step you will take this week to examine your soul?

The Wisdom of the Earth's Divisions
Al-Razi explains the earth's four divisions and their divine purposes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the wisdom behind the earth's four divisions—seas, mountains, deserts, and inhabited areas—as explained by al-Razi.

Core Idea
Seas, mountains, and deserts serve the inhabited areas, showing divine design.

After outlining the six ranks, al-Razi focuses on one of the four mothers—the earth. He explains that only one-sixth of the earth is inhabited, and that the other three sections (seas, mountains, deserts) are created for the benefit of that small inhabited portion.

Guided Path
1
Learn the earth's four divisions
Read where al-Razi states that the inhabited portion of earth is one-sixth, and it is divided into seas, mountains, deserts, and inhabited areas.
The inhabited areas are minuscule compared to the others.
2
Understand the purpose of each division
Study al-Razi's explanations for seas (transport), deserts (air purification), and mountains (water and medicine storage).
3
Connect the parts to the whole
Reflect on how the larger, seemingly useless divisions actually serve the small inhabited parts, proving God's wisdom.
This leads to the conclusion about acknowledging the Wise and Merciful God.

?

?

Where is your heart right now—is it growing in that acknowledgment, or have you become numb to the obvious design around you? ?
You live in a tiny inhabited fraction of the earth, yet God engineered the entire planet for your sake. Do you live as if you are the center of a loving Creator's attention, or do you act as though your existence is random and unsupported? If you truly believed that mountains store water just for you, how would you treat the water you drink today?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Varying Speeds in One Celestial Sphere as Proof of Divine Design
Demonstrates that varying speeds in a single sphere require a wise designer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the infinite gradations of motion within a single celestial sphere serve as a rational proof that the universe is ordained by a wise and powerful Creator.

Core Idea
Infinite speeds in one sphere demand an infinitely wise Ordainer.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a profound argument from the design of the heavens. The author notes that in a single rotating sphere, from the stationary poles to the fast-moving equator, there are infinite points with distinct speeds. Since these points are intrinsically similar, their differentiation cannot arise from themselves—it must be by the precise ordination of Allah, the Almighty, the All-Knowing. You are about to read a classic kalam argument that uses celestial mechanics to prove divine existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once through to get the flow. Then read the English translation to ensure comprehension.
Pay attention to the key terms: قطبا الكرة, منطقة, فلك الثوابت.
2
Identify the three key observations
The author mentions three zones: the stationary poles, the fast equator, and intermediate points. Notice how each is described in terms of speed.
3
Understand the infinite gradations
Since points are infinite, the speeds are infinitely graded. Reflect on why this is significant: it shows the complexity within a single sphere.
The example of the fixed stars' slow motion and the tiny circle taking 36,000 years illustrates the scale.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that this intricate specification must be by divine ordination. Ask yourself: why can't this be by chance or nature?
The author says all parts are 'entirely similar' yet have different motions—this points to an external designer.
5
Connect to broader kalam
This argument is a form of the teleological argument (design). It shows that even within one celestial sphere, there is evidence of intentional design.

وبعضها بطيئة. بل نقول: هذا المعنى حاصل في الفلك الواحد، فإنه إذا استدار الفلك، فكل نقطة تفرض في سطحه فقد استدارت أيضاً. إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول: أما قطبا الكرة، فهما ساكنان. وأما منطقتها فهي متحركة على أسرع الوجوه، وكل نقطة هي أقرب إلى المنطقة، فحركتها أسرع من حركة النقطة التي هي أبعد من المنطقة. وإذا كانت تلك النقط غير متناهية، وجب أن يقال: إنه لا نهاية لمراتب الحركات الواقعة في الكرة الواحدة، بسبب السرعة والبطء. فحركة فلك الثوابت، وإن كانت بطيئة جداً إلا أن الدائرة الصغيرة جداً، المستديرة حول قطبها، إنما تتم حركتها في مدة سنة وثلاثين ألف سنة، ومدار تلك الدائرة أصغر من مدار قشر الجاورسة [؟] الواحدة. والكرة الواحدة افترض فيها هذه الحركات المختلفة، بسبب البطء والسرعة، وتلك النقط والأجزاء بأسرها متشابهة. فاختصاص كل واحد منها بحركته الخاصة، وبمقداره الخاص في البطء والسرعة، لا بد وأن يكون بتقدير العزيز العليم.
And some of them are slow. Rather we say: this meaning occurs in a single sphere, for when the sphere rotates, every point assumed on its surface has also rotated. If you know this, we say: As for the two poles of the sphere, they are stationary. As for its equator, it is moving in the fastest manner, and every point that is closer to the equator, its motion is faster than the motion of a point that is farther from the equator. And if those points are infinite, it must be said that there is no limit to the ranks of motions occurring in a single sphere, due to speed and slowness. The motion of the sphere of the fixed stars, although it is very slow, yet the very small circle revolving around its pole completes its motion in a period of thirty-six thousand years, and the orbit of that circle is smaller than the orbit of one shell of [the sphere of] Jupiter [?]. In a single sphere, these different motions are assumed due to slowness and speed, even though those points and parts are entirely similar. So the specification of each one with its own motion and its own measure of slowness and speed cannot be except by the ordination of the Almighty, the All-Knowing.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the intricate variations of motion within a single celestial sphere necessitate a wise and powerful ordainer (al-ʿazīz al-ʿalīm). The author begins by affirming that diverse speeds exist in one sphere: the poles are stationary, the equator moves fastest, and intermediate points have intermediate speeds. Since the points on the sphere's surface are infinite, the ranks of motion are infinite. He then gives an example: the sphere of fixed stars rotates very slowly, yet a tiny circle near its pole takes 36,000 years to complete one revolution—its orbit is even smaller than the orbit of Jupiter's shell. Despite all these points and parts being similar in substance, each has a distinct speed. This differentiation cannot be self-caused; it must be by the precise ordination (taqdīr) of God, who is Almighty and All-Knowing. The argument is a classic kalam proof from the design of the heavens.

Misconception: The argument is about the number of spheres or the fact that there are multiple celestial spheres with different motions.
Correction: The argument focuses on a single sphere and the infinite gradations of motion within it, not on multiple spheres.

Checkpoint
What does the author use the varying speeds of points on one celestial sphere to demonstrate?
A) The sphere is eternal and self-moving.
B) The motion is caused by the sphere's own nature.
C) The differentiation must be by divine ordination.
D) The speeds are random.

Key Takeaway
The infinite gradations of motion within a single celestial sphere demonstrate that the universe's intricate design cannot be self-caused but requires the ordination of an All-Wise, All-Powerful Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage argues that the parts of the sphere are identical in substance yet differ in motion, forcing the conclusion that a Wise Ordainer specified each. If your own soul has varying states—peace, anxiety, faith, doubt—while remaining one person, does this not also point to a Designer who ordains your inner motions? Will you turn to Him to order your heart?
?
If the infinite ranks of speed in a single sphere demand an All-Knowing Creator, then the infinite variety of experiences in your life — each with its own pace and purpose — cannot be random. Are you living as if your circumstances are under His precise design, or do you resist His ordination when the speed of your life feels too slow or too fast?

Objection from Natural Causes and First Answer: Celestial Movements
Razi presents an objection from natural causes and answers by arguing against infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the naturalistic objection by showing that celestial movements themselves require a cause, leading to the impossibility of infinite regress and the affirmation of God.

Core Idea
Infinite regress forces us to acknowledge the First Mover, the free-choosing God.

In this passage, Razi addresses a naturalistic objection: why not attribute plant growth to the seasons and celestial motions? He shows that even if we grant that, the celestial motions themselves must be caused. This leads to a dilemma: either infinite regress (impossible) or a Creator. Thus, the very argument intended to remove God ends up affirming Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the flow: objection, need for answer, the first answer with its two branches, and the Quranic conclusion.
Pay attention to the verse endings (يَتَفَكَّرُونَ and يَعْقِلُونَ).
2
Understand the objection
The objector asks: why not attribute the growth of plants to natural causes like the seasons and the movements of the sun and moon? This challenges the teleological argument from plants.
3
Follow the first answer
Razi grants the premise for the sake of argument: changes in the lower world are connected to celestial movements. Then he asks: what caused those movements themselves?
This is a key move: pushing the causal question one step back.
4
Recognize the infinite regress argument
If celestial movements are caused by other spheres, an infinite regress (tasalsul) follows, which is rationally impossible. If they are caused by the wise Creator, then God is established as the first cause.
The argument ends the regress at God.
5
Reflect on the Quranic connection
Razi cites the verses about the subjection of celestial bodies and notes that they end with 'who reason' because rational understanding of the impossibility of infinite regress leads to the conclusion of a single free-choosing Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمسائل أن يسأل فيقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المؤثر فيه : طبائع الفصول ، وحركات الشمس والقمر ؟ ولما كان الدليل لا يتم إلا بالجواب عن هذا السؤال ، لاجرم كان مجال الفكر والنظر والتأمل باقيا . فلهذا جعل مقطع هذه الآية : قوله : ( يتفكرون ) ثم إنه تعالى : أجاب عن هذا السؤال من وجهين : الأول : هب أن تغيرات العالم الأسفل، مربوطة بأحوال حركات الكواكب والأفلاك. فتلك الحركات كيف حصلت ؟ فإن كان حصولها بسبب أفلاك أخرى لزم التسلسل ، وإن كان من الخالق الحكيم ، فذلك يوجب الإقرار بوجود الإله تعالى وهذا هو المراد بقوله تعالى : ( وسخر لكم الليل والنهار والشمس والقمر والنجوم مسخرات بأمره . إن في ذلك لآيات لقوم يعقلون ) فجعل مقطع هذه الآية قوله ( لقوم يعقلون ) والتقدير : كأنه قيل : إن كنت عاقلاً فاعلم أن التسلسل باطل . فوجب انتهاء الحركات إلى حركة يكون موجدها غير متحرك . وذلك هو الإله القادر المختار . ولما تم الدليل في هذا المقام ، لا جرم جعل مقطعه قوله : ( إن في ذلك لآيات لقوم يعقلون )
And the questioner may ask, saying: Why may it not be that the efficient cause (mu'aththir) in it is the natures of the seasons and the movements of the sun and moon? And since the proof is not complete except by answering this question, inevitably the domain of thought, reflection, and contemplation remained. Therefore He made the concluding part of this verse His saying: 'who reflect.' Then He, exalted, answered this question from two aspects: First: Suppose that the changes of the lower world are connected to the states of the movements of the planets and celestial spheres. Then those movements, how did they occur? If their occurrence was due to other celestial spheres, an infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow, and if from the wise Creator, then that necessitates acknowledgment of the existence of God, exalted. This is the intended meaning of His saying: 'And He subjected for you the night, day, sun, and moon, and the stars are subjected by His command. Indeed in that are signs for a people who reason.' So He made the concluding part of this verse His saying: 'who reason.' The estimate: It is as if it is said: If you are rational, then know that infinite regress is invalid. So motions must terminate in a motion whose maker is non-moving. That is the powerful, free-choosing God. And when the proof was completed in this context, He consequently made its concluding part His saying: 'Indeed in that are signs for a people who reason.'
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the first part of the answer to it. The objector (al-sa'il) asks why the growth of plants could not be attributed to natural causes, specifically the natures of the seasons and the movements of the sun and moon. The author notes that the proof from plants would not be complete without answering this objection, which is why the verse ends with 'who reflect'—the matter requires thought. God then answers in two ways. The first answer assumes, for argument's sake, that changes in the lower world are connected to celestial movements. The author then asks: how did those celestial movements themselves occur? If they are caused by other spheres, an infinite regress (tasalsul) results, which is impossible. If they are caused by the wise Creator, then that leads to the affirmation of God. This is the meaning of the verse about the subjection of the night, day, sun, moon, and stars. The verse ends with 'who reason' (ya'qilun) because a rational person knows infinite regress is false, and thus motion must end with a first mover who is not itself moved—that is the powerful, free-choosing God.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Islamic theology denies any causality in nature and attributes everything directly to God.
Correction: Islamic theology, as represented by Razi, does not deny natural causality entirely. It accepts secondary causes like seasons and celestial movements, but argues that these causes themselves depend on God and that an infinite regress of causes is impossible, leading to a First Cause.

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress argument shows that even if we attribute natural effects to intermediate causes, those causes themselves require a First Cause that is not itself caused—the powerful, free-choosing God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You just learned that an infinite regress is invalid—that every chain of causes must end in a First Cause that is not itself moving. But in your own life, where are you relying on 'secondary causes'—people, systems, your own efforts—without ultimately entrusting the outcome to that First Cause? What would it look like to genuinely depend on God as the true Mover behind every means?
?
The passage ends by linking rationality with recognizing that infinite regress is impossible and that God is the First Mover. Are you truly using your reason to follow the argument to its conclusion, or do you sometimes intellectualize just enough to avoid the personal surrender that this truth demands? What would it mean for you to be a person who truly 'reasons' in this way?

Diversity in Nature Proves a Free Agent
Razi uses a rose leaf example to argue for a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observed diversity in a single object (like a two-colored rose leaf) proves that the cause of nature is a free chooser, not a necessitating essence.

Core Idea
Uniform causes cannot produce diverse effects; therefore, diversity demands a free creator.

Razi presents a powerful argument from everyday observation. Using the simple example of a rose leaf with one side red and the other black, he shows that if nature were a blind, necessitating force, it would produce identical results everywhere. Since we see variation, the ultimate cause must be a free agent who chooses to create diversity. This passage grounds a key theological proof in the natural world.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Recognize that planets and natural forces relate equally to all parts of a single leaf or grain. Their influence is uniform in principle.
2
Observe the fact
Consider the rose leaf: one side is intensely red, the other intensely black. This is a clear, observable difference within the same object.
The example is chosen for its stark contrast.
3
Apply the rational principle
Recall the rational axiom: a necessitating cause (mujib bi al-dhat) produces uniform effects. It cannot produce variation under identical conditions.
This principle is established in the intellect.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since we observe variation, the cause cannot be a necessitating nature. It must be a powerful free chooser (fa'il mukhtar) who creates diversity intentionally.
5
Connect to the Quranic verse
Reflect on the verse ending with 'who are mindful' (yadhakkarun). The verse prompts us to remember what reason already knows: that diversity points to a free agent.
Razi notes this is the intended meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني من الجواب : هو أن نسبة الكواكب والطبائع إلى جميع أجزاء الورقة الواحدة ، والحبة الواحدة : واحدة . ثم إنا نرى الورقة الواحدة من الورد: أحد وجهيها في غاية الحمرة، والوجه الثاني في غاية السواد . فلو كان المؤثر موجباً بالذات لامتنع حصول هذا التفاوت ، لأنه تقرر في العقول : أن تأثير الموجب بالذات لا يختلف . وحيث حصل التفاوت في الآثار ، أن المؤثر قادر مختار . وهذا هو المراد من قوله : ( وما ذرأ لكم في الأرض مختلفاً ألوانه . إن في ذلك لآية لقوم يذكرون ) كأنه قيل له : تذكر ما ترسخ في عقلك . أن الموجب بالذات والطبع لا يختلف تأثيره. فلما رأيت بعينك حصول هذا الاختلاف ، فاعرف أن المؤثر فيه ليس هو الطبائع الموجبة بالذات ، بل المؤثر فيه هو الفاعل المختار . فلهذا السبب جعل مقطع هذه الآية : قوله : ( لقوم يذكرون ) ولنكتف من هذا الجنس بهذا القدر ، فإنه بحر واسع . وقد ذكرناه على سبيل الاستقصاء في ( التفسير الكبير فمن أراد هذا النوع فعليه بذلك الكتاب . والله أعلم بالصواب
And the second aspect of the answer: that the relation of the planets and natures to all parts of a single leaf and a single grain is one and the same. Yet we see a single leaf of a rose: one of its two sides is extremely red, and the other side is extremely black. So if the efficient cause were necessitating by essence (mujib bi al-dhat), this differentiation would be impossible, because it is established in intellects that the effect of the necessitator by essence does not differ. And when differentiation occurs in effects, the efficient cause is a powerful free chooser. This is the intended meaning of His saying: 'And what He has scattered for you in the earth of different colors. Indeed in that is a sign for a people who are mindful.' It is as if it is said to him: Remember what is rooted in your intellect: that the necessitator by essence and nature does not have differing effects. So when you witnessed with your own eyes the occurrence of this difference, know that the efficient cause of it is not the necessitating natures, but the efficient cause is the free agent. For this reason, He made the concluding part of this verse His saying: 'who are mindful.' And let us suffice from this type with this amount, for it is a vast sea. And we have mentioned it in detail in al-Tafsir al-Kabir. So whoever wants this type, let him refer to that book. And God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage presents the second answer to the objection. The author argues that the planets and natural forces relate equally to all parts of a single leaf or grain; their influence is uniform. Yet we observe a rose leaf where one side is intensely red and the other intensely black. If the cause were a necessitator by essence (mujib bi al-dhat) – that is, a cause that produces its effect necessarily and uniformly – such difference would be impossible, because reason affirms that the effect of a necessitator does not vary. Since we see variation, the cause must be a powerful free chooser (qadir mukhtar). This is the meaning of the verse 'And what He has scattered for you in the earth of different colors' – the verse ends with 'who are mindful' (yadhakkarun) because it reminds one of what reason already knows: that a necessitating nature cannot produce diversity. The author then concludes by noting that this topic is vast and refers readers to his Tafsir al-Kabir for more detail.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The example disproves only necessitarian causality (mujib bi al-dhat). It does not deny that natural causes exist, but shows that the ultimate cause is a free agent who can produce diverse effects from a uniform substrate.

Key Takeaway
The diversity in creation is a sign that the creator is a free agent, not a blind necessity.

?

?
The verse ends with 'for a people who are mindful.' Mindfulness here is active remembrance of a rational truth. Are you letting your intellect be guided by observation to see God's free choice, or do you allow abstract philosophies to cloud that truth? Take five minutes to look at a single leaf on a tree and recite this proof to yourself.

Four Divisions of Views on the World's Agent
The author classifies views on the world's agent into four exclusive divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four exclusive divisions of views regarding the agent of the corporeal world and the logical reasoning that makes them exhaustive.

Core Idea
All views about the world's agent fall into just four logical possibilities.

The author begins with observable phenomena—the changing seasons due to the sun—to ground the discussion. From this, the intellect ascends to consider the unseen cause of the world. He then presents a classification of all possible positions on the world's agent, demonstrating that only four are logically possible. This lesson introduces that classification and its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the initial observation
Read the Arabic and translation, paying attention to how the author uses sensory experience (seasons changing with the sun) as a starting point for rational inquiry about the unseen.
The author emphasizes that we 'see with our eyes' the changes, then the intellect rises to consider what is not seen.
2
Identify the binary splits leading to four divisions
Trace the logical tree: Does the world have an agent? If yes, is the agent necessitating or volitional? If volitional, does it consider human interests or not? These binary choices produce exactly four possibilities.
3
Understand each division's meaning
Review each division: (1) No agent, (2) Necessitating agent, (3) Volitional agent that considers interests, (4) Volitional agent that disregards interests. Relate each to real-world philosophical or theological positions.
The passage explicitly names these four and says there is no fifth.
4
Appreciate the exhaustiveness claim
Consider why the author says 'no more beyond these four'. Reflect on how the logical alternatives cover every possibility, leaving no room for a fifth view.
The commentary explains the logical derivation in detail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول
اتاك المذاهب بحسب ا سيم
اعلم : أنا نشاهد بأبصارنا هذا العالم العنصري ، ونشاهد أيضاً : أن أحوال هذا العالم تتغير عند تغير أحوال الشمس في القرب من سمت الرأس ، وفي البعد منه . فإن بسبب حركة الشمس تحدث الفصول الأربعة . وبسبب حدوثها تتغير أحوال هذا العالم. فهذا المقدار محسوس ، ثم عند هذا ترقى العقل واعتبر أحوالاً غائبة عن الحس، وذلك الاعتبار هو أنه يقال : هذا العالم الجسماني . إما أن لا يكون له مؤثر ، أو يقال : له مؤثر موجب بالذات . أو يقال له : مؤثر فاعل بالاختيار ، ولا يفعل إلا ما يوافق مصالح الناس ، أو يقال : له مؤثر فاعل مختار ، ولا يبالي بمصالح الناس ، فهذه أقسام أربعة . لا مزيد عليها . لأن هذا العالم، إما أن لا يكون له مؤثر وهو القسم الأول . أو يكون له مؤثر وذلك المؤثر إما موجب وهو القسم الثاني . أو مختار ، وذلك المختار إما أن يراعي مصالح الخلق وهو القسم الثالث . أو لا يلتفت إليها وهو القسم الرابع . فهذا مذهب أهل العلم (۱) في معرفة الفعل والفاعل ولا مزيد على هذه الأربعة
أما القسم الأول : وهو قول من ينفي المؤثر أصلا ، فلا أعرف في الدنيا
Chapter One
[?] the schools according to [?]
Know: We see with our eyes this elemental world, and we also see that the states of this world change upon the change of the sun's states in proximity to the zenith and in distance from it. For due to the motion of the sun, the four seasons occur, and due to their occurrence the states of this world change. This much is sensed. Then, after this, the intellect ascends and considers states absent from sense, and that consideration is that it is said: This corporeal world either has no agent, or it is said: It has an agent that is necessitating by essence, or it is said: It has an agent that acts by choice and does not do except what agrees with the interests of people, or it is said: It has an agent that acts by choice and does not care about the interests of people. These are four divisions, no more. Because this world either has no agent—that is the first division—or it has an agent, and that agent is either necessitating—that is the second division—or it is choosing, and that which is choosing either considers the interests of creation—that is the third division—or pays no attention to them—that is the fourth division. This is the way of the people of knowledge (1) concerning knowledge of act and agent, and there is no more beyond these four. As for the first division: It is the saying of one who denies the agent altogether, and I know not in the world...
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage classifies all possible views regarding the agent of the corporeal world into four mutually exclusive and exhaustive divisions. The author begins by pointing to observable phenomena: we see the elemental world and its changes due to the sun's motion (seasons). This is the starting point for intellectual reasoning about unseen realities. The intellect then considers the world as a whole and asks about its agent. The classification proceeds as follows: First possibility: the world has no agent (atheism). Second: it has an agent that is necessitating by essence (like a cause that produces its effect necessarily). Third: it has an agent that acts by choice but only in accordance with human interests (this could refer to certain philosophical or religious views that hold God's actions are bound by wisdom/welfare). Fourth: it has an agent that acts by choice but does not regard human interests (this could be the Ash'ari view of absolute divine will). The author emphasizes that these four are exhaustive—there is no fifth division—because the logical alternatives are: either there is an agent or not; if there is, it is either necessitating or volitional; if volitional, it either considers or disregards created interests. At the end, the author identifies this classification as the approach of the people of knowledge. The first division (denial of any agent) is then introduced but the explanation is cut off in the available text.

Key Terms
عالم عنصري
مؤثر
موجب بالذات
فاعل بالاختيار
مصالح الناس
أقسام أربعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think there are five or more possible views on the world's agent (e.g., multiple gods, no god but with exceptions, etc.)
Correction: The author shows that any view must fall into one of four categories based on logical splits: existence of an agent, its mode (necessity or will), and its regard for interests. These are exhaustive.

Checkpoint

B) Four
C) Five
D) Two

Key Takeaway
The classification of views on the world's agent into four exclusive and exhaustive divisions is a foundational tool for theological discourse, derived from logical reasoning based on sensory observation.

The Philosophers' Proof for a Necessary Efficient Cause
Razi presents the full argument for a necessary efficient cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical steps that led the majority of philosophers to affirm a necessary efficient cause for the world.

Core Idea
The world's contingency demands a cause that is both non-body and non-choosing.

Razi now presents the second group of thinkers: those who accept an efficient cause for the world but deny that it acts by choice. Their proof runs through several premises about bodies, contingency, and causality. This lesson traces their argument step by step, showing why they ended up with a necessary-by-essence cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the argument is laid out in a chain of reasoning.
Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'fa' and 'thumma'.
2
Identify the premises
List the four main claims: the world is a body, every body is contingent, every contingent needs a cause, and the cause cannot be a body.
Also note the additional premise that choice-based causation is inconceivable.
3
Understand the conclusion
See how the negation of choice leads to the conclusion that the cause is necessary by essence, and that the world is necessary through another.
Consider why the philosophers identify this as the doctrine of Aristotle's followers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني : وهم الذين يثبتون [ المؤثر (4) ] الموجب بالذات، فهؤلاء هم الذين قالوا : هذا العالم جسم، وكل جسم فإنه ممكن لذاته ، بحسب ذاته ، وبحسب جميع أجزاء ماهيته. وكل ممكن فلا بد له مؤثر . والمؤثر في جميع الأجسام ، يمتنع أن يكون جسماً ، ثم قالوا : المؤثر على سبيل الاختيار : غير معقول، بناء على الشبهات التي ذكرناها . فثبت : أن ذلك المؤثر موجب بالذات ، فهذا العالم ممكن الوجود لذاته ، واجب الوجود بوجوب سببه وعلته . وهذا مذهب جمهور الفلاسفة ، الذين هم أتباع و أرسطاطاليس ) فإن هذا القول هو الذي اختاره هذا الرجل، ونصره [ أصحابه (۱) ] وأتباعه من المتقدمين والمتأخرين
As for the second division: they are those who affirm the [efficient cause (4)] that is necessary by essence. These are those who said: this world is a body, and every body is contingent in itself, in its essence, and in all parts of its quiddity. And every contingent necessarily has an efficient cause. And the efficient cause of all bodies cannot be a body. Then they said: an efficient cause by way of choice is inconceivable, based on the doubts we mentioned. So it is established that that efficient cause is necessary by essence. Thus this world is contingent in existence in itself, and necessary in existence due to the necessity of its cause and reason. And this is the doctrine of the majority of philosophers, who are followers of Aristotle, for this saying is what this man chose and supported, [his companions (1)] and his followers among the earlier and later ones.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage presents the view of the second division: those who affirm an efficient cause but hold it to be necessary by essence (al-mu'aththir al-mujib bi al-dhat). The argument proceeds as follows: 

1. The world is a body. 
2. Every body is contingent (mumkin) in itself, in its essence, and in all parts of its quiddity (mahiyya). That means its existence is not necessary from itself. 
3. Every contingent requires an efficient cause (mu'aththir) to bring it into existence. 
4. The efficient cause of all bodies cannot itself be a body. This is because if it were a body, it would also be contingent and require a further cause, leading to an infinite regress. 
5. Moreover, they claim that an efficient cause that acts by choice (ikhtiyar) is inconceivable, based on doubts previously mentioned (presumably arguments that choice implies change or imperfection in the agent). 
6. Therefore, the efficient cause must be necessary by essence (mujib bi al-dhat)—meaning it necessitates its effect by its very nature, without choice or deliberation. 
7. Consequently, the world is contingent in itself (possible of existence) but necessary through its cause (wajib al-wujud bi wujub sababihi wa 'illatihi). That is, given the necessary cause, the world necessarily exists as its effect. 

This is identified as the doctrine of the majority of philosophers, especially the followers of Aristotle, who supported this view.

Key Terms
مؤثر
موجب بالذات
ممكن
واجب بالغير
جسم
شبهات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that affirming a necessary efficient cause implies pantheism or that the cause is identical to the world.
Correction: The philosophers maintain that the cause is distinct from its effect; the world is contingent in itself and necessary only through its cause.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why can the efficient cause of all bodies not be a body?
A) Because bodies are eternal
B) Because then it would also be contingent and require a cause
C) Because bodies are composed of atoms
D) Because the cause must be material

Key Takeaway
The philosophers conclude that the world has a necessary efficient cause that is not a body and does not act by choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every contingent thing, including your own existence, is dependent on a cause that acts by necessity, what does that say about your own choices? Do you truly have free will, or are you just part of an inevitable chain? How does this challenge your sense of responsibility?
?
The philosophers claim that an efficient cause by choice is inconceivable. But you experience choice every day. Is your experience of free will an illusion, or is there a different kind of cause at work? Stand before the mirror and ask: 'Who or what is the real cause of my actions?'
?
The argument concludes that the world is necessary through its cause. If that cause is necessary by essence, then your existence is not an accident—it is a necessary consequence. Does this fill you with a sense of purpose or dread? How will you live today knowing that you are not self-sufficient but utterly dependent?

Five Views on Action and Agent
Classification of five opinions on action and agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five distinct philosophical positions on action and agent that emerged from classical Islamic thought, ranging from materialism to emanationism.

Core Idea
Five views on action and agent: from material necessity to divine emanation.

This passage summarizes the key opinions on causation and agency that arose from earlier classifications. By examining these five views—from Democritus's atoms to Neoplatonic emanation—you will see how Islamic theologians mapped the spectrum of causal theories. Each view represents a different answer to the question: How does action proceed from an agent?

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the views
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify each of the five opinions listed, noting their distinctive claims.
The views are clearly numbered in the text.
2
Notice the underlying principle
Observe how the views differ on whether causation is necessary or contingent, and whether the cause is material or immaterial.
The first two views posit inherent necessity; the last two affirm a necessary cause; the third denies any cause.
3
Compare the fourth and fifth views
Both affirm a necessary efficient cause, but the fourth says only one effect emanates first, while the fifth says all possibles emanate simultaneously.
This distinction reflects different emanationist theories.
4
Reflect on theological relevance
Consider why these classifications matter for Islamic theology: they frame debates about divine will, power, and causality.
This connects to broader discussions on God's relation to creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد حصل لنا من هذا التقسيم في الفعل والفاعل أنواع من المقالات: فالأول: قول الدهرية القائلين بوجوب السموات في ذواتها وفي صفاتها. والقول الثاني: قول ديمقراطيس الذي قال: أصل العالم أجزاء كرية صلبة متحركة بذواتها. والقول الثالث: قول من يقول: إنه لا يمتنع في العقل حدوث الحوادث لا لمؤثر أصلاً. والقول الرابع: قول من أثبت لهذا العالم مؤثراً موجباً بالذات، وزعم: أن الصادر الأول منه يجب أن يكون واحداً فقط. والقول الخامس: قول من يقول: مؤثر العالم موجب بالذات، إلا أنه علة لجميع الممكنات دفعة واحدة على الترتيب الممكن منها.
So there has resulted for us from this division concerning action and agent types of opinions: The first: the saying of the Dahriyya (materialists) who affirm the necessity of the heavens in their essences and attributes. The second saying: the saying of Democritus who said: The origin of the world is spherical solid particles moving by themselves. The third saying: the saying of one who says: It is not impossible in reason for events to occur without any cause at all. The fourth saying: the saying of one who affirmed for this world an efficient cause that is necessary by essence, and claimed: the first emanation from it must be one only. The fifth saying: the saying of one who says: The efficient cause of the world is necessary by essence, except that it is a cause for all possibles at once in the possible order from them.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage presents a summary of five distinct opinions on action and agent that emerge from the previous classifications. The first is that of the Dahriyya (materialists) who assert the necessity and eternity of the heavens. The second is that of Democritus, who posits atoms (spherical solid particles) moving by themselves. The third opinion is that it is rationally possible for events to occur without any cause (occasionalism or radical contingency). The fourth opinion affirms a necessary efficient cause that produces only one first effect (Neoplatonic emanation). The fifth opinion also affirms a necessary cause but holds that it produces all possibles simultaneously in a possible order. This enumeration shows the breadth of philosophical and theological positions on causation and the origin of the world.

علة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third view (events without cause) is the same as the Mujbira view.
Correction: It is distinct because the Mujbira view affirms a divine cause, while the third view denies any cause at all.

Five Proofs Against Self-Motion in Atoms
Philosophers' five proofs that a body cannot be self-moving.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five rational proofs used by Islamic philosophers to refute Democritus' claim that atoms are self-moving.

Core Idea
A body cannot cause its own motion because motion requires change and a cause external to the changing thing.

Democritus argued that atoms are necessary in themselves and self-moving, producing the cosmos through random collisions. The philosophers countered with five logical arguments showing that no body can be the cause of its own motion. These proofs rely on fundamental principles of causation, the nature of change, and the relationship between parts and whole.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of Democritus' view and the philosophers' refutation.
Notice the structured enumeration of proofs.
2
Understand the two general objections
The passage first states two overarching objections: a body cannot be necessary in itself, and it cannot be self-moving. Focus on the second objection and its five proofs.
3
Examine Proofs 1-3
Work through the first three proofs: (1) change vs. permanence, (2) corporeality equality, (3) uniform motion direction. Understand the logic of each.
These proofs attack the idea that corporeality itself causes motion.
4
Examine Proofs 4-5
Study the fourth proof (agent vs. patient) and the fifth proof (motion depends on parts). Note how they show self-motion is impossible.
The fourth proof is about the same thing cannot be both cause and receiver.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
After reviewing all proofs, consider why the philosophers conclude that a body cannot be self-moving. This refutes Democritus' atomism.
The conclusion is that motion requires an external mover.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المذهب الثاني : وهو قول ( ديمقراطيس ، بأن الأجزاء واجبة الوجود لذواتها، وهي متحركة لذواتها، فحصل من حركاتها المضطربة هذه الأفلاك

فاعلم أن الفلاسفة أبطلوا هذا المذهب من وجوه : الأول : ما سبق أن الجسم لا يكون واجب الوجود لذاته

والثاني : إن الجسم يمتنع أن يكون متحركاً لذاته

واحتجوا عليه بوجوه :

الأول : إن الجسم غير متغير، والحركة عبارة عن نفس التغير . والباقي لا يكون علة للمتغير .

والثاني : إن الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية . فلو كانت الجسمية علة للحركة ، لوجب أن يكون كل جسم متحركاً بالذات

والثالث : إن الجسمية لما كانت علة للحركة ، وجب أن تكون جميع حركات الأجسام إلى حيز واحد [ فيلزم اجتماع الأجسام الكثيرة في الحيز الواحد (١) ]

والرابع : إن الجسم قابل للحركة ، فيمتنع أن يكون مؤثراً فيها ، لأن الواحد بالنسبة [ إلى الواحد (۲) ] لا يكون قابلا وفاعلا معاً .

الخامس : إن حركة الجسم ، موقوفة على حركة كل واحد من أجزائه . وما كان موقوفاً على الغير ، لا يكون واجباً [ بالذات (۳) ]

فهذه هي الوجوه التي عولوا عليها في بيان أن الجسم يمتنع أن يكون متحركاً لذاته
As for the second school – which is the statement of Democritus that the atoms are necessary in existence in themselves, and they are self-moving, and from their chaotic movements resulted these celestial spheres – know that the philosophers refuted this school in several ways: First: What preceded, that a body cannot be necessary in existence in itself. Second: That a body cannot be self-moving. And they brought proofs for it: First: The body is unchangeable, and movement is change itself. That which remains cannot be a cause of that which changes. Second: Bodies are equal in corporeality. So if corporeality were a cause of motion, then every body would necessarily be self-moving. Third: Since corporeality is a cause of motion, all motions of bodies would have to be toward one location, which would necessitate the aggregation of many bodies in one location. Fourth: The body is receptive of motion, so it is impossible for it to be effective in it, because the one thing, in relation to one thing, cannot be both receptive and effective simultaneously. Fifth: The motion of a body depends on the motion of each one of its parts. That which depends on something else cannot be necessary [in itself]. These are the aspects they relied on in demonstrating that a body cannot be self-moving.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage presents Democritus' doctrine that atoms are self-necessary and self-moving, and that random atomic motions produced the cosmos. The philosophers refute it with two general objections: (1) based on earlier proofs, a body cannot be necessary; (2) a body cannot be self-moving. The second objection is supported by five specific proofs. Proof 1: The body is inherently unchangeable (since it is permanent), while movement is change; a permanent thing cannot cause change. Proof 2: All bodies share corporeality equally; if corporeality were the cause of motion, then every body would be self-moving, which is false (some bodies are at rest). Proof 3: If corporeality caused motion, all motions would be in the same direction, leading to all bodies converging in one place, which does not happen. Proof 4: A body is receptive of motion (it can be moved), so it cannot be the agent of motion; the same thing cannot be both patient and agent in the same respect. Proof 5: Motion of a whole body depends on motion of its parts; what depends on another cannot be self-necessary. The conclusion: body cannot be self-moving.

أجزاء
واجب الوجود
متحرك بالذات
تغير
حركة
جسمية
قابل
فاعل
موقوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These proofs are about motion itself, not about whether a body can be the ultimate cause of its own motion.
Correction: The proofs specifically target the idea of a body being self-moving (self-caused motion), not motion in general. They argue that a body cannot be the originator of its own motion because of its nature and dependence on parts.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The five proofs collectively demonstrate that self-motion in a body is logically impossible, thereby refuting Democritus' atomism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If everything that moves depends on something else, what does that mean for your constant inner movements—your desires, plans, worries? Which of these do you need to consciously hand back to the One who alone moves without being moved?

The Teleological Argument Against Democritus
Presents a teleological argument against Democritus: the celestial spheres' perfect order cannot arise by chance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the teleological argument against Democritus' atomism, which holds that the perfect order of the celestial spheres cannot arise by chance, and how theologians refute it based on the origination of bodies.

Core Idea
Perfect order cannot come from random chance.

Democritus claimed that everything, including the heavens, resulted from random atomic collisions. But can chance produce such intricate order? In this third refutation, Sanusi presents a teleological argument: the celestial spheres exhibit perfect attributes, making their emergence by sheer chance intellectually improbable. He then notes that the theologians offer a more direct refutation based on the temporal origination of all bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the argument is presented as the third refutation.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the teleological argument
The passage says the celestial spheres have perfect qualities; therefore, it is intellectually unlikely they arose by chance. Ask: Why does order imply a cause?
This is an argument from design.
3
Consider the theologians' alternative refutation
The author notes that theologians refute Democritus by proving the origination of all bodies. How does this differ from the teleological argument?
The theologians' approach is more direct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثالث في إبطال هذا المذهب : إن هذه الأفلاك مع كونها موصوفة بأكمل الصفات ، وأحسن النعوت اللائقة بالأجسام ، يبعد في العقل حدوثها ووقوعها ، على سبيل الاتفاق. وأما المتكلمون فقد أبطلوا هذا القول أيضاً بالبناء على حدوث الأجسام
The third aspect in refuting this doctrine: These celestial spheres, despite being characterized by the most perfect attributes and the finest qualities befitting bodies, it is intellectually unlikely that they would come into existence and occur by sheer chance. As for the theologians, they also refuted this statement by building on the origination of bodies.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage presents a teleological argument against Democritus' atomism. The argument: the celestial spheres (the heavens) are characterized by the most perfect attributes and qualities appropriate for bodies. Given their perfection and order, it is intellectually improbable that they came into existence and occur by sheer chance (i.e., random atomic collisions). This is an argument from design, implying purpose and a cause. The author then notes that the theologians (mutakallimun) also refuted this view by appealing to the temporal origination of all bodies, which they have already established. Their approach is more direct: all bodies are originated, so a random process cannot account for their existence.

Key Terms
وجه
إبطال
أفلاك
