Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that chance is absolutely impossible.
Correction: The passage states that it is intellectually improbable (yab'ud fi al-'aql), not impossible. Chance is theoretically possible but extremely unlikely given the observed order.

Checkpoint
According to the third refutation of Democritus, what makes it improbable that the celestial spheres arose by chance?
A) They are eternal
B) They exhibit perfect order and attributes
C) They are made of atoms
D) They are self-moving

Key Takeaway
The teleological argument shows that the perfected order of the heavens cannot be attributed to random chance, pointing to a purposeful cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see the perfect order of the heavens every night. Do you live as if your own existence is also the result of purposeful design, or do you act as if it were random? What would change today if you truly embraced that purpose?
?
The passage says it is 'intellectually unlikely' that the heavens arose by chance. Where in your life have you attributed something good to 'luck' rather than to a purposeful Creator? Will you consciously shift that attribution starting now?
?
The theologians built their refutation on the principle that everything is created and contingent. Do you ever feel self-sufficient, as if you are the source of your own abilities or successes? How would acknowledging your complete dependence on the Creator change your humility and gratitude?

Refuting Occasionalism: Events Have Causes
Refutes occasionalism by appealing to observed regularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observed regularity of the universe refutes the claim that events occur without an effective cause.

Core Idea
Randomness never produces the consistent order we see; therefore, events must have a cause.

You have already learned that proving the existence of the Necessary Being (al-Ṣāniʿ) establishes causality. Now we examine a third false doctrine – the claim that events occur without any cause at all. The author reminds us that this has been refuted earlier, but he adds a fresh argument from the regularity we observe in both the celestial and terrestrial worlds. This argument shows that if events were truly accidental, they would not be perpetual or even mostly regular; yet the universe displays a necessary order and a consistent system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the false doctrine
Read the opening sentence and note what the third false doctrine claims: events occur without an effective cause (مؤثر).
This is the doctrine of occasionalism.
2
Understand the argument from regularity
The author says that accidental (اتفاقي) things are neither perpetual (دائم) nor mostly occurring (أكثري). Reflect on why this is a problem for occasionalism.
If events were truly random, they would not exhibit the regularity we see.
3
Connect to observed order
We observe that the higher and lower worlds occur in a necessary order (ترتيب لازم) and a consistent system (نظم). This regularity contradicts pure accident.
Therefore, events must have a cause—ultimately the Necessary Being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المذهب الثالث وهو قول من يقول : الحوادث تحدث لا لمؤثر . فالكلام المستقصى فيه ، قد تقدم في مسألة إثبات [ الصانع (٥)] واجب الوجود لذاته. وأيضاً : فكل ما كان اتفاقياً، فإنه لا يكون دائماً ولا أكثرياً . ونحن نشاهد أحوال العالم الأعلى والعالم الأسفل واقعة على ترتيب لازم ، ونظم غير ...
As for the third doctrine – the statement of those who say: Events occur without an effective cause – the detailed discussion of it has already preceded in the topic of proving the Maker, the Necessary of Existence in Himself. Also: Everything that is accidental is neither perpetual nor mostly occurring. And we observe the states of the higher and lower worlds occurring in a necessary order and a consistent system...
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage presents the third false doctrine: the claim that events (accidents) occur without any effective cause (i.e., pure occasionalism with no secondary causality). The author says the full refutation has already been given earlier in the discussion proving the existence of the Necessary Being (al-Ṣāniʿ). He then adds a new argument: anything that is purely accidental (by chance, without cause) will not be perpetual (dāʾim) nor even mostly occurring (aktharī). In other words, randomness does not produce regularity. But we observe that the states of both the higher world (celestial) and the lower world (terrestrial) occur in a necessary order and a consistent system (the text cuts off, but the implication is clear). Therefore, events cannot be without a cause; they must have a cause, which is the Necessary Being. This is an argument from the uniformity and order of nature to the existence of a cause.

Key Terms

حوادث
مؤثر
اتفاقي
دائم
أكثري
ترتيب لازم
نظم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument completely denies that occasionalism is possible
Correction: The argument uses observed regularity to affirm a cause, not to deny occasionalism entirely.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument against the claim that events occur without a cause?
A) Theologians have proven that bodies are originated
B) Accidental things are not perpetual or mostly occurring, but we observe regularity
C) Atoms are eternal
D) The celestial spheres are perfect

Key Takeaway
Events cannot be purely accidental because the universe exhibits a consistent, necessary order; this order points to an effective cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the sunrise every morning without fail, do you take this regularity for granted? How does this order strengthen your certainty that Allah is the cause? Will you pause today after Salah to reflect on one such constant in your life and thank Allah for it?
?
Think of an event you previously attributed to 'luck' or 'coincidence'. How does this lesson challenge that attribution? Commit to recognizing one event today and consciously saying 'This is from Allah'.
?
The author says 'everything accidental is not perpetual or mostly occurring.' Is there an area of your life where you rely on chance rather than Allah's decree (e.g., hoping for good results without effort)? What one step will you take to replace that reliance with trust in Allah and appropriate action?

Four Proofs Against Self-Motion of Bodies
Enumerates four proofs (second through fifth) that a body cannot be self-moving.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand four distinct logical proofs that demonstrate why a body cannot be the source of its own motion, each based on a different aspect of corporeality.

Core Idea
A body cannot be self-moving because its nature entails dependence on an external mover.

In this passage, the author presents four arguments (the second through fifth) that refute the idea that a body can move itself. Each proof takes a different facet of what it means to be a body—its equality in corporeality, its need for a single place, its passivity, and its composition from parts—to show that self-motion is logically impossible. These proofs form a key part of the kalām tradition's demonstration that matter cannot explain motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four proofs
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the numbered proofs from the second to the fifth. Observe how each begins with 'The second', 'The third', etc.
The first proof is missing due to a page boundary, but the logic of the others is self-contained.
2
Analyze each proof's conditional logic
For each proof, identify the conditional structure: 'If corporeality were the cause of motion, then...' Note the contradiction that follows from each assumption.
Focus on the conclusion: each proof shows an absurdity if bodies were self-moving.
3
Understand the underlying principles
The second proof uses equality in corporeality; the third uses unity of motion; the fourth uses receptivity vs. effectivity; the fifth uses dependence on parts. Grasp these distinct principles.
These principles are drawn from physics and metaphysics of the tradition.
4
Connect proofs to broader argument
Ask yourself: how do these proofs collectively support the claim that motion in bodies requires a non-physical mover? The conclusion states these are the proofs they relied on.
This connects to the larger topic of divine existence and causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إن الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية . فلو كانت الجسمية علة للحركة ، لوجب أن يكون كل جسم متحركاً بالذات
والثالث : إن الجسمية لما كانت علة للحركة ، وجب أن تكون جميع حركات الأجسام إلى حيز واحد فيلزم اجتماع الأجسام الكثيرة في الحيز الواحد
والرابع : إن الجسم قابل للحركة ، فيمتنع أن يكون مؤثراً فيها، لأن الواحد بالنسبة إلى الواحد لا يكون قابلا وفاعلا معاً .
الخامس : إن حركة الجسم، موقوفة على حركة كل واحد من أجزائه . وما كان موقوفاً على الغير، لا يكون واجباً بالذات
فهذه هي الوجوه التي عولوا عليها في بيان أن الجسم يمتنع أن يكون متحركاً لذاته
The second: That bodies are equal in corporeality. So if corporeality were the cause of motion, then it would be necessary that every body be moving by itself.
The third: That since corporeality was the cause of motion, it would be necessary that all motions of bodies be to a single place, necessitating the gathering of many bodies in one place.
The fourth: That the body is receptive to motion, so it is impossible for it to be an effecter in it, because the one in relation to the one cannot be both receptive and agent simultaneously.
The fifth: That the motion of the body is dependent on the motion of each one of its parts. And that which is dependent on another cannot be necessary by itself.
These are the proofs upon which they relied in demonstrating that it is impossible for a body to be self-moving.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage enumerates five proofs (al-wojuh al-khamsa) refuting the claim that a body can be self-moving (mutaharrik li-dhatihi). The first proof is missing due to the page boundary, but the second through fifth are given. The second argues that bodies are equal in corporeality (jismiyya); if corporeality were the cause of motion, then every body would necessarily be in motion, which is not the case. The third argues that if corporeality were the cause, all motions would be toward a single place, leading to all bodies occupying one space. The fourth argues that a body is receptive (qabil) to motion, so it cannot be an effecter (mu'aththir) of motion, as one thing cannot be both agent and patient. The fifth argues that a body's motion depends on the motion of its parts, and what depends on another cannot be necessary by itself (wajib bi al-dhat). The author concludes that these are the proofs they relied on.

Key Terms
جسمية
حركة
متحرك بالذات
علة
قابل
فاعل
واجب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Students may think these proofs assume bodies are not self-moving, but they are logical deductions showing contradictions if one assumes self-motion.
Correction: The proofs do not assume the conclusion; they show that assuming self-motion leads to absurdities, thus proving the impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, why can a body not be the effecter of its own motion?
A) Because it is divisible into parts.
B) Because it is receptive to motion and cannot be both agent and patient simultaneously.
C) Because it is created in time.
D) Because its motion depends on something else.

Key Takeaway
The four proofs logically preclude bodies from being self-moving, thereby pointing to an external mover as the source of motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a body cannot move itself, then consider the movements of your own body. Do you ever act as if you are self-sufficient in your actions, forgetting the external mover? How does this realization humble you, and what specific change can you make today to acknowledge that all your movements depend on Allah?
?
Think of a time when you felt you were the sole cause of an achievement. In light of this lesson, how should you reinterpret that event? Write down a commitment to attribute all motion, both physical and spiritual, to the true Mover, and practice saying 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' more frequently.
?
The fourth proof states that one thing cannot be both receptive and agent simultaneously. Reflect on your own actions: Is there an area of your life where you try to be both the receiver and the giver, forgetting that your entire existence is a gift from Allah? What would it look like to embrace pure receptivity before Him?
